b'Dave Fleischer \xe2\x80\x94 a short, bald, gay, Jewish 61-year-old with bulging biceps and a distaste for prejudice \xe2\x80\x94 knocked on the front door of a modest home in a middle-class neighborhood on the west side of Los Angeles. It was an enthusiastic knuckle-thump, the kind that arouses suspicion from dogs in yards halfway down the block but, crucially, can also be heard by humans watching cable news at high volume.'b'If he had his way, Fleischer would knock on doors with a golf ball. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what the Mormons use,\xe2\x80\x9d he said on this sunny, bird-chirping Saturday in February. Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s staff at the Los Angeles-based Leadership Lab \xe2\x80\x94 which goes door to door to reduce bias against L.G.B.T. people, with a current focus on transgender discrimination \xe2\x80\x94 didn\xe2\x80\x99t take to the golf-ball suggestion, but Fleischer wanted me to know that he is \xe2\x80\x9cnot opposed to stealing a good idea from the Mormons.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'A gray-haired Hispanic woman named Nancy cracked open the front door, though not enough to let her little dog eat our ankles. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re out talking to voters about an important issue \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer began, only to have Nancy excuse herself and walk away. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t sure she would return; the last two voters he\xe2\x80\x99d met pleaded busyness. But after shooing the dog into another room, Nancy appeared in her doorway again. She smiled shyly and asked Fleischer, the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s director, how she could help him. Had he been completely honest, he might have said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m here to make you less prejudiced. It could take awhile.\xe2\x80\x9d But instead he began with a simple question: If she were to vote on whether to \xe2\x80\x9cinclude gay and transgender people in nondiscrimination laws,\xe2\x80\x9d would she be in favor or opposed?'b'\xe2\x80\x9cIn favor,\xe2\x80\x9d she assured him. Fleischer asked her to rate that support on a scale from zero to 10. \xe2\x80\x9cA 10,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have friends who are gay.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'A typical canvassing conversation might have ended there. Nancy, it seemed, was a supporter \xe2\x80\x94 no need to worry about her. But Fleischer is wary of what he calls the \xe2\x80\x9canti-discrimination declaration.\xe2\x80\x9d At the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s two-hour pre-canvass training that morning, volunteers were warned about \xe2\x80\x9cfake 10s,\xe2\x80\x9d people who think of themselves as against discrimination \xe2\x80\x94 many of them Democrats \xe2\x80\x94 but who can nonetheless be swayed by emotion-based appeals that provoke prejudice and fear.'b'Those appeals worked last November in Houston, where the same voters who elected a lesbian mayor in 2009 repealed a city ordinance protecting transgender people from discrimination in employment, housing and public accommodations. Armed with a \xe2\x80\x9cno men in women\xe2\x80\x99s bathrooms\xe2\x80\x9d message, anti-L.G.B.T. groups argued that transgender people (or those pretending to be) would pose a threat to children in public restrooms. One group aired a 'b'lurid television ad'b' of a man following a frightened, wide-eyed young girl into a bathroom stall. Though there was currently no ballot measure in Los Angeles to worry about, there was a palpable urgency to the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s work. A few days before, the Human Rights Campaign released 'b'a report'b' warning about 44 anti-transgender bills filed in 16 states. Then, last month, the North Carolina General Assembly passed a bill preventing cities and counties from enacting their own anti-discrimination legislation, stripping existing protections.'b'At the door, Fleischer asked Nancy if she knew any transgender people. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t. He then did something few political consultants would advise: He introduced her to the opposition\xe2\x80\x99s favorite argument. He handed her a small video player, on which she watched a Baptist minister in Houston make the case about bathrooms. Fleischer then returned to his scale, asking Nancy what number felt right for her now. \xe2\x80\x9cI know I\xe2\x80\x99m in favor of gays, because I\xe2\x80\x99ve worked with them and socialized with them,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI think they\xe2\x80\x99re wonderful. But for transgenders? Give me a five.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Nancy wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the only person to significantly decrease her support after watching the video. Across the street, a man in his late 30s who said he was liberal and pro-L.G.B.T.-rights moved to a five from an eight, explaining that he was deeply worried about \xe2\x80\x9cthe bathroom issue.\xe2\x80\x9d The man\xe2\x80\x99s concern seemed informed by his experience in a New York City nightclub; he hinted at his discomfort standing at a urinal next to a drag queen. Nancy had no such seemingly relevant personal experiences, nor did she appear particularly concerned about bathroom safety. For her, the video seemed to clarify that Fleischer was specifically asking her about transgender people, a group she had no experience with and seemed to have little inherent empathy for. To get Nancy to a true 10 capable of withstanding opposition messaging, Fleischer needed to help her \xe2\x80\x9ctap into her own empathy and connect emotionally to transgender people.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Fanned out across the neighborhood were more than three dozen Leadership Lab volunteers, many of them local college students, as well as progressive activists from around the country hoping to learn about changing voters\xe2\x80\x99 minds. Over the last six years, Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s unorthodox canvassing technique has attracted the attention of social scientists, liberal groups and even presidential-campaign consultants. It has also attracted controversy. In 2014, Science published a study claiming to show that an approximately 20-minute conversation with a gay or lesbian canvasser trained by Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s team could turn a gay-marriage opponent into a supporter. But Science 'b'retracted the study'b' five months later, after the lead author couldn\xe2\x80\x99t produce his data and admitted to lying about aspects of the experiment\xe2\x80\x99s design.'b'The fraudulent study called into question the validity of the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s deep-canvassing approach. Had it all been wishful thinking? Maybe, as The Wall Street Journal suggested, Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s efforts merely \xe2\x80\x9cflattered the ideological sensibilities of liberals.\xe2\x80\x9d But this week, 'b'a new study published in Science'b' by David Broockman, an assistant professor of political economy at Stanford, and Joshua Kalla, a graduate student in political science at Berkeley, appears to serve as vindication of Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s work. Leadership Lab-trained volunteers were 'b'found to be successful at reducing transgender prejudice'b' in front-door conversations, the effects persisting months later in follow-up surveys.'b'Betsy Levy Paluck, an associate professor at Princeton who studies bias, believes the study will have broad implications for those in her field. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do social scientists know about reducing prejudice in the world? In short, very little,\xe2\x80\x9d she writes in the same issue of Science, adding that the new study\xe2\x80\x99s results \xe2\x80\x9cstand alone as a rigorous test of this type of prejudice-reduction intervention.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Fleischer is planning more interventions. Though he has devoted much of his political and community-organizing career to L.G.B.T. issues, he believes this kind of canvassing could change people\xe2\x80\x99s thinking on everything from abortion and gun rights to race-based prejudice. He also hopes it will usher in a new era of political persuasion. \xe2\x80\x9cModern political campaigns have focused mostly on communicating with people who already agree with them and turning them out to vote,\xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer says. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what we\xe2\x80\x99ve learned by having real, in-depth conversations with people is that a broad swath of voters are actually open to changing their mind. And that\xe2\x80\x99s exciting, because it offers the possibility that we could get past the current paralysis on a wide variety of controversial issues.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'It took a 'b'devastating loss at the ballot box for Fleischer to see the political wisdom in heart-to-hearts with strangers. In 2008, he was in Ohio mobilizing African-American and Latino voters for Barack Obama when California residents passed Proposition 8, banning same-sex marriage in the state. Fleischer headed west to work with the Los Angeles L.G.B.T. Center, which houses the Leadership Lab, and proposed an unusual idea to his new colleagues: Canvassers should talk to Prop 8 supporters about why they had voted against same-sex marriage. Then they should try to change the voters\xe2\x80\x99 minds.'b'The idea grew out of Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s own experience as a \xe2\x80\x9cJewish, liberal gay kid\xe2\x80\x9d in Chillicothe, Ohio. He likes to say that he has been talking to people who disagree with him since he was 4. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I would have only talked to people who agreed with me, I would have only talked to my mom and dad,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me. \xe2\x80\x9cInteracting with people different than me was a normal thing, and certainly not undesirable or scary. It\xe2\x80\x99s almost the opposite of growing up today in the age of Facebook and political polarization, where it\xe2\x80\x99s easy to always be among like-minded people, your self-isolation complete before you have your first beer.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'At first, Fleischer and his team tried cerebral arguments and appeals to fairness in their doorway conversations with same-sex-marriage opponents who didn\xe2\x80\x99t express deep religious objections. \xe2\x80\x9cThat failed miserably,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. Eventually, the canvassers tried eliciting more emotional experiences. They urged voters to talk about anyone they knew who was gay or lesbian \xe2\x80\x94 and, more important, to speak about their own marriages. \xe2\x80\x9cThat changed everything,\xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer told me. \xe2\x80\x9cMost people consider marriage the most important and meaningful thing they ever did. Talking about marriage brought up deep emotion. If marriage was the most valuable thing in their own life, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they also want their gay friends \xe2\x80\x94 or gay people \xe2\x80\x94 to experience it, too?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Though Fleischer thought his new approach was working, he wanted to know whether the persuasion lasted. During a 2013 trip to New York City, he visited the Columbia Univer\xc2\xadsity political-science professor Donald Green, whose experiments on voter behavior \xe2\x80\x94 including his findings that canvassing is a more effective mobilization tool than telephone calls or direct mail \xe2\x80\x94 partly inspired a focus on building a ground game, a strategy mastered by the Obama campaign.'b'Green was skeptical that the canvassers were as persuasive as they thought they were. His previous research suggested that \xe2\x80\x9cpeople don\xe2\x80\x99t change their mind very easily, and when they are persuaded to think differently, the effect is usually temporary,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me. But he also knew that political persuasion had not been studied often. \xe2\x80\x9cRemarkably, we don\xe2\x80\x99t know very much about what forms of campaign communications are most persuasive,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. Green connected Fleischer with Michael LaCour, then a U.C.L.A. graduate student in political science and statistics, who said he could design a study to assess the long-term effectiveness of Leadership Lab canvassers at increasing support for same-sex marriage among voters in Los Angeles who had supported Prop 8. LaCour, joined late in the process by Green as a co-author, published the results in the December 2014 issue of Science. The study claimed to find that though both gay and straight canvassers were effective at the door, only voters contacted by gay canvassers remained persuaded nearly a year later.'b'The study made international news and seemed to confirm what many gays and lesbians believed in their guts: that knowing a gay person is a powerful antidote to anti-gay bias. It also seemed to bolster the \xe2\x80\x9ccontact hypothesis\xe2\x80\x9d theory of prejudice reduction, which finds that personal contact decreases bias against a minority group. Previous research, though, including a study of teenagers in an Outward Bound program assigned to either mixed-race or all-white groups, suggested that lasting prejudice reduction happened after weeks of regular contact. LaCour appeared to be breaking new ground, showing that one brief but memorable interaction could reduce prejudice.'b'Broockman and Kalla were intrigued by LaCour\xe2\x80\x99s findings and hoped to replicate it for an experiment measuring the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s transgender canvassing. But the more they analyzed LaCour\xe2\x80\x99s study design and results, the more problems they found. Yes, Leadership Lab volunteers had spoken to voters in Los Angeles about gay marriage. But when pressed, LaCour couldn\xe2\x80\x99t produce any evidence that he had conducted the follow-up surveys of voters that would have been essential to measuring canvassing\xe2\x80\x99s long-term influence. He also admitted to lying about having received funds for his study from several organizations, including the Ford Foundation.'b'A shocked and embarrassed Green requested that Science retract the study; soon after, Princeton rescinded a teaching offer to LaCour. News of the retraction stunned Fleischer, who worried that the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s marriage canvassing would be tainted by association. He vowed to keep at it, but soon there was no need. When the Supreme Court legalized gay marriage nationwide in 2015, Fleischer and his team could turn their focus to the next L.G.B.T. battlefield: transgender rights.'b''b'Though there is'b' scant research on transgender prejudice, what is known suggests transgender people face \xe2\x80\x9cwidespread prejudice and discrimination,\xe2\x80\x9d Aaron Norton and Gregory Herek wrote in their 'b'2012 study'b' of heterosexual attitudes toward transgender Americans. The year before, 'b'a survey'b' of more than 6,000 transgender and gender-nonconforming people revealed that an astonishing 41 percent had tried to commit suicide.'b'To test whether transphobia could be overcome during a face-to-face encounter, Broockman and Kalla measured a 2015 canvassing effort in Miami by volunteers from the Leadership Lab and SAVE, a local L.G.B.T. organization. The groups feared a backlash against a recent ordinance that prohibited discrimination based on gender identity. The experiment divided voters into a \xe2\x80\x9ctreatment\xe2\x80\x9d group engaged in a conversation intended to reduce transgender prejudice and a \xe2\x80\x9cplacebo\xe2\x80\x9d group targeted with a conversation about recycling. Before the canvass conversations, both groups completed what they believed to be an unrelated online survey with dozens of social and political questions, including some designed to measure transgender prejudice. After the canvass, the groups filled out four follow-up surveys, up to three months later.'b'Broockman and Kalla found that the treatment group was \xe2\x80\x9cconsiderably more accepting of transgender people\xe2\x80\x9d and that a single, approximately 10-minute conversation with a stranger \xe2\x80\x9ccan markedly reduce prejudice for at least three months.\xe2\x80\x9d Unlike LaCour\xe2\x80\x99s invented finding that the messenger matters more than the message, Broockman and Kalla found that both transgender and nontransgender canvassers were effective. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too bad that the takeaway was that only gay people could persuade people about gay marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d Broockman says about LaCour\xe2\x80\x99s retracted study. \xe2\x80\x9cEveryone basically ignored the canvassing aspect, and that the message and the quality of the conversation at the door is what seems to matter.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Broockman and Kalla point to Leadership Lab canvassers\xe2\x80\x99 ability to engage voters in two prejudice-reduction behaviors at the door: \xe2\x80\x9cperspective taking\xe2\x80\x9d (the ability to empathize with another\xe2\x80\x99s experience) and \xe2\x80\x9cactive processing\xe2\x80\x9d (deep or effortful thinking). Both were on display during many of the canvass conversations I observed, including the one with Nancy, the woman who moved to a five from a 10 after watching the opposition video.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cIs this the first time you\xe2\x80\x99ve thought about transgender people?\xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer asked her soon after she backtracked.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cYeah, I would say so,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI know it exists, and I hear stories, and I see them on TV. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t have any friends like I do my gay friends.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Fleischer nodded and removed a picture of his friend Jackson from his wallet. \xe2\x80\x9cFor me, I never had a transgender friend I was really close to until I was 56,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, handing Nancy the picture. \xe2\x80\x9cJackson grew up as a girl, but he knew even when he was 5 or 6 that he was really a boy. It was only in his 20s that he started to tell his folks the truth, and he started making the transition to living as a man. He\xe2\x80\x99s married to a woman now, and he\xe2\x80\x99s so much happier. And he can grow a better beard than I can!\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Nancy laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the thing \xe2\x80\x94 they\xe2\x80\x99re happier when they come out, whenever everybody knows,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. She seemed to be connecting Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s experience to that of her gay friends.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cRight, because otherwise you have the biggest secret in the world, and everyone thinks something about you that\xe2\x80\x99s not true,\xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer said, before pivoting to a story about Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s being demeaned by a waiter in a restaurant. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like seeing people mistreat Jackson. To me, protecting transgender people with these laws is just affirming that they\xe2\x80\x99re human.\xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer then steered the conversation to Nancy\xe2\x80\x99s experiences with discrimination. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve probably had a time when people have judged you unfairly?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d Nancy said. Over the next few minutes, she recounted several instances of racism after moving to Los Angeles from Central America with her husband. Still, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t appear emotional in retelling the experiences. Fleischer wasn\xe2\x80\x99t surprised; people rarely feel safe enough at first to express deep hurt. It usually isn\xe2\x80\x99t until Fleischer opens up about his own experience \xe2\x80\x94 including feeling different in his small, conservative Ohio town \xe2\x80\x94 that voters feel safe to \xe2\x80\x9cget vulnerable, too,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. Nancy had mostly dismissed Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9chow did that make you feel?\xe2\x80\x9d questions, but his personal story prompted a shift. As Fleischer returned to the discrimination she had faced, Nancy paused and said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt felt terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d A few minutes later, when he asked her if she saw a connection between \xe2\x80\x9cyour experience and how you want to treat transgender people,\xe2\x80\x9d she said she did. \xe2\x80\x9cI see transgender people as the same as I see myself,\xe2\x80\x9d Nancy told him. She ended a solid 10, a rating he was confident could survive opposition messaging.'b''b'Earlier that morning'b', Leadership Lab volunteers sat on stackable chairs and watched video clips of front-door encounters on a projector screen. Fleischer\xe2\x80\x99s team videotapes many of its conversations with voters, then \xe2\x80\x9canalyzes the tape like a football team might so we can figure out what\xe2\x80\x99s working and what\xe2\x80\x99s not,\xe2\x80\x9d explained a field organizer, Steve Deline.'b'Knocking on a stranger\xe2\x80\x99s door is scary, and a lot of that morning\xe2\x80\x99s training session was spent boosting the confidence of first-time canvassers. The leaders worked to keep the mood relaxed and optimistic. Fleischer does improv in his spare time, and the training sometimes felt like a well-oiled comedy routine. When the subject turned to potentially awkward initial encounters with voters, the Leadership Lab staff member Laura Gardiner and the longtime volunteer Nancy Williams (who is transgender) did some front-door role-playing.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHi, my name is Laura, and I\xe2\x80\x99m with the Leadership Lab,\xe2\x80\x9d Gardiner told Williams, channeling a shy first-time canvasser. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you have a few minutes today to talk about transgender people?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Williams played a busy voter. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have to teach my hamster to speak Finnish today.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Gardiner turned to the volunteers. \xe2\x80\x9cWe want to avoid asking for permission,\xe2\x80\x9d she told them. \xe2\x80\x9cJust dive in. Treat it like the most normal thing in the world. Like, of course we\xe2\x80\x99re on your doorstep on a Saturday talking about transgender issues!\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Moments later, Williams reminded the volunteers to be open and nonjudgmental. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re asking voters not to discriminate, to be less prejudiced, and we need to walk that walk,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat means not making assumptions based on the voter\xe2\x80\x99s age, race or their religion. Some folks may have a crucifix on the door. That doesn\xe2\x80\x99t tell you about the person inside.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'On this particular day, volunteers would be canvassing in a predominantly black neighborhood, so Gardiner reminded them to be sensitive to experiences of race-based discrimination. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat an African-American person has faced because of their race is not the same as the discrimination that I\xe2\x80\x99ve faced for being bisexual, or that my friend has faced for being transgender,\xe2\x80\x9d she told the group. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there is a similarity, because at the root there\xe2\x80\x99s the feeling of being judged, of having someone make assumptions about you, and that does not feel good.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But sometimes the gulf between the volunteer and the voter can seem insurmountable. After the first canvass I attended, the Leadership Lab project manager Ella Barrett seemed uncharacteristically sullen. When I asked how her day went, she shook her head and recounted a series of disheartening conversations with voters she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t persuade. In one, a social worker (\xe2\x80\x9ca social worker!\xe2\x80\x9d Barrett marveled) announced that being transgender is a mental illness; in another, a man matter-of-factly said he hoped to develop a \xe2\x80\x9cstraight pill\xe2\x80\x9d to change gay people.'b'Though not all voters would engage emotionally, I was surprised by how many did. Canvassers often had to politely extricate themselves after 20 minutes \xe2\x80\x94 voters were sad to see them go. \xe2\x80\x9cIf only I could have 10 minutes with Ted Cruz,\xe2\x80\x9d Fleischer said once. He was only half joking. Fleischer has an unwavering confidence in his ability to persuade most people to be \xe2\x80\x9cmore empathetic and less prejudiced,\xe2\x80\x9d and his optimism is shared by progressive groups who train with him. The day before one canvass, representatives from an animal rights group told me they hoped to better understand how to help people connect emotionally to animal welfare.'b'Fleischer is especially interested in learning whether deep canvassing can affect people\xe2\x80\x99s thinking on two issues \xe2\x80\x94 racial prejudice and abortion rights. Beginning in 2014, the Leadership Lab teamed up with Planned Parenthood to canvass in support of pro-choice policies. Though the abortion debate is less obviously rooted in prejudice than transgender discrimination, Fleischer and his canvassers noticed that many voters reacted negatively to a short video of a middle-aged woman recounting having an abortion when she was 22. People would often be \xe2\x80\x9cvery judgmental\xe2\x80\x9d of the woman on the video or any woman who had had an abortion, Fleischer said. To combat that, canvassers tried to get voters to reflect about challenging decisions they had made in their own sex lives or relationships \xe2\x80\x94 or times they were judged harshly. Volunteers also encouraged people to talk about anyone they were close to who had an abortion.'b'Eager to know if his abortion canvassing was persuasive, Fleischer asked Broockman and Kalla to measure it. But the researchers found that the persuasion attempts had \xe2\x80\x9czero effect,\xe2\x80\x9d Broockman said. Still, Fleischer isn\xe2\x80\x99t ready to give up. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause abortion is such a politically polarized issue,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit could just be that we have to get better at making voters trust us and open up.\xe2\x80\x9d But it could also be that the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s transgender canvassing success is an anomaly. While a discussion of transgender rights can trigger deeply ingrained feelings about sex and gender roles, the issue is also a fairly recent political consideration for many people. Melissa Michelson, a political-science professor at Menlo College in Atherton, Calif., who studies voter mobilization and public attitudes on L.G.B.T. issues, told me that changing people\xe2\x80\x99s minds about transgender rights might simply come down to \xe2\x80\x9cwhich side gets to a voter\xe2\x80\x99s door first to do the persuading.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'What\xe2\x80\x99s the best 'b'way to convince a voter at the door? Though most political canvassing today is focused on mobilizing supporters, an increasing number of researchers, think tanks and campaign operatives have \xe2\x80\x9cturned their attention to persuasion in the last few years,\xe2\x80\x9d says Columbia\xe2\x80\x99s Donald Green.'b'Jeremy Bird, a Hillary Clinton adviser who was the national field director for Obama\xe2\x80\x99s 2012 re-election effort, told me that his team conducted a number of experiments to try to have a greater impact when canvassing. \xe2\x80\x9cWe studied everything, from the kinds of conversations we should be having to the characteristics that made a voter persuadable,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cWe trained our volunteers to connect with voters at the door on a personal and values level, not to talk at them with scripted talking points. I think people don\xe2\x80\x99t talk enough about the focus on persuasion we had, because the story line became, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, they won because turnout was so high.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Becky Bond, a Bernie Sanders campaign adviser and an admirer of the Leadership Lab\xe2\x80\x99s work, says that the Sanders campaign has focused on marshaling the enthusiasm of volunteers to persuade people. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t think of a campaign that\xe2\x80\x99s put more volunteers on the ground in a primary season to have quality, face-to-face conversations with voters,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me.'b'Still, the Leadership Lab is unusual in its focus on quality over quantity. A typical state or national campaign, even one with a ground-game focus, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want its volunteers spending 10 or 15 minutes at a door. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99re talking about having real, quality conversations with voters, you can\xe2\x80\x99t bring that to scale without a really large number of people,\xe2\x80\x9d says Tim Saler, a Republican strategist at Grassroots Targeting, which works to mobilize and persuade voters. \xe2\x80\x9cTechnology has helped a bit with the scale challenge, but there\xe2\x80\x99s always the question: Do you knock on as many doors as possible, or do you knock on fewer doors and have potentially more fruitful interactions?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'There\xe2\x80\x99s also a lot that can go wrong when fresh-faced canvassers descend on unfamiliar neighborhoods. In 2004, for example, some 3,500 orange-hat-wearing Howard Dean supporters (many bused in from around the country) managed to annoy Iowa voters days before the state\xe2\x80\x99s Democratic caucus. \xe2\x80\x9cThe curse of the orange hats,\xe2\x80\x9d read a headline in Salon. There are other potential problems. \xe2\x80\x9cCanvassers can get mugged, they can get lost, they can get attacked by wild geese,\xe2\x80\x9d Michelson told me. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know if they\xe2\x80\x99re at McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s on their iPhone, and you can\xe2\x80\x99t always be sure what they\xe2\x80\x99re saying to voters. That lack of control scares campaigns. It\xe2\x80\x99s much easier to put all your volunteers in a cozy phone bank where everyone gets to hang out and eat pizza.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Though Fleischer prefers talking to voters face to face, he isn\xe2\x80\x99t opposed to sequestering volunteers in a phone bank to help L.G.B.T. activists in another state. In 2014, Fleischer and his team modified their canvassing work to persuade and mobilize voters by phone. Leadership Lab volunteers spoke with 3,330 residents in Pocatello, Idaho, a small, heavily Mormon city facing a ballot referendum that would have reversed a local nondiscrimination ordinance protecting gay and transgender people. The effort helped defeat the anti-L.G.B.T. ballot measure by a mere 80 votes.'b''b'After a long 'b'day of canvassing on that Saturday, tired but exuberant volunteers returned for a debriefing. One canvasser stood up and spoke of moving a man to a seven from a three. Another \xe2\x80\x94 a tattooed student who identifies as gender-nonconforming \xe2\x80\x94 proudly recalled persuading a voter \xe2\x80\x9cwho clearly had no experience with anyone who identified as being outside the gender binary. He said I blew his mind, and that he would never forget the conversation we had!\xe2\x80\x9d Meg Riley, a 60-year-old Unitarian Universalist minister from Minnesota who volunteers with a racial-justice group, recounted her eventful day. Her second conversation, she said, was with a black man in his 50s who was a seven on the 10-point scale. The man\xe2\x80\x99s daughter, though, would have none of it: She practically pushed him out of the way to tell Riley they were a 10. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m with Black Lives Matter, and I know a lot of trans people,\xe2\x80\x9d the woman told Riley. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re a 10! This family is a 10!\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Several of Riley\xe2\x80\x99s conversations proved poignant. She told voters about her own transgender child, Jie, now an adult. She recounted that when Jie was 3, the toddler responded to a question about possible Christmas presents by asking: \xe2\x80\x9cCould Santa turn a girl into a boy?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Riley\xe2\x80\x99s devotion to Jie had a visible impact on several voters, including the mother of a 7-year-old girl. The woman eventually told Riley that she had voted against gay marriage in California, but that she now regretted that choice. \xe2\x80\x9cI made a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. On the issue of transgender rights, the woman seemed mostly supportive but stopped at a nine. She said she was trying to evolve on the issue, though. As Riley prepared to leave for the next house on the block, the woman called out. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me a few years, and I know I\xe2\x80\x99ll be a 10!\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b''b''b''b''b'Forget the images'b' of men in hard hats standing before factory gates, of men with coal-blackened faces, of men perched high above New York City on steel beams. The emerging face of the American working class is a Hispanic woman who has never set foot on a factory floor. That\xe2\x80\x99s not the kind of work much of the working class does anymore. Instead of making things, they are more often paid to serve people: to care for someone else\xe2\x80\x99s children or someone else\xe2\x80\x99s parents; to clean another family\xe2\x80\x99s home.'b'The decline of the old working class has meant both an economic triumph for the nation and a personal tribulation for many of the workers. Technological progress has made American farms and factories more productive than ever, creating great wealth and cutting the cost of food and most other products. But the work no longer requires large numbers of workers. In 1900, factories and farms employed 60 percent of the work force. By 1950, a half-century later, those two sectors employed 36 percent. In 2014, they employed less than 10 percent.'b'For more than a century, since the trend was first documented, people have been prophesying a dire future in which the working class would no longer work. In 1964, a group of prominent liberals wrote President Johnson to warn of a \xe2\x80\x9ccybernation revolution\xe2\x80\x9d inexorably creating \xe2\x80\x9ca permanent impoverished and jobless class established in the midst of potential abundance.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Machines have taken the jobs of millions of Americans, and there is every indication that the trend will continue. In October, Budweiser successfully tested a self-driving truck by delivering beer more than 120 miles to a warehouse in Colorado. In December, Amazon opened a small convenience store near its Seattle headquarters that has no cashiers. Customers \xe2\x80\x94 for now, Amazon employees only \xe2\x80\x94 are billed automatically as they leave the store. In January, Bank of America opened branches in Denver and Minneapolis that are staffed by a lone employee, A.T.M.s and video terminals. And Americans are making a growing share of purchases online: about 8.4 percent of retail sales in 2016. These changes are driven by consumer preferences, not just by corporate cost-cutting imperatives. People like shopping in bed in the middle of the night. People like that computers make fewer mistakes. And people grow accustomed to computers. A few years ago, I watched a woman walk up to a bank teller and ask where she could find an A.T.M. The teller asked if she could help. No, the woman said, she just needed to withdraw some money.'b'But the forecasters were wrong in the most important respect. Workers continue to find work, but now the jobs are in service. Taking care of aging baby boomers, in particular, has become by far the largest driver of job growth in the American economy. Among the occupations the Bureau of Labor Statistics expects to grow most rapidly over the next decade: physical-therapy assistants, home health aides, occupational-therapy assistants, nurse practitioners, physical therapists, occupational-therapy aides, physician assistants. ... You get the idea. Nine of the 12 fastest-growing fields are different ways of saying \xe2\x80\x9cnurse.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In 1950, service work made up about 40 percent of working-class labor in the United States. By 2005, that share had climbed to 56 percent, according to data from a 2013 analysis by the economists David Autor at M.I.T. and David Dorn at the University of Zurich. The available evidence, Autor said, suggests that this trend has continued \xe2\x80\x9cvery rapidly\xe2\x80\x9d over the last decade, increasing the share of American workers who work in the service industries.'b'The rise of service-sector employment reflects the fact that Americans on average have more money to spend and that we are spending relatively less of that money on physical goods, because those goods have become cheaper. It took 10.5 hours of work at average wages to buy a bicycle in 1979; it took just four hours in 2015. Most Americans don\xe2\x80\x99t want a second and third bicycle, so that leaves more money for other purposes. And increasingly, the money is spent on services: help around the home, entertainment and vacations and, most of all, education and health care.'b'These jobs are difficult to mechanize or to perform with greater efficiency. Convalescents cannot be trained to eat more quickly. A phlebotomist cannot draw blood from two arms at once. Robots, as yet, cannot change diapers. Moreover, consumers may have an emotional investment in seeing this caring work performed by people rather than machines. They may be willing to pay for a personal touch.'b'Another limitation on our ability to program computers to do the work of people is summarized by the observation of the Hungarian scientist Michael Polanyi that \xe2\x80\x9cwe can know more than we can tell.\xe2\x80\x9d Consider the work of a security guard, who is basically tasked with sounding the alarm if something doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem right. Technology improves security, but it is not easy to write a formula approximating intuition.'b'The Cassandras, however, were right to warn about poverty in the midst of abundance. Personal-service providers \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cservants,\xe2\x80\x9d as they once were called \xe2\x80\x94 tend to be poorly paid. There is little job security; the benefits are meager; the work is physically demanding and emotionally draining. It is not particularly surprising that women and immigrants have been more likely to take these jobs than native-born men. For many of the caretaking service jobs, less than 10 percent of the work force is male.'b'The wages of service work increasingly determine the welfare of the American working class and, to a substantial degree, the broader economy. But politicians have paid little attention. That\xe2\x80\x99s partly because Americans continue to view service work as a way station, not a way of life. Teenagers get their first job at McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s; mothers dip back into the work force as receptionists; seniors make a little extra money as Walmart greeters. The reality is that these are the kinds of jobs millions of Americans hold for their entire working lives. And increasingly, these are the jobs their children will perform, too.'b''b'\xe2\x80\x94Binyamin Appelbaum 'b''b'Santa Clara, Calif. \xe2\x80\xa2 The Home Health Aide'b''b'By Elise Craig'b''b''b'Two major events'b' in Ofelia Bersabe\xe2\x80\x99s life persuaded her to become a home health aide. In 2008, the Bay Area electronics company where she worked for 21 years as a sample-department supervisor let her go in a mass layoff. And that same year, her mother, whom she followed to the United States from the Philippines nearly two decades earlier, died. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen she passed away in my arms, I felt that I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t given her the full attention I wanted to,\xe2\x80\x9d Bersabe said. \xe2\x80\x9cI promised myself that I would concentrate on taking care of women, especially seniors. In that way, I feel like I\xe2\x80\x99m still taking care of her.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Nine years later, at age 69, Bersabe is among nearly a million Americans who work as home health aides, a field that is expected to grow 38 percent by 2024, faster than most other occupations, thanks in large part to the aging baby-boom population. Already a senior herself, Bersabe works 65 hours a week caring for two elderly clients with dementia. She spends five 12- and 14-hour night shifts in her clients\xe2\x80\x99 homes, providing companionship, reminders to take medicine and light housekeeping for one client, and everything from bathing and dressing to diaper changing for the other.'b'Having two jobs is partly a necessity and partly a hedge; should one of her clients die, she can still rely on income from the other. She also needs the money. Because she is a member of the Service Employees International Union Local 2015, the agencies that Bersabe works for pay her $16.13 and $11 an hour. California\xe2\x80\x99s current minimum wage is $10.50, but the living wage for someone like Bersabe is $11.29.'b'Bersabe is grateful for the money and has no complaints about her wages, but she acknowledges that making a living in the Bay Area\xe2\x80\x99s technology bubble is tough. She lost her house after the 2008 layoff, and she and her husband, who works nights as a security guard, now share a three-bedroom apartment rental behind Levi\xe2\x80\x99s Stadium, where the 49ers play, with two other elderly couples.'b'The gratitude Bersabe\xe2\x80\x99s clients show her \xe2\x80\x94 one kisses her when she arrives \xe2\x80\x94 is incredibly fulfilling, she said, but the work is hard. The client welcomed Bersabe as an old friend that she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen in a long time.'b'Bersabe expects to work through her 70s and 80s and maybe even into her 90s. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll work as long as I can stand on my own two legs,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAs long as I can drive and walk and God permits me, I\xe2\x80\x99ll enjoy the job I love.\xe2\x80\x9d Bersabe has no intention of being a burden to her three children, who have families of their own and are scattered from Kentucky to the Philippines. \xe2\x80\x9cHonestly, I am thinking, when the time comes, who is going to take care of me?\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to bother my kids. I want them to see me as a kicking woman, like I was before.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Elise Craig is a freelance writer and editor based in San Francisco.'b''b'Brooklyn \xe2\x80\xa2 The Hair Braider'b''b'By Jazmine Hughes'b''b''b'On a recent 'b'visit to Jennifer\xe2\x80\x99s Beauty World, in Flatbush, Brooklyn, Sonia Ufot started by detangling my tightly coiled hair and blow-drying it pin-straight, then she parted a small section into an individual plait, wrapped a section of filler hair around the root and braided it straight down for 16 inches. She majored in economics at Delta State University in Nigeria, but while she was at school, she found out she had thyroid cancer and eventually moved to the United States for treatment. She started braiding hair for the money and never returned to economics. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s more money doing hair,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me. She charges between $100 to $250 for each style, depending on its intricacy. She hasn\xe2\x80\x99t gone on vacation in five years.'b'Unlike most people trained in cosmetology, braiders, many of whom are immigrants, don\xe2\x80\x99t use chemicals, sharp objects or heat; just a comb, oil, water and their hands. Individual states legislate the requirements for becoming a licensed hair braider; in several, braiders \xe2\x80\x94 even those with a pre-existing knowledge of braiding \xe2\x80\x94 are required to complete more than 2,000 hours of training at cosmetology schools, which can cost tens of thousands of dollars and whose classes have almost nothing to do with styling black women\xe2\x80\x99s hair. She was racing to pick up her son Manny from day care, but she talked with the guy later that evening. She told him that she earned $8.50 an hour, just 25 cents above the Illinois minimum wage. She\xe2\x80\x99d been at the same McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s in Cicero, a largely Latino town west of Chicago, for about four years. In all that time, she had one raise: 10 cents. The man explained that she\xe2\x80\x99d been cheated even out of her low pay: Workers at restaurants had been required, illegally, to punch out before tallying up their registers or breaking down French-fry boxes. \xe2\x80\x9cI think about it now,\xe2\x80\x9d Alvarez says. \xe2\x80\x9cGod, I was stupid.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'A few weeks after meeting the organizer, Alvarez said, she and her co-workers handed in a petition demanding that the store manager show them more respect in front of customers. Not only did the yelling stop, she said, but they soon received a raise of as much as 75 cents. \xe2\x80\x9cPushing works,\xe2\x80\x9d Alvarez says she learned. In March 2014, she joined her first rally, a multicity coordinated Fight for $15 day of action.'b'Alvarez is now 24 and still works at the same McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s. Nationally, jobs in retail and food services outnumber those in manufacturing by more than two to one. The country\xe2\x80\x99s largest private employers include Walmart, McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s, Kroger and the conglomeration of KFC, Pizza Hut and Taco Bell. These chains are no longer places just for teenagers to work part time: The average age of a fast-food employee has climbed to 29; like Alvarez, a third of them have spent some time in college.'b'Fight for $15 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t operate like a traditional union: There are no contracts with employers, and Alvarez and others pay no dues. The Service Employees International Union has been its primary source of funding from its inception in New York City, in 2012. Yet Fight for $15 organizers take credit for winning wage increases for 22 million workers in America. Although the federal minimum wage of $7.25 hasn\xe2\x80\x99t budged since 2009, numerous cities and states since 2012 have raised their base pay to $12, $13 and even $15 an hour. Under President Trump, the effort is sure to face additional hurdles. But Fight for $15 helped lead the campaign to derail Trump\xe2\x80\x99s first pick for labor secretary, Andrew Puzder, organizing workers from his fast-food chains to march on their stores and to share their stories of wage theft. Low pay is an issue that crosses party lines: Two of every five U.S. workers earn less than $15 an hour.'b'Today Alvarez makes $11 an hour at McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s. She has never been put on the schedule for a full 40-hour week; she receives no benefits, health coverage or overtime. Yet the raise has allowed her to replace the moldering floorboards in her basement apartment. And Alvarez has been transformed in other ways by the labor fight. She has emerged as one of its spokeswomen, and the job has taken on a new significance. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen people say we should go back to school, I say the adjunct professors who joined us went to school,\xe2\x80\x9d Alvarez said. \xe2\x80\x9cHow come they\xe2\x80\x99re not doing better? The road has changed over the two decades that he has been driving trucks. \xe2\x80\x9cIn today\xe2\x80\x99s day and age, it\xe2\x80\x99s really stressful,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a lot more likely to be struck because of other drivers using their cellphone.\xe2\x80\x9d The regulatory landscape has changed, too. Every year, for example, Culver has to undergo a Department of Transportation physical, because he has sleep apnea.'b'In recent years, online giants like Amazon have pushed for ever-faster delivery times, sometimes delivering orders on the same day. But Dolan, who makes slightly more than that, points out that, unlike at many other companies, most Zappos workers are full-time employees who receive benefits like health insurance and retirement plans.'b'At Zappos, employees are encouraged to interact with co-workers throughout the business and eat together on campus. The company frequently hosts events, ranging from product demonstrations to scavenger hunts, which are open to all employees. Dolan said this is one thing that makes her current work so much better than previous jobs. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been in places where I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t even allowed to take a break with someone if they were a different pay grade than I was,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.'b'Another difference from Dolan\xe2\x80\x99s previous call-center jobs is that Zappos reps aren\xe2\x80\x99t limited in the amount of time they are allowed to spend on each conversation. \xe2\x80\x9cAt other jobs, I\xe2\x80\x99d be stressed because I\xe2\x80\x99d have to resolve each call in about five minutes in order to make my numbers,\xe2\x80\x9d Dolan said. She knows she could be making more money somewhere else, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t enjoy her day as much. \xe2\x80\x9cI figured I\xe2\x80\x99d do this for six months just to get something local on my r\xc3\xa9sum\xc3\xa9 so I could look for something else,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I haven\xe2\x80\x99t looked for a job since I stepped foot in here. I don\xe2\x80\x99t plan on it.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Eric Steuer is a contributing writer for Wired based in San Francisco.'b''b'Columbus, Ohio \xe2\x80\xa2 The Meat Cutter'b''b'By Abe Streep'b''b''b'Every day at 'b'2 p.m., Ruhatijuru Sebatutsi, a Congolese refugee, rides a bus from outside Columbus, Ohio, where he lives with his wife and eight children, with 10 of his colleagues. The bus travels 40 miles southwest to Washington Court House, population about 14,000, and drops its passengers near a plant owned by the SugarCreek Packing Company, which produces pork and poultry products like bacon bits and sausage patties. Just before 4, Sebatutsi, 40, changes into the uniform of a meat cutter: cap, gloves and scrubs. Then he takes his place in a line of men and women from Myanmar, the Democratic Republic of Congo, Bhutan and rural Ohio. Sebatutsi fled war in Congo as a teenager and spent most of his life in Rwanda\xe2\x80\x99s sprawling Gihembe refugee camp before being relocated to Ohio in 2015. Once the shift starts, he pushes meat through cutting machines. \xe2\x80\x9cYou push again and again,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me through a translator. \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t require a lot of knowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d There are three breaks per nine-hour shift: 15 minutes early on, a 30-minute meal break, then 10 minutes toward the end. At 1 a.m. he takes the bus home. When he arrives, his wife and children are sleeping. He works seven days a week, making $11.50 an hour, time and a half on Saturdays and double on Sundays. \xe2\x80\x9cI am so lucky,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'Central Ohio is particularly welcoming to refugees, having resettled more than 17,000 since 1983. In November 2016, the region became a flash point for those opposed to resettlement when a Somali-born Ohio State University student was killed by police after attacking pedestrians. Still, in early February, the Columbus mayor, Andrew Ginther, signed an executive order supporting the resettlement of refugees. His reasons were economic as well as altruistic. Nearly 12 percent of Columbus is foreign-born. According to a recent study from the New American Economy, a nonprofit research-and-advocacy group, Columbus-area immigrants pay $1.2 billion a year in taxes and have an annual spending power of $3.2 billion. They work as doctors and engineers, open small businesses employing thousands, perform manual labor in warehouses and frequently do the jobs that many other Americans will not, like meatpacking.'b'SugarCreek is one of many meatpacking businesses nationwide that have turned to refugees. Last spring, the company approached Community Refugee and Immigration Services (CRIS), a Columbus refugee- resettlement agency. \xe2\x80\x9cThey needed people,\xe2\x80\x9d said CRIS\xe2\x80\x99s Marcus Gorman, who arranges employment for the newly resettled. \xe2\x80\x9cThey had a lot of opportunities for entry-level workers, and they were accustomed to working with folks that spoke little to no English.\xe2\x80\x9d Now, Gorman said, about 65 of CRIS\xe2\x80\x99s clients work at SugarCreek. Since Sebatutsi started last November, he has opted to work every day, which he said is the best part of the job. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a lot of overtime, and you can make money.\xe2\x80\x9d But, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s also the worst thing.\xe2\x80\x9d He is no longer able to join his family at church. All that pushing, slicing and packaging is repetitive, but it\xe2\x80\x99s far better than life in Gihembe. \xe2\x80\x9cThe kids can ask you for something, you cannot provide,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut here you work, you take care of your problems, you do something for yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Abe Streep is a contributing editor for Outside and a contributing writer for The California Sunday Magazine.'b''b'Dalton, Ga. \xe2\x80\xa2 The Carpet Whisperer'b''b'By Lizzie O\xe2\x80\x99Leary'b''b''b'The warehouse'b' where Guadalupe Guido works is so bright inside that it feels like noon no matter the time of day. Huge metal racks hold row after row of carpet that will be trucked from this plant in Dalton, Ga., all over the United States. Dalton calls itself the Carpet Capital of the World, and everyone in Guido\xe2\x80\x99s family \xe2\x80\x94 her mother, father, sister and herself \xe2\x80\x94 work for one of Dalton\xe2\x80\x99s carpet companies.'b'Guido, who is 23 and goes by Lupe, has been working at Engineered Floors for almost two years. She has risen quickly, starting on yarn machines, before driving a Hyster forklift, working as a production clerk and now serving as a tufting scheduler. She creates orders, dictating the type of yarn to use and how much footage of carpet each job will need. \xe2\x80\x9cWithout this person,\xe2\x80\x9d she said of her role, \xe2\x80\x9cthere really is no show.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In the political sphere, manufacturing work is often associated with men, and specifically white men. But women have always worked in carpets in Dalton and at Engineered Floors. And Dalton is about 50 percent Latino, which is reflected in the work force here. Many people in the city came from Mexico in the 1970s and \xe2\x80\x9980s to work in the industry, including Guido\xe2\x80\x99s parents, Jose and Martina.'b'Lupe Guido was born in Dalton and dreamed initially of becoming a lawyer. \xe2\x80\x9cI talk a lot,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a laugh. But at 18, she wanted independence and her own money and went to work at Mohawk Industries, one of the largest carpet companies in the city, where her mother still works. It was not what her parents had in mind. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was growing up, it was always: \xe2\x80\x98Go to school, go to school,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be working how I\xe2\x80\x99m working.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But the carpet industry, like most of American manufacturing, has undergone radical changes. The hardest and most dangerous jobs are now performed by machines that are mostly run by computers, and those computers are watched over by people like Guido.'b'Engineered Floors is new in comparison to Mohawk or Shaw Industries, the two companies that have dominated Dalton for decades. But Guido has been able to move up quickly. She earns $15.50 an hour, up from $11 an hour when she started in 2015. At the end of each year, she gets a $500 bonus.'b'Guido said she would like to stay at Engineered Floors if she can. To do so requires vigilantly managing her time so that she can move up. She\xe2\x80\x99s awake at 6 a.m., clocks into her job at 7 and works a 10-hour shift until 5 p.m. After dinner with her fianc\xc3\xa9, who also works in the carpet industry, Guido spends her nights studying for a bachelor\xe2\x80\x99s degree in human-resource management at Georgia Northwestern Technical College. One day, she wants to be a department manager or a plant manager. \xe2\x80\x9cThe way I\xe2\x80\x99ve been growing and growing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want for that to stop.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Lizzie O\xe2\x80\x99Leary is the host of the radio program \xe2\x80\x9cMarketplace Weekend.\xe2\x80\x9d More reporting by O\xe2\x80\x99Leary and the producer Eliza Mills about 'b'the carpet industry'b' in Dalton, Ga., is on Marketplace Weekend.'b''b'Las Vegas \xe2\x80\xa2 The Hotel Cleaner'b''b'By Amanda Fortini'b''b''b'Most mornings,'b' Wendy Almada \xe2\x80\x94 a guest-room attendant, or G.R.A., at the Aria Resort and Casino on the Las Vegas Strip \xe2\x80\x94 puts on a pair of latex gloves and tackles the bathroom first. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to do beds,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI like to clean bathrooms.\xe2\x80\x9d Next she yanks off the sheets, bags them and makes up the bed. She dusts, vacuums, empties the garbage. If a light bulb is out or the carpet needs to be shampooed, she puts in a work order. She scans the room for any last detail that she might have overlooked, then clocks out and moves on to the next one. She cleans 13 rooms a day, with suites counting as two or three.'b'Sometimes the job is that straightforward, but often she opens the door to find what all G.R.A.s dread: a \xe2\x80\x9ctrashed\xe2\x80\x9d room. \xe2\x80\x9cThey eat and they leave all the trash everywhere: cans, food,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, gesturing with her hands. She was seated at a folding table in a trailer parked in the lot of the Las Vegas Culinary Workers Union. \xe2\x80\x9cEspecially the bachelor party. Oh, my God, those are bad.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Because she is a member of the union, the job pays her $17.65 an hour. The Culinary Workers Union Local 226, chartered in 1935, is the largest union in Nevada, representing 57,000 men and women from 167 countries (its membership is 56 percent Latino and 81 percent people of color), among them cooks, bartenders, cocktail servers, porters, bellmen and guest-room attendants. The average wage of a culinary-union worker is $23 an hour, compared with the $10 an hour made by a nonunionized Las Vegas hospitality worker or the $6 an hour Almada, who is 42 and who moved to the United States from the Mexico border town of Agua Prieta (she got her green card when she was 11, and became a citizen when she was 22), was paid at her last job in a coffee-cup-making factory in Tucson.'b'She said she was fired from that job for taking two months\xe2\x80\x99 maternity leave, which prompted her and her (now former) husband to move to Las Vegas. They had heard that there were jobs at the casinos, where 90 percent of the hospitality work force on the Strip belong to the culinary union. But it was only after they divorced and she needed to support herself and her three children that Almada applied for her job at the Aria.'b'That was six years ago. Almada enumerates all the ways that the job and its attendant union membership has improved her life: free health insurance and pharmacy benefits; a pension; job security; and soon a $20,000 down payment she will use to buy her first house. Initially she didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand the benefits and regulations the union conferred. This is a common reason workers can still be taken advantage of, particularly those who don\xe2\x80\x99t speak English. For the past seven months, though, Almada has been on an extended, contractually allowed, union-sanctioned leave of absence to serve as an organizer at Mandalay Bay Casino; she is teaching other workers to read and understand their own contracts. \xe2\x80\x9cI never had these rights in Arizona,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause I had my little one, they fired me and nobody helped me. If I was working here with the union, that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t happen to me.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Amanda Fortini is a contributing editor at Elle magazine and a visiting lecturer at the University of Nevada, Las Vegas.'b''b'Boston \xe2\x80\xa2 The Pipe Fitter'b''b'By Carlo Rotella'b''b''b'From the job 'b'site in Roxbury where he works as a union pipe fitter, Nate Awan can see the building that once housed the Phillis Wheatley Middle School, from which he was expelled for fighting and other misbehavior. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was back in my knucklehead time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'The fifth child of an overwhelmed single mother, Awan was kicked out of four schools and took a bullet in the shoulder before going to prison at 18 for trying to shoot a rival gang member. While serving a three-year sentence, he resolved that he would change his fate.'b'With the help of a former prosecutor who took an interest in his rehabilitation, Awan found his way into Operation Exit, a program founded in 2014 in which the city of Boston partners with unions to channel residents with criminal backgrounds into the trades. Having sampled carpentry, sheet-metal work and other options, Awan, who had always been fascinated by welding but doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like heights, chose pipe fitting over iron work.'b'There\xe2\x80\x99s plenty of politics in the building trades, but there\xe2\x80\x99s also an ethos of craft meritocracy. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not what you look like, it\xe2\x80\x99s what your clevis hangers look like,\xe2\x80\x9d Awan said, referring to the brackets used to support pipes. \xe2\x80\x9cAll at the same level, nothing crooked. It\xe2\x80\x99s about your work ethic.\xe2\x80\x9d He\xe2\x80\x99s already planning to get his 5-year-old son into the local when he\xe2\x80\x99s old enough.'b'Awan, who is 28 and currently a second-year apprentice making $30 an hour plus benefits, can become a journeyman in three years, and he looks forward to continuing up the scale of seniority and pay toward the top rate of $50 or so an hour. He still lives near the Four Corners section of Dorchester where he grew up, but he\xe2\x80\x99s shopping for a house outside the city. \xe2\x80\x9cYou come from no money for food, nothing, to where you\xe2\x80\x99re buying a house, buying a car,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYour son needs a school uniform, you can get him one.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Boston\xe2\x80\x99s mayor, Martin Walsh, intends Operation Exit to enable this kind of transformation of prospects and consciousness. Walsh, who also grew up in Dorchester and had his own troubles (a bullet grazed his leg) before putting his life on course in the building trades, says: \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve had 80 graduates come through this program, and we\xe2\x80\x99re expanding from the building trades into coding and culinary arts. Some of these guys were impact players on the street. It has an effect.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Programs that reduce crime by connecting offenders and potential offenders to meaningful work are getting more attention across the country. Some, like Operation Exit, focus on re-entry after prison; others, like the Chicago CRED initiative recently started by the Emerson Collective and Arne Duncan, the former U.S. secretary of education, try to reduce gun violence by teaching job and life skills to young men adrift from both school and the labor market. It takes a significant investment of time and resources to shift a life from a dead-end trajectory to a viable future in this way, but Awan testifies to the approach\xe2\x80\x99s effectiveness. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m no surgeon or big-time prosecutor,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut for me \xe2\x80\x94 a product of his environment, taken from my mother at 9, incarcerated at 18, sweeping and mopping in the hole in prison for 19 cents an hour, living like a peasant \xe2\x80\x94 this here is a lottery ticket.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Carlo Rotella is the director of American studies at Boston College and the author, most recently, of \xe2\x80\x9cPlaying in Time.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b''b''b''b''b''b'I'b'n Philadelphia, before the first game of the N.B.A. season, Russell Westbrook worked through his warm-up routine. He was loose and laughing but \xe2\x80\x94 as always \xe2\x80\x94 precise. Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s internal clock had driven him to be the first player out on the court, a full three hours before tipoff, and he was performing his routine in front of a largely empty arena. He started with chip-ins and free throws, easy stuff to get his body going, and then moved on to his signature shot: the pull-up jumper. Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s pull-up is one of modern basketball\xe2\x80\x99s most recognizable weapons \xe2\x80\x94 if not yet quite on the level of Kareem\xe2\x80\x99s skyhook or Jordan\xe2\x80\x99s turnaround or Dirk\xe2\x80\x99s one-legged fadeaway, then at least at the edge of that territory. The move is a surprise attack. Westbrook is unreasonably fast and aggressive, a flying, screaming whirlwind of ferocious highlight dunks, and he charges the hoop with so much raging menace that defenders have no choice but to scramble backward to try to stop him. That\xe2\x80\x99s when he hits them with the pull-up: He stops instantly and \xe2\x80\x94 while the defender\xe2\x80\x99s momentum continues to drag him backward \xe2\x80\x94 leaps perfectly straight into the air, like a fighter pilot ejecting from his cockpit to escape an explosion, except that Russell Westbrook 'b'is'b' the explosion: He is the explosion and the control all at once. The whole thing seems to defy the laws of motion. Growing up in Los Angeles, Westbrook practiced this move so many tens of thousands of times on the playground that he and his father referred to it as \xe2\x80\x9cthe cotton shot,\xe2\x80\x9d because they always expected it to go through the net.'b'Now, in Philadelphia, in front of the ushers and ball boys, Westbrook practiced his cotton shot. He backed up almost to half-court, squared his huge shoulders, dipped, charged, stopped, rose, fired \xe2\x80\x94 and missed. He backed up and tried again. Again he missed.'b'It was only warm-ups, but everything surrounding Westbrook in that moment seemed historic, and the misses struck me as a bad sign. I watched him shoot 11 times from the same spot and make only three. At one point, he missed five in a row. Russell Westbrook was cold. The cotton shot, at a very inconvenient moment, seemed to have turned to iron.'b'As season openers go, this game was unusually loaded with expectations. It was a sort of Independence Day: the first game of Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s professional career without Kevin Durant as his teammate. For eight seasons, Westbrook and Durant were one of the great inscrutable duos in all of sports, superstars with wildly opposing personalities and playing styles, overachieving together in Oklahoma City, one of the smallest markets in the N.B.A. Durant was basketball\xe2\x80\x99s greatest prodigy since LeBron James, a mild-mannered, long-limbed scoring genius with a baby face and a golden jump shot. Westbrook was the scowling underdog on a never-ending mission to prove the entire universe wrong.'b'On the scale of creative tension, Westbrook-Durant fell somewhere between Lennon-McCartney and Goofus-Gallant. They clashed and blended, encouraged and diminished one another, in ways that were hard to parse. Durant led the league in scoring; Westbrook led the league in turnovers. Durant was the metronome; Westbrook the guitar solo. Durant was the scenic cliff; Westbrook the waterfall raging primally over the top of it. Occasionally, TV cameras would catch the two of them squabbling during a timeout, but they always appeared at the media table together afterward to insist that they were friends and brothers. Durant once called Westbrook his \xe2\x80\x9chype man\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 the Flavor Flav to his Chuck D. The whole relationship was a puzzle. Were they real friends, work friends, secret rivals, frenemies, secret real-world frenemies? On sports TV, Westbrook and Durant inspired as much talking-head bloviation as a celebrity affair.'b'The most surprising thing about the partnership, however, was how well it worked. Westbrook and Durant turned the Oklahoma City Thunder, against all odds, into one of the reigning powers in the N.B.A. In 2012, when both stars were still only 23 \xe2\x80\x94 an age at which most players are just beginning to find their footing \xe2\x80\x94 the Thunder made it all the way to the finals. In a six-year span, they reached the Western Conference finals four times. (It took major injuries to keep them out.) The only thing Durant and Westbrook never did together was win a championship. They came agonizingly close, including last season, when they held a 3-1 lead in the playoffs against the mighty Golden State Warriors. But they never could quite push over the top. Still, it seemed inevitable that their day would eventually come.'b'Until last July, when Durant blew everyone\xe2\x80\x99s minds by leaving Westbrook, suddenly and gracelessly, to join the aforementioned Warriors, making their superteam even more super. Just like that, Westbrook was on his own. The hype man was out front. And anyone who knew anything about basketball was dying to see what would happen next. It seemed less like a question of sports than a Zen koan: What is the sound of one hand clapping?'b'If anyone could answer that, could explore its full range of sonic possibilities, it was Russell Westbrook. The sound might be wonderful or terrible, or wonderfully terrible, or it might be nothing at all. The only certainty was that we were all about to hear it, at maximum volume, for an entire season.'b''b'That night in 'b'Philadelphia, Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s poor warm-up shooting carried over into the game, not only for himself but also for his teammates. He was setting players up for easy shots \xe2\x80\x94 layups and open threes \xe2\x80\x94 but they kept blowing them. When Westbrook tried on his own, his luck was no better. He missed tough contested jumpers, drove into crowds of defenders and was blocked, forced passes and threw the ball away. The 76ers were widely expected to be the worst team in the league this season, but after five minutes they led the Thunder 16-5. The Philadelphia crowd was going crazy.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe scared!\xe2\x80\x9d the people behind me kept screaming.'b'This storm of negativity triggered one of those Westbrook moments when he seems to pop out of space-time. Off a Sixers turnover, Westbrook took the ball near half-court, charged toward the rim, drew a hard foul and still somehow exploded forward, twisting between two defenders, to toss in a layup from a steep angle. He stood there for a second after the whistle, berating the crowd. A courtside fan \xe2\x80\x94 a bespectacled white man in a bulging Allen Iverson jersey \xe2\x80\x94 screamed back at Westbrook and flipped him off with both hands.'b'Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s face, in happy moments, is smooth and boyish, with deep dimples and a classic beauty mark on his cheek; his facial hair grows in tiny sparse wisps that suggest an eighth grader who hasn\xe2\x80\x99t yet learned how to shave. (Westbrook claims that he has never actually shaved.) But now his sweet face changed. The bright, round eyes narrowed and darkened; the lips twisted into a sneer. Westbrook, when he needs it, has weapons-grade powers of stinkface, and he unleashed their full nuclear payload on the fan. He pointed him out to the referees in disgusted disbelief. Security came to escort the man away. Westbrook was still shaking his head in fury as he stepped up to make the free throw, and on the next few possessions he seemed lit up by anger, driving and passing and scoring so relentlessly that the fans behind me stopped taunting him and grew quiet, then started complaining about the man in the Iverson jersey, asking why the hell he had thought it was a good idea to antagonize Westbrook in the first place. Animated by rage, Westbrook carried his team, and by halftime the score was tied. Westbrook walked off the floor cursing fiercely.'b'The second half brought plenty more to be angry about. No one but Westbrook could create anything, and no one could finish the things he created. After OKC\xe2\x80\x99s center, Steven Adams, managed to miss an easy alley-oop Westbrook had set him up for, the crowd started chanting: \xe2\x80\x9cK.D. LEFT YOU!\xe2\x80\x9d ('b'clap, clap, clap-clap-clap'b') \xe2\x80\x9cK.D. LEFT YOU!\xe2\x80\x9d So Westbrook started shooting, and \xe2\x80\x94 just as he had in warm-ups \xe2\x80\x94 he missed and he missed, but still he didn\xe2\x80\x99t stop. At all times, Westbrook surfs right on the perilous line between confidence and recklessness, and tonight he was surfing and wiping out hard, only to get back up and immediately wipe out again.'b'With just under three minutes left and the Thunder trailing by 3 points, Westbrook dribbled hard at the basket and, without any apparent shadow of self-doubt, stopped to rise for a pull-up jumper \xe2\x80\x94 precisely the shot I had watched him clank so many times before the game. Now, however, he made it, and then with around a minute left he did it again, giving the Thunder the lead, and in the end, as the crowd gurgled on its own disappointment, the Thunder won by 6 points. Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s final stat line was dangerously overstuffed: 32 points, 12 rebounds, nine assists. He should probably have had closer to 15 assists, but that stat depends largely on the skill of the players around you, and with this group, nine seemed like a minor miracle.'b'After the game, Westbrook sat at his locker wearing only a towel, with the veins bulging out of his legs so hard he looked like a map of the human circulatory system. (Joe Sharpe, the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s head trainer, told me that Westbrook could probably be a bodybuilder if he wanted to.) Slowly, while the media watched in silence, Westbrook started getting dressed. He put on underwear covered with the words \xe2\x80\x9cWhy Not?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s personal slogan, which he invokes to justify everything from his fashion choices to his shot selection. (As he tweeted once, apropos of nothing: \xe2\x80\x9cWHYNOT?!!! #whynot #whynot\xe2\x80\x9d) He pulled on a pair of skinny black jeans, ripped at the knees, then a bright yellow sweatshirt that said, on the front, \xe2\x80\x9cParanormal: Out of the Ordinary.\xe2\x80\x9d Over this he put on a denim jacket.'b'It was, I thought, an interesting outfit.'b'But there was one more piece, and it was the coup de gr\xc3\xa2ce: Over his jeans, Westbrook pulled on a kilt.'b'He rubbed some Vaseline on his face and turned to face the media for an official round of questions. As usual, he spoke to the reporters without ever looking at them. It was as if he were being interviewed by the locker-room door, with which he maintained fierce eye contact, with occasional follow-up questions from the ceiling tiles or the carpet.'b'Why are you wearing a kilt? someone asked.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook said.'b'And so began life after Kevin Durant.'b''b'Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s season has'b' a chance to be historic because of one magical statistic: the triple-double. A triple-double means, simply, that a player has reached double figures in three major categories during the course of a game \xe2\x80\x94 almost always points, rebounds and assists. If that sounds like dry math, it is not. In the N.B.A., the triple-double is a sacred threshold of excellence.'b'Triple-doubles are difficult and rare. You don\xe2\x80\x99t stumble into one \xe2\x80\x94 it takes a special kind of player, a dominant generalist. Even the outliers capable of achieving them tend to get only a handful per year; Magic Johnson, the modern king of the stat, got 18 in his best season. A triple-double is not a statistical parlor trick, like hitting for the cycle in baseball. It actually captures the essence of basketball, the way the game allows every player to do everything on the floor whenever the opportunity arises. To get a triple-double, a player has to control the action physically, strategically and socially; he has to become a funnel through which the energy of an entire game pours.'b'Westbrook has always had the potential to ring up triple-doubles, and in past seasons he has put together impressive flurries of them. This season, he has gone bananas. Some games, it feels as if Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s stats are moving in fast-forward \xe2\x80\x94 as if he is pulling numbers out of wormholes that only he can access. One night, Westbrook generated 10 assists in only 15 minutes; another, he finished with 26 points, 11 rebounds and 22 assists \xe2\x80\x94 totals not seen since Johnson himself. For years, the popular narrative stressed how much Durant was sacrificing to fit in next to Westbrook. It has become clear that the opposite was certainly just as true.'b'Westbrook is now playing against history. The Holy Grail of triple-doubling is not just to pile up a ridiculous number of them but to actually 'b'average'b' a triple-double for an entire season. This has only happened once, in 1961-62, when Oscar Robertson averaged 30.8 points, 12.5 rebounds, and 11.4 assists \xe2\x80\x94 numbers straight from the mouth of God. Until recently, his feat seemed as untouchable as DiMaggio\xe2\x80\x99s 56-game hitting streak or Wilt\xe2\x80\x99s 100-point game. Matching Robertson\xe2\x80\x99s record is doubly tricky because not only was he an aberrant superfreak, he also happened to play in an unusually fast era, when teams flew up and down the court generating extra possessions in which to rack up gaudy statistics. Today\xe2\x80\x99s players are limited by the modern game\xe2\x80\x99s slow pace. Even LeBron James, the greatest player of his generation and someone whose gifts seem precision-engineered to produce triple-doubles \xe2\x80\x94 size for rebounding, vision for assists, speed and agility for scoring \xe2\x80\x94 has never come close to averaging one for an entire season. That\xe2\x80\x99s how basketball works: There are unavoidable trade-offs, pragmatic allocations of resources. Averaging a triple-double is like having a helicopter that is also a boat that can also write the Great American Novel. It is more than just an engineering impossibility \xe2\x80\x94 it is an existential cheat.'b'When Durant left last summer, some fans speculated that Westbrook might have a chance to pull this madness off. Rational people, myself included, responded with scornful laughter. It was absurd, particularly for a player who was only 6-foot-3 and surrounded by mediocre shooters. To actually average a triple-double would be not only historic and impressive but also a little weird, almost disturbing.'b'But this is where we have to take into account Russell Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s personality.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a different dude,\xe2\x80\x9d Anthony Morrow, a Thunder shooting guard, told me.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s weird, yeah,\xe2\x80\x9d Steven Adams said. He paused. \xe2\x80\x9cBizarre.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused again. \xe2\x80\x9cNot normal.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Westbrook is not merely a superelite athlete (he is commonly referred to as the most athletic player in the N.B.A.); he also has a highly unusual mind. He is moody, stubborn, loyal and fiercely private, a control freak and a perfectionist. Outside a very small circle of family and friends, he refuses to be known. The triple-doubles, their sheer unreasonableness, are as much a reflection of this personality as of any particular basketball skills. Each time he registers one it is like a signal \xe2\x80\x94 a ping from the sealed box of his private mind.'b''b'Russell Westbrook III'b' grew up 1,300 miles from Oklahoma City, in Los Angeles. He was born in 1988, under the smiling moon of Magic Johnson. Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s father, Russell Westbrook Jr., worked at a bread factory and played basketball in his free time on the local playgrounds. When I asked Westbrook III what kind of player his dad was, he burst into laughter.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cPlayer?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cPlayer!?\xe2\x80\x9d It was as if he had never thought of his father that way before. \xe2\x80\x9cMan, they argued the whole time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I remember most. They played for five minutes and argued for 40 minutes. All day.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s father was strict and devoted, determined above all to keep his family out of the way of trouble. When Westbrook showed an interest in basketball, his father devised endless drills that they ran through, just the two of them, for hours at a time. They focused on the midrange jumper, the cotton shot, practicing it off one foot and two feet, on the move and standing still.
