Westbrook learned to identify all the possible ways you can mess the shot up: being off balance, moving too fast, not having your feet set. \xe2\x80\x9cA lot of times, I make it when those things are not right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all my dad. That\xe2\x80\x99s all him and myself, basically.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Westbrook constantly invokes his family \xe2\x80\x94 his parents, his brother, his wife \xe2\x80\x94 to explain why he is the way he is, but he won\xe2\x80\x99t allow reporters to talk to them. When I asked if I could speak with his father or brother, he told me he would ask his mother instead \xe2\x80\x94 and then added, immediately: \xe2\x80\x9cMy mom never talks. She never talks. She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like to talk.\xe2\x80\x9d (I never spoke with her.)'b'As a high school player, Westbrook was spectacularly unheralded. He was the shortest kid on his team, not even able to dunk until his senior year. Although he already possessed most of the qualities that would eventually make him great \xe2\x80\x94 the crazy speed and fearlessness, the motor that never stopped \xe2\x80\x94 basketball was fundamentally a game of size, and Westbrook didn\xe2\x80\x99t have it. A lifetime of arguing on playgrounds looked as if it might be his best case.'b'Then came a miraculous growth spurt. Toward the end of high school, Westbrook shot up seven inches, from 5-foot-8 to 6-foot-3. Suddenly, all his intangible gifts were attached to formidable size. Westbrook was still not an elite recruit \xe2\x80\x94 it was too late for that \xe2\x80\x94 but he was good enough to earn a last-minute scholarship to U.C.L.A. after one of its star players declared unexpectedly for the N.B.A. draft. Westbrook was a bench player, a defensive specialist; his job was to enter the game and wreak havoc. During his freshman year, he averaged only nine minutes and 3 points a game. That same year, at the University of Texas, the freshman Kevin Durant was playing 36 minutes a game and averaging 26 points and 11 rebounds \xe2\x80\x94 by many reckonings, the greatest freshman year in N.C.A.A. basketball history.'b'During his sophomore year, Westbrook played more of a central role at U.C.L.A., and his powerful dunks made him a hero of ESPN highlight reels. Still, no one was in danger of confusing him with Kevin Durant, who was by then winning N.B.A. Rookie of the Year. Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s second-year averages remained relatively modest \xe2\x80\x94 13 points, four assists, four rebounds \xe2\x80\x94 but it was enough to entice the Seattle SuperSonics (soon to be, scandalously, the Oklahoma City Thunder) to take him in the 2008 N.B.A. draft. They picked him fourth, a position almost everyone else thought was a serious reach. As a Seattle Times columnist put it the day after the draft: \xe2\x80\x9cEquipped with two first-round picks, they came away with Westbrook and Serge Ibaka. That\xe2\x80\x99s like getting two free airline tickets and booking trips to Gary, Ind., and Kosovo.\xe2\x80\x9d No one was more surprised on draft night than Westbrook himself, who was afraid he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to be picked at all. \xe2\x80\x9cI was like: Damn, am I gonna be the only one left in the greenroom?\xe2\x80\x9d he told me. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is about to be embarrassing.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In theory, in the beginning, Westbrook and Durant seemed like a perfectly complementary pair: the scrappy defensive specialist next to the once-in-a-generation scorer. Westbrook could defer to Durant on offense and cover his shortcomings on defense. Instead, Westbrook took over the team.'b'He had few of the skills that defined a traditional point guard: panoramic vision, flawless ball-handling, a deadly outside shot. But his personality was more suited to leading than Durant\xe2\x80\x99s was. This, in fact, was a major part of why the team drafted Westbrook, for his unique psychological profile: his ability to withstand crushing stress and pressure and doubt without losing confidence or focus. Many of Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s elite skills were mental, and he would need them right away. The Thunder started his rookie season 3-29, and there were serious debates about whether they were the worst team ever. Westbrook led the league in turnovers, and the team\xe2\x80\x99s ownership was widely reviled for stealing the franchise from Seattle. Even when the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s fortunes changed the following season, when they made the playoffs and almost beat the world-champion Lakers, and then the following season, when Westbrook averaged 22 points a game and became an All-Star and the team made it all the way to the Western Conference finals \xe2\x80\x94 even then, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t particularly easy. A narrative was beginning to develop: Westbrook was a volatile, reckless ball hog, an inferior player who refused to defer to the clearly superior Durant.'b''b'Mo Cheeks is 'b'the Russell Westbrook whisperer. Cheeks was a legendary 1980s point guard for the 76ers, an unheralded prospect who turned himself, through the power of scrappiness, into a recurrent All-Star and an N.B.A. champion, the point guard for Dr. J and Moses Malone and Charles Barkley. Cheeks joined the Thunder as an assistant coach in Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s second year, and Westbrook has been his special assignment ever since.'b'Early on, Cheeks and other coaches bullied Westbrook around the floor while he dribbled to improve his ball-handling. When Westbrook had emotional outbursts in the middle of games, Cheeks was the one to talk him down. They still work together early every morning: Westbrook is always the first player to the gym, hours before practice starts, and he will pull Cheeks out of the morning coaches\xe2\x80\x99 meeting so they can run through shooting and post moves.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a driven man,\xe2\x80\x9d Cheeks told me. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a very disciplined person. Never late, always on time.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'I asked Cheeks if he\xe2\x80\x99d ever been around a player like Westbrook.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x94 I \xe2\x80\x94 I don\xe2\x80\x99t know many with that explosiveness,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe power and the speed.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'What about Barkley? Or Allen Iverson?'b'\xe2\x80\x9cCharles was powerful, but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t have the speed of this guy,\xe2\x80\x9d Cheeks said. \xe2\x80\x9cIverson\xe2\x80\x99s a good name. He played like that \xe2\x80\x94 that same ferociousness, same competitive drive.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But Iverson, infamously, was not a fan of practice, and Westbrook treats every practice as if it\xe2\x80\x99s Game 7 of a playoff series. This is something that surprised Billy Donovan, the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s head coach, when he joined the organization last season. Even at the beginning of a morning walk-through, when the coaches were just trying to get everyone loosened up, Westbrook would take his first touch of the ball and explode as if he\xe2\x80\x99d been shot out of a cannon to throw down a tomahawk dunk. He is like this always. \xe2\x80\x9cWe play 82 games,\xe2\x80\x9d Donovan said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are gonna be nights when your team just doesn\xe2\x80\x99t have it. Four games in five nights, you\xe2\x80\x99re tired, you\xe2\x80\x99re beaten down, it\xe2\x80\x99s a long trip, there\xe2\x80\x99s flight issues. Russell is so unique that it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t happen to him. It\xe2\x80\x99s nuts.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my 39th year,\xe2\x80\x9d Cheeks said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve asked many people, and no one really has had an explanation for this guy. This guy plays with power and speed all the time. Like, all the time. This is kind of a \xe2\x80\x98cool\xe2\x80\x99 league. You don\xe2\x80\x99t play like that all the time. He does it 'b'all'b' the time. Practice. Preseason games. A lot of guys make the All-Star team, but how many have been two-time All-Star M.V.P.?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The answer is: Several, but only Russell Westbrook has been unreasonable enough to do it in consecutive years. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m good enough to relax and be cool,\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook told me. \xe2\x80\x9cJust be chillin\xe2\x80\x99 and be cool.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'I asked Semaj Christon, one of the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s backup point guards, what it was like to guard Westbrook in practice every day.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a problem,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not fun,\xe2\x80\x9d Anthony Morrow told me. \xe2\x80\x9cI done seen him literally hurt people, on offense, and it\xe2\x80\x99s not an offensive foul.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In the swirling cloud of contradiction that surrounds Westbrook, one paradox stands out. He often looks, on the court, like a force of pure chaos: a wild, petulant, fire-breathing hothead. And yet he is also, especially in his personal life, relentlessly devoted to order and control. He builds his days around a series of inflexible routines: calls to his parents, a designated parking spot, morning shooting on Court 3. He expects every room to be neat and clean, at work and at home.'b'Before games, Westbrook always eats a peanut-butter-and-jelly sandwich, and it must be prepared just so: bread cut on a diagonal, fillings spread very thin. (In Oklahoma, the team chefs know exactly how to do it, but on the road Westbrook can\xe2\x80\x99t trust anyone, so he makes it himself.) Three hours before tipoff, Westbrook warms up; at T-minus-60 minutes he goes to chapel. When the pregame countdown clock hits precisely 6:17 \xe2\x80\x94 never a second more or less \xe2\x80\x94 Westbrook leaps off the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s bench and screams, \xe2\x80\x9cTwo lines,\xe2\x80\x9d initiating the team\xe2\x80\x99s final layup drill. I asked Westbrook if there was some kind of numerology behind this \xe2\x80\x94 a June 17 birthday, a favorite Bible verse. \xe2\x80\x9cNo particular reason,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI just do it. Nothing special.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Westbrook is unbelievably attuned to the tiniest details of his shoes. He has his own personal rack at the practice facility. \xe2\x80\x9cMost guys, I can just bring a pair of shoes,\xe2\x80\x9d the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s equipment manager, Wilson Taylor, told me. \xe2\x80\x9cRussell wants a certain pair. He knows which are for games, which are for practice. He determines which shoes are coming on a road trip.\xe2\x80\x9d When Nike sent Westbrook its latest model, the Jordan 31, he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t happy with the sole flexibility, so he asked the company to build him a special hybrid using the previous model\xe2\x80\x99s sole. Even in that custom shoe, however, the insoles aren\xe2\x80\x99t thick enough for Westbrook, so Taylor replaces each of them by hand.'b'On the court, what looks like madness is often deliberate. Westbrook is obsessive about studying film. He insists on knowing every play from every position. Steven Adams told me that Westbrook occasionally wakes him up in the middle of the night, on the team plane, to show him an angle he might have missed. \xe2\x80\x9cI ain\xe2\x80\x99t gonna lie, bud,\xe2\x80\x9d Adams said. \xe2\x80\x9cWatching video, especially after a game, at like 2 in the morning \xe2\x80\x94 it\xe2\x80\x99s rather hard to stay up. He maintains the same focus. It\xe2\x80\x99s amazing.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Everyone had stories like these. I got the feeling that Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s personality, the sheer intensity of his presence, is an energy field that seeps into every square centimeter of the Thunder organization. When Westbrook is around, everyone is aware of him, and he is never not around. Players arrive at the gym in the morning to find Westbrook already there. Sometimes they come in at night to put up extra shots, and Westbrook will already be there again, in the weight room, working out. You can begin to imagine how Kevin Durant, a talent with equal or greater claim to the organization but not nearly Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s force of personality, might have been driven to look for his own space elsewhere.'b'Although everyone who works with Westbrook gushes about him endlessly \xe2\x80\x94 he is loyal and generous and as real as a human could ever possibly be, they say \xe2\x80\x94 he also has a talent for keeping the people around him slightly on edge. He has a foulmouthed, teasing charm, and a formidableness that makes people think twice, and often a third and fourth time, before mentioning something they\xe2\x80\x99re not sure he\xe2\x80\x99ll like. Perhaps this tension is another way Westbrook has found to maintain focus, in himself and in others \xe2\x80\x94 the social equivalent of grinding a blade across a rough stone to keep it sharp.'b''b'I first met'b' Westbrook while writing an article for this magazine in 2012, and we did not exactly hit it off. He kept me waiting for over an hour for an interview that was spectacularly unproductive; despite my best efforts to keep our conversation going, it petered out in less than 10 minutes, some of which time Westbrook actually spent on the phone telling a friend that he was currently doing an interview but would be done with it very soon. It felt almost as if he were performing some kind of art project.'b'He shut down questions with ruthless efficiency. The P.R. staff had mentioned to me that Westbrook was famous in the organization for giving everyone nicknames: Alfalfa, Ace Ventura, Snakes on a Plane. I asked him what nicknames he\xe2\x80\x99d given his teammates.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cThose are my secrets,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t let out secrets.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The most revealing thing took place afterward, when Westbrook went into the P.R. office to banter with the staff. He sat down to look over a stack of contracts. (Westbrook handles most of his own business; he often sits in the back of the practice facility to pay his bills.) He admired a new addition to the room \xe2\x80\x94 a big, free-standing, color-coded season schedule \xe2\x80\x94 and asked the P.R. guys if he could have it. They said no, they were using it to plan the season, but they would be happy to get another one printed out especially for him. Westbrook asked why he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t just take this one right now, and then they could get another schedule printed for themselves. That way he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have to wait. He was the player, he said. Shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t he have priority?'b'Even in the middle of the P.R. office, in other words, Westbrook was having a P.R. problem. He was being a jerk. Watching this performance in person, however, even immediately after my unsuccessful interview, I could see why everyone liked him anyway. Westbrook wasn\xe2\x80\x99t only a jerk, or at least not uncomplicatedly a jerk. By traditional social standards, saying the things Westbrook was saying is bad manners. But that\xe2\x80\x99s in the same way that, by traditional basketball standards, it\xe2\x80\x99s a bad idea to charge into a one-on-four fast break and pull up for a contested free-throw-line jumper. He was a social gambler as well as a basketball gambler, one of those people who know how to play on the line between charisma and rudeness, teasing and affection, especially among people he likes.'b'Westbrook started to sign his stack of documents. He held the pen in his left hand.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cLittle-known fact about Russell,\xe2\x80\x9d one of the P.R. guys said to me. \xe2\x80\x9cHe shoots with his right hand, it says he\xe2\x80\x99s right-handed in the media guide, but he writes with his left.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s head snapped up. He looked straight at me, where I was leaning against the door frame.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t put that in your article,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'I told him we could make a deal: I would keep it out of my article if he would tell me one of his teammates\xe2\x80\x99 nicknames.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t get to bargain with me,\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook said \xe2\x80\x94 and then he added a curse word with so much venom it made me laugh out loud. He went back to signing papers with his left hand.'b''b'A month into'b' the 2016 season, Westbrook arrived in New York agonizingly close to the impossible Oscar Robertson dream: averaging a triple-double. The only stat holding him back was rebounding. He was at 9.9 per game \xe2\x80\x94 an extremely high number, historic in itself for a point guard of his size, frankly a little unrealistic. But still: single digits. The statisticians had crunched and crunched and determined that at Madison Square Garden that night, Westbrook would need to pull down 11 rebounds in order to get his average up to 10.'b'Rebounding has always been one of Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s superpowers. He is athletic enough to leap through vast spaces, strong enough to bully people in close combat and, most important, persistent enough to get himself, with unholy urgency, to the places around the rim most likely to yield rebounds. I have seen Westbrook streak in from a distant corner of the floor to tip in a missed 3-pointer off the glass \xe2\x80\x94 a hurtling, perfectly timed run that looked almost like a center fielder\xe2\x80\x99s sprinting back to leap and steal a home run just as it cleared the wall.'b'In the end, every rebound comes down to one small coordinate somewhere near the basket: a grapefruit of space that materializes suddenly, unpredictably, and then disappears in a second, almost like the golden snitch in quidditch. Westbrook is frighteningly good at getting to that elusive point before anyone else, almost regardless of his starting position, and making it his own. He owns the grapefruit of space.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cSome of these rebounds over power forwards and centers \xe2\x80\x94 that\xe2\x80\x99s will,\xe2\x80\x9d Morrow told me. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see it in his eyes. It\xe2\x80\x99s just a level of focus and will that I have never seen before. Never. Nobody. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been around a lot of focused dudes, but to be consistently like that \xe2\x80\x94 that\xe2\x80\x99s crazy.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'At Madison Square Garden, Westbrook needed 11 rebounds to cross the sacred threshold, and started with the Knicks\xe2\x80\x99 first shot: It came off the back of the rim, and Westbrook casually took it out of the air with his left hand. Three minutes later, a 3-pointer rattled in and out and popped directly into Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s hands. The ball seemed to seek him out, over and over, and when it didn\xe2\x80\x99t, he was perfectly willing to go out of his way to get it. In the second quarter, Westbrook rained down a storm of stats on the Knicks that included a series of crucial and difficult rebounds, several of which looked even more impressive because they came at the expense of Kristaps Porzingis, New York\xe2\x80\x99s gigantic young center, who at 7-foot-3 is a full foot taller than Westbrook. Twice, Porzingis blocked Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s shot only to see Westbrook end up with the ball; another time, Westbrook sprang off both feet and violently snatched a rebound out of Porzingis\xe2\x80\x99s very high hands.'b'By the end of the first half, Westbrook had already collected nine rebounds and was on the brink of a triple-double, which Knicks fans were openly cheering for him to get. The triple-double arrived 79 seconds into the second half, at which point the journalists around me started tweeting furiously that, at only 20 minutes of game action, it was the fastest triple-double of the modern era \xe2\x80\x94 until the Thunder P.R. staff pointed out that, as a matter of fact, Westbrook did one the previous year in under 18 minutes. (Westbrook now holds the second-, third- and fourth-fastest triple-doubles in N.B.A. history.)'b'Even with his triple-double secured, Westbrook would not stop rebounding. My favorite of the night was his 14th: he drove, drew three defenders, passed to a teammate for a wide-open jump shot and ended up deep out of bounds. Instead of just hanging out there, admiring his handiwork, Westbrook turned, tracked the shot with his eyes, saw that it was falling short and, at precisely the moment the ball hit the front of the rim, exploded into the air with shocking intensity. It did not look like a normal professional basketball player trying to get his hand on a ball to extend a possession late in the third quarter of the 19th game of the season. It looked like a man in the middle of a winner-takes-all dunk contest against the Devil himself to prevent the incineration of planet Earth. It looked as if Westbrook were insulted by the very concept of distance, and so he annihilated it, soaring and spearing the ball out of the air from between two waiting Knicks. Spike Lee, courtside, was standing to celebrate the initial missed shot, but when Westbrook came flying in to seize it, he clutched his head in exasperation and hopelessness and despair.'b'By the time the game ended, Westbrook had 27 points, 14 assists and 17 rebounds \xe2\x80\x94 far more than he needed. He was officially averaging a triple-double, and then some.'b'This came in the middle of what would turn out to be a streak of seven straight games with triple-doubles \xe2\x80\x94 a feat so outrageous it was last done by Michael Jordan in 1989. Westbrook and his numbers became a leaguewide spectacle. A few weeks later, after hundreds of questions about it, Westbrook expressed annoyance at the whole phenomenon.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHonestly, man,\xe2\x80\x9d he told reporters, \xe2\x80\x9cthis triple-double thing is kind of getting on my nerves. People think if I don\xe2\x80\x99t get it, it\xe2\x80\x99s like a big thing. When I do get it, it\xe2\x80\x99s a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d He added, \xe2\x80\x9cFor the 100th time, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But this of course was hard to believe, because Westbrook cares about everything.'b''b'In Oklahoma City'b' in December, on a day off between his triple-doubles, Russell Westbrook sat on the Rolling Thunder Book Bus. This is a staple of the team\xe2\x80\x99s community-outreach program: a school bus that has been converted into a mobile library. That afternoon it was parked, under the wide blue Great Plains sky, in front of a suburban elementary school. Westbrook was there early, long before the kids started lining up, before even the team\xe2\x80\x99s P.R. director arrived. He sat in the bus\xe2\x80\x99s driver\xe2\x80\x99s seat, sheltered from the Oklahoma wind, waiting, passing the time by cranking the tall doors open and closed with a lever near the steering wheel.'b'Open, closed, open, closed.'b'Eventually, a line of third graders formed, and Westbrook prepared himself by stockpiling, methodically in his left hand, a supply of bookmarks and bracelets, which he would hand to the kids as they came through. One by one, the students walked to the bus door. When they saw Westbrook sitting at the top of the stairs, they were variously awed or thrilled or confused or stoic.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cAre you really Russell Westbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d a little boy asked him.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook said.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cI met you at a gas station before!\xe2\x80\x9d one girl said.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was at the game last night!\xe2\x80\x9d another said.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was, too!\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook answered.'b'He directed the third graders toward their favorite reading: Captain Underpants, Goosebumps, Bunnicula, Bad Kitty. (The Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s backup point guard, Semaj Christon, was in the back, helping them find specific books.) \xe2\x80\x9cHave fun in school,\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook told everyone as they left the bus.'b'Midway through the session, a boy stepped onto the Book Bus with one of his shoelaces dragging behind him.'b'Westbrook looked down at the floor.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cTie your shoe, man,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'The boy looked down, too, then kept walking to the back of the bus and picked a book.'b'When the boy re-emerged, Westbrook handed him a bracelet and a bookmark.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHave fun in school,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd tie your shoe up.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The boy walked off the bus, shoelace still flopping around.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t forget to tie your shoe!\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook shouted out the door.'b'From that point on in his Book Bus session, Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s focus was locked onto the children\xe2\x80\x99s shoelaces, a strangely high percentage of which seemed to be loose. \xe2\x80\x9cYour shoe is loose,\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook would say as a child walked onto the bus. \xe2\x80\x9cTie your shoe up.\xe2\x80\x9d He was cheerful but firm; he seemed genuinely concerned. Dozens of kids passed through the bus, and Westbrook scrutinized each of their shoes, and not a single loose lace was allowed to pass without comment. Over and over, he told the kids to tie their shoes. Even when the laces were not all the way untied, just trending in that direction, Westbrook pointed it out: \xe2\x80\x9cTie your shoes up.\xe2\x80\x9d It was as if he had identified a public safety epidemic that he was single-handedly going to fix, one child at a time.'b'One boy stepped onto the bus with both of his shoes intentionally untied, laces dragging like catfish whiskers.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou like to wear your shoes like that, huh?\xe2\x80\x9d Westbrook said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I used to do, so I get it. But you gotta tuck \xe2\x80\x99em in.\xe2\x80\x9d And before the boy could go back and get his book, Westbrook actually knelt down on the floor of the bus and tucked the boy\xe2\x80\x99s shoelaces into the sides of his shoes.'b'After a while, the Book Bus session ended, and the kids and the players took a photo together outside, and then all the kids returned to their classrooms, laces safely snug on their feet, and Russell Westbrook drove off in a white Cadillac sedan, flexing his mind-muscle on other problems.'b''b'Sometimes a place'b' and a person align in unexpectedly powerful ways. For its entire existence, Oklahoma City has suffered from a particularly nasty case of a particularly American curse: volatility. It was founded in 1889, in one chaotic afternoon, with a land run that brought the population instantly from zero to 10,000. Its economy is a never-ending cycle of oil-industry booms and busts. Its weather features killer tornadoes erupting suddenly out of placid skies. Even Oklahoma\xe2\x80\x99s formerly stable earth has, in recent years, begun to spasm: activities associated with hydrofracking, source of the region\xe2\x80\x99s most recent economic boom, have kicked off an escalating series of earthquake swarms.'b'The departure last summer of Kevin Durant \xe2\x80\x94 a pillar of stability for almost 10 years, a player who had talked all his career about how he wanted to stay with the Thunder forever \xe2\x80\x94 was magnified, for locals, by the knowledge of this history. This is what happens in OKC. Talented people leave, dreams die, booms go bust. Especially when things seem to be at their best, everything tends to fall apart.'b'After Durant left, there was endless speculation that Westbrook would leave, too, and it would have been hard to blame him if he had. Looking around the league, Westbrook would have seen almost nothing but bigger cities with stronger teams and better restaurants and livelier fashion scenes \xe2\x80\x94 any one of which would have bent over backward for the chance to add him to its mix. Instead, Westbrook signed a contract extension to stay in Oklahoma. It was, in some ways, a crazy choice, unreasonable on many levels, which made it of course the perfect Westbrook thing to do.'b'Westbrook once told me that growing up in Los Angeles, he had no idea Oklahoma City even existed. And yet he has become, in his odd balance of chaos and control, his volatile lurching toward greatness, a sort of human embodiment of the place. Westbrook was already beloved in OKC, but his decision to stay turned him into a legend. The city was hung with banners saying \xe2\x80\x9cWHY NOT?\xe2\x80\x9d and the tallest skyscraper lit up with Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s name: \xe2\x80\x9cTHANK YOU RUSS.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cThere are people in Oklahoma City who hold themselves differently because of Russell Westbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d Sam Presti, the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s general manager, told me. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean that literally. They stand up straighter. People, not necessarily athletes, draw confidence from him and his disregard for the judgment or labeling from others. I\xe2\x80\x99m confident that there are a lot of people in this city that go into job interviews saying to themselves: \xe2\x80\x98Come on, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got this \xe2\x80\x94 be Westbrook, think Westbrook.\xe2\x80\x99 And that might actually help them get the job, and if they don\xe2\x80\x99t, they walk out feeling sorry for the person that missed the opportunity to hire them.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'This is the lesson of Russell Westbrook. In a deeply imperfect world \xe2\x80\x94 a world where a shooting touch will suddenly abandon you at the worst possible moment, where your teammates might not be good enough to make a win possible, where an economy might suddenly collapse for no apparent reason, where the decency of strangers cannot be presumed \xe2\x80\x94 in a world like that, Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s approach to life might actually be the most rational one. You control the things you can control (family, daily routines, the occasional big choice) and outside that you fling yourself with wild abandon, every day, at every object that seems worthy of pursuit. In the absence of guarantees, in the absence of certainty, in the new American volatility, we can bank on only one thing: total presence, total sincerity, total effort, all the time. That is the sound of one hand clapping.'b'Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s critics have been very loud for many years. They are quieter today, but they\xe2\x80\x99re still out there, disputing the magnitude of his triple-doubles by pointing to his high turnover rate and his low shooting percentage, his inefficiencies, his mistakes. But, as Billy Donovan, the Thunder\xe2\x80\x99s head coach, told me, this is missing the point. \xe2\x80\x9cBefore you go to the criticism,\xe2\x80\x9d Donovan said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have to understand Russell Westbrook. The No. 1 thing for him is how hard he competes. It starts there. That doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean he plays a perfect game, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean that he never turns the ball over, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean that he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t miss a defensive assignment. But when the ball is loose on the glass or on the floor, when it requires energy and passion and fire \xe2\x80\x94 Russell is bringing that. So many players live with regret: They could have done a little bit more, been more committed, worked harder. I think he\xe2\x80\x99s going to have a great level of peace when his time is done. He\xe2\x80\x99s going to be able to move on with his life. He\xe2\x80\x99s going to have peace in his heart.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Through 41 games,'b' the official halfway point of the season, Russell Westbrook was actually doing it: He was averaging a triple-double \xe2\x80\x94 30.7 points, 10.5 assists, and 10.7 rebounds. Stat-heads calculated that, if you adjusted Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s numbers to reflect the pace of the Oscar Robertson era, they would look like nothing we\xe2\x80\x99ve ever seen in the history of anything: nearly 50 points, 17 rebounds, 17 assists. Historical comparisons are impossible to draw in such a linear way, of course. But still. It is possible that we are witnessing, in real time, a performance as aberrant as anything any of the primordial basketball legends (Wilt, Oscar, Bill Russell, Pistol Pete) ever did. We may as well start broadcasting Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s games in grainy black and white.'b'And the second half of the season may actually be better. Westbrook\xe2\x80\x99s self-mythology \xe2\x80\x94 his belief that he is an underdog \xe2\x80\x94 was no doubt stoked last month when he learned that, despite all his exorbitant numbers, despite his decision to stay in Oklahoma, despite the game-winning shots and the surprising success of his relatively untalented team, he was not chosen to start in this year\xe2\x80\x99s All-Star Game. His spot was taken by a player more popular with N.B.A. fans, the ever-sunny Steph Curry \xe2\x80\x94 Kevin Durant\xe2\x80\x99s new point guard. So Westbrook did what he does and launched himself into his next triple-double.'b''b''b''b''b''b'O'b'ne night in September 2014, when he was chief executive of Breitbart News, Stephen Bannon hosted cocktails and dinner at the Washington townhouse where he lived, a mansion near the Supreme Court that he liked to call the Breitbart Embassy. Beneath elaborate chandeliers and flanked by gold drapes and stately oil paintings, Jeff Sessions, then a senator from Alabama, sat next to the guest of honor: Nigel Farage, the insurgent British politician, who first met Sessions two years earlier when Bannon introduced them. Farage was building support for his right-wing party by complaining in the British press about \xe2\x80\x9cuncontrolled mass immigration.\xe2\x80\x9d Sessions, like other attendees, was celebrating the recent collapse in Congress of bipartisan immigration reform, which would have provided a path to citizenship for some undocumented people. At the dinner, Sessions 'b'told a writer for Vice'b', Reid Cherlin, that Bannon\xe2\x80\x99s site was instrumental in defeating the measure. Sessions read Breitbart almost every day, he explained, because it was \xe2\x80\x9cputting out cutting-edge information.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Bannon\xe2\x80\x99s role in blocking the reform had gone beyond sympathetic coverage on his site. Over the previous year, he, Sessions and one of Sessions\xe2\x80\x99s top aides, Stephen Miller, spent \xe2\x80\x9can enormous amount of time\xe2\x80\x9d meeting in person, \xe2\x80\x9cdeveloping plans and messaging and strategy,\xe2\x80\x9d as 'b'Miller later explained'b' to Rosie Gray in The Atlantic. Breitbart writers also 'b'reportedly'b' met with Sessions\xe2\x80\x99s staff for a weekly happy hour at the Union Pub. For most Republicans in Washington, immigration was an issue they wished would go away, a persistent source of conflict between the party\xe2\x80\x99s elites, who saw it as a straightforward economic good, and its middle-class voting base, who mistrusted the effects of immigration on employment. But for Bannon, Sessions and Miller, immigration was a galvanizing issue, lying at the center of their apparent vision for reshaping the United States by tethering it to its European and Christian origins. (None of them would comment for this article.) That September evening, as they celebrated the collapse of the reform effort \xe2\x80\x94 and the rise of Farage, whose own anti-immigration party in Britain represented the new brand of nativism \xe2\x80\x94 it felt like the beginning of something new. \xe2\x80\x9cI was privileged enough to be at it,\xe2\x80\x9d Miller said about the gathering last June, while a guest on Breitbart\xe2\x80\x99s SiriusXM radio show. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s going to sound like a motivational speech, but it\xe2\x80\x99s true. To all the voters out there: The only limits to what we can achieve is what we believe we can achieve.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The answer to what they could achieve, of course, is now obvious: everything. Bannon and Miller are ensconced in the West Wing, as arguably the two most influential policy advisers to Donald J. Trump. And Jeff Sessions is now the attorney general of the United States. The genesis of their working relationship is crucial to understanding the far-reaching domestic goals of the Trump presidency and how the law may be used to attain them over the next four years. Bannon and Sessions have effectively presented the country\xe2\x80\x99s changing demographics \xe2\x80\x94 the rising number of minority and foreign-born residents \xe2\x80\x94 as America\xe2\x80\x99s chief internal threat. Sessions has long been an outlier in his party on this subject; in 2013, when his Republican colleagues were talking primarily about curbing illegal immigration, he offered a proposal to curb 'b'legal'b' immigration. ('b'It failed in committee'b', 17 to one.)'b'Talking to Bannon on air in September 2015, Sessions, who has 'b'received'b' 'b'awards'b' from virulently anti-immigrant groups, 'b'described'b' the present day as a dangerous period of \xe2\x80\x9cradical change\xe2\x80\x9d for America, comparing it to the decades of the early 20th century, when waves of immigrants flooded the country. He said that the 1924 immigration quota system, which barred most Asians and tightly capped the entry of Italians, Jews, Africans and Middle Easterners, \xe2\x80\x9cwas good for America.\xe2\x80\x9d Bannon is also uncomfortable with the changing face of the country. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen two-thirds or three-quarters of the C.E.O.s in Silicon Valley are from South Asia or from Asia, I think \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9d 'b'he said'b' on the radio with Trump in November 2015, vastly exaggerating the actual numbers. \xe2\x80\x9cA country is more than an economy. We\xe2\x80\x99re a civic society.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'At a time when other, more libertarian conservatives had begun to embrace critiques of the criminal-justice system, each man saw crime as yet another way that the fabric of society was deteriorating. While Bannon was chief executive, Breitbart created a specific tag for articles called \xe2\x80\x9cblack crime\xe2\x80\x9d and ran article after article demonizing the Black Lives Matter movement (calling protesters \xe2\x80\x9cblood-lusting junkies\xe2\x80\x9d) and showing Latino immigrants as violent (\xe2\x80\x9cOne Sex Offender Illegal Alien Caught After Another Alleged Offender Legalized\xe2\x80\x9d). The site also frequently covered Sessions\xe2\x80\x99s condemnations of criminal-justice reform. Opposing a bipartisan bill to reduce sentences for some nonviolent drug offenses, Sessions said last May that Republican supporters of the legislation \xe2\x80\x9cin no way represent the conservative movement\xe2\x80\x9d and warned against \xe2\x80\x9csigning death warrants for thousands of American innocent citizens.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'As the Republican primary season progressed, it became clear to Sessions and Bannon that Trump could be the vessel for their brand of Republicanism. Back in August 2015, Bannon emailed a friend, 'b'according to The Daily Beast'b', that while he felt good about other candidates like Ted Cruz, he was ready to pick Trump, because he was \xe2\x80\x9ca nationalist who embraces\xe2\x80\x9d Sessions\xe2\x80\x99s immigration plan. Six months later, Sessions became the first senator to endorse Trump for president. Last August, Sessions 'b'helped create'b' a new immigration policy for Trump, which called for reducing immigration by, among other things, tightening the rules about visas for high-skilled workers. That same month, Bannon took over Trump\xe2\x80\x99s campaign.'b'Their shared view was central to Trump\xe2\x80\x99s Inaugural Address, which, 'b'according to The Wall Street Journal'b', Bannon and Miller principally wrote. For a president taking office amid peacetime and economic growth, the speech offered a singularly dark vision. Trump spoke of \xe2\x80\x9cAmerican carnage\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 a country made increasingly dangerous by \xe2\x80\x9cthe crime and the gangs and the drugs,\xe2\x80\x9d its economy ravaged by production abroad, its borders infiltrated by marauders. The speech was a perfect distillation of the foreboding view of America broadcast by Breitbart \xe2\x80\x94 a land in disarray and decline that has reached the point of crisis.'b'In fact, violent crime has been 'b'declining sharply'b' for 25 years; with a small uptick in 2015, it remains low. The number of undocumented immigrants has 'b'fallen slightly'b' in the last decade, and these newcomers are 'b'less likely'b' to commit violent crimes than people who were born here. Evidence shows that immigrants are an engine of economic growth and entrepreneurship. While they take a small bite out of the wages of native-born workers without a high school diploma, they provide an overall boost to productivity that increases the pay of more educated workers by up to 10 percent, 'b'labor economists say'b'. As for the \xe2\x80\x9ctotalitarian threat\xe2\x80\x9d that, according to Sessions, Americans face from radical Islam, since Sept. 11, white supremacists and other non-Muslim extremists have killed nearly twice as many Americans as radical Muslims, 'b'according to the New America Foundation'b'.'b'Why would the Trump administration paint a picture so starkly at odds with reality? It\xe2\x80\x99s simple: A vision of the nation besieged provides clear justification for policies that will advance Sessions, Bannon and Miller\xe2\x80\x99s divisive nationalism. In the administration\xe2\x80\x99s early moves, we can already see the contours beginning to take shape. An executive order presented as an emergency measure to protect the country from terrorists winds up barring immigrants coming here to study or work from seven countries that have not been a source of terrorist attacks in the United States since Sept. 11. Another order refers to immigrants who \xe2\x80\x9cpose a risk to public safety\xe2\x80\x9d and then makes millions of the undocumented people in the country a priority for deportation. Impending catastrophe grants the president broad powers, and those powers are used broadly.'b'It is through the Justice Department that the administration is likely to advance its nationalist plans \xe2\x80\x94 to strengthen the grip of law enforcement, raise barriers to voting and significantly reduce all forms of immigration, promoting what seems to be a longstanding desire to reassert the country\xe2\x80\x99s European and Christian heritage. It\xe2\x80\x99s not an accident that Sessions, who presumably could have chosen from a number of plum assignments, opted for the role of attorney general. The Department of Justice is the most valuable perch from which to transform the country in the way he and Bannon have wanted. With an exaggerated threat of disorder looming, the nation\xe2\x80\x99s top law-enforcement agency could become a machine for trying to fundamentally change who gets to be an American and what rights they can enjoy.'b''b'The Department of'b' Justice employs a staff of 115,000, including more than 10,000 lawyers. Besides encompassing the F.B.I. and the Drug Enforcement Administration, the department administers the federal prison system; prosecutes financial fraud, federal environmental crimes and national-security violations; and oversees the country\xe2\x80\x99s 93 United States attorneys (who prosecute federal crimes throughout the country). The department also includes two critical sites for setting legal policy. The Office of the Solicitor General decides the positions the federal government takes in the appeals courts and before the Supreme Court, and the Office of Legal Counsel advises the president about the legality of his actions and executive orders, passing judgment like an internal court.'b'The traditional explanation for the founding of the Department of Justice in 1870 has heroic overtones: Congress and President Ulysses S. Grant were purportedly determined to enforce Reconstruction and protect the civil rights of former slaves. But in recent years, a more pragmatic narrative has taken hold. As the Fordham law professor Jed Handelsman Shugerman explains it, the story is really about cutting costs, weeding out cronyism and reining in radical Republican lawyers who wanted to aggressively enforce the policies of Reconstruction. In a 2014 article 'b'in The Stanford Law Review'b', Shugerman points out that the creation of the Justice Department reduced the federal legal staff by one-third. While a handful of determined lawyers successfully prosecuted Ku Klux Klan members in the early years, for the most part, Shugerman told me, \xe2\x80\x9cthe D.O.J. handcuffed the federal government\xe2\x80\x99s lawyers from doing more.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'At the time, lawyers worked all over Washington, in the State Department, the Interior Department and elsewhere. But it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t until the opening in 1934 of the Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s current headquarters, an imposing building the size of a large square block, that the department became a strong institutional presence. Justice Department lawyers flexed their muscles defending the New Deal, and the agency expanded in scope.'b'For all the vast sweep of the Department of Justice as a whole, much of its influence over the major political conflicts of our time, and certainly those poised to dominate the next four years, is concentrated within the civil rights division, charged as it is with ensuring equal protection under the law. With a staff of roughly 700, the division is basically the watchdog of the government and also a kind of police of the nation\xe2\x80\x99s police, because it has the power to investigate departments for violations of civil rights law. It can also sue localities for discrimination in housing, education and employment, on the basis of sex, national origin, religion or disability as well as race. The division also protects the rights of people in prison and mental institutions.'b'When the division was founded in 1957, it was staffed by a handful of lawyers who largely refrained from directly challenging Jim Crow in the South. But when Robert F. Kennedy became attorney general in 1961, things changed. He \xe2\x80\x9chad his own ambitions,\xe2\x80\x9d Shugerman says. \xe2\x80\x9cEven as J.F.K. tried to steer the ship down the middle, R.F.K. pulled it toward civil rights.\xe2\x80\x9d In the 1970s and early \xe2\x80\x9980s, the civil rights division\xe2\x80\x99s ranks grew, attracting top law-school graduates to nonpartisan civil-service jobs. Many of them hoped to serve in the tradition of lawyers like John Doar, a Republican from Wisconsin who lived in a dorm for weeks with James Meredith, the first African-American student to attend the University of Mississippi, during his struggle to register for classes in 1962. \xe2\x80\x9cMost of us were liberals, and we were passionate about fighting the country\xe2\x80\x99s long history of racial injustice,\xe2\x80\x9d says William Yeomans, who worked in the division for 24 years, beginning in 1981.'b'Political battles over the priorities of the division have been escalating for decades. During Democratic presidencies, the division has moved to challenge intentional discrimination by individuals (like a landlord who turns away an African-American couple and then rents to a white one) and has sought to combat policies that disproportionally affect minorities, even if it\xe2\x80\x99s not easy to prove they\xe2\x80\x99re racially motivated (like a low-income housing program that funnels African-American recipients into mostly minority areas). Republican administrations since the Reagan era, by contrast, have tended to bring individual rather than broad suits while also treating civil rights as race-neutral or colorblind and opposing affirmative action. They have also been equally likely to sue on behalf of white and black people who say they face unequal treatment. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Republican view of civil rights focuses on the protection of the individual,\xe2\x80\x9d says Robert Driscoll, chief of staff of the civil rights division during George W. Bush\xe2\x80\x99s first term. The Republican approach has caused tension with the career staff. \xe2\x80\x9cWhenever the G.O.P. is in office, there\xe2\x80\x99s a certain amount of heat on whoever is in charge,\xe2\x80\x9d Driscoll says. \xe2\x80\x9cThe career people hated us when I was there.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'During the Obama administration in particular, the civil rights division began to take on a whole gamut of institutions. Under the leadership of Tom Perez, it went after banks, arguing that Wells Fargo and Bank of America systematically steered African-American and Latino homeowners toward subprime loans, and winning the 'b'largest settlements'b' in the history of the Fair Housing Act, totaling about $570 million. In 2014, Vanita Gupta arrived from the A.C.L.U. to lead the division, and under her direction, the department addressed prisons and schools, working to 'b'reduce solitary confinement'b', 'b'discouraging the arrest of students'b' for disciplinary infractions and 'b'instructing schools'b' across the country to let transgender students use the bathrooms of their choice. (Sessions led the charge in the Trump administration to 'b'rescind the order'b' about bathroom access.) Another significant power Perez and Gupta exercised was oversight of local police forces, investigating 25 departments for problems like excessive use of force, unlawful arrests and racially discriminatory policing. Twenty of those investigations ended in consent decrees, a form of agreement that entails monitoring by the courts.'b'In this way, the Justice Department under Obama used civil rights enforcement \xe2\x80\x94 or the potential for it \xe2\x80\x94 to check private, local and state entities that otherwise would have escaped federal intervention. But Sessions can pull back on such oversight, even to an extent (if he chooses) beyond that of his Republican predecessors. Invoking Trump\xe2\x80\x99s nightmare vision of America as beset by lawlessness, the department can unbind the hands of law-enforcement agencies around the country. On issues like immigration and voting rights, with public perceptions influenced by inflated tales of criminality (improper use of benefits, voter fraud), a Sessions-led Department of Justice can both feed the narrative of crime and react to it, through heightened investigations and prosecutions. A civil rights system built up to protect the minority from the majority could quickly turn into the reverse.'b''b'Crime \xe2\x80\x94 especially urban crime \xe2\x80\x94'b' lies at the heart of the new nationalist message, in part as an argument for why liberal-run cities, with their dense, diverse, polyglot communities, shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t serve as a model for the nation as a whole. Since the outset of his campaign, Trump has exaggerated the violence and poverty of \xe2\x80\x9cinner cities,\xe2\x80\x9d painting them as war zones and blaming Democrats for the destruction. The politics behind this sort of language are not new: For 50 years, Republican candidates for president have won by stoking fear of crime, promising to \xe2\x80\x9crestore order and respect for law in this country,\xe2\x80\x9d as Richard Nixon put it in 1968. But Trump has been fixated on the issue, ignoring actual crime statistics to 'b'inaccurately blame'b' African-Americans for most white homicides and falsely claim that the murder rate is at its highest point in 47 years.'b'While many conservative figures kept their distance from Trump during the campaign, one group embraced him wholeheartedly: law-enforcement unions. The rank and file did, too. A survey before the election by Police Magazine found that 84 percent of officers who planned to vote backed him, while just 8 percent supported Hillary Clinton. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s views on the Black Lives Matter movement, which many cops have seen as unfair and a danger to policing minority neighborhoods, no doubt played a part. Last summer, Trump called the movement a \xe2\x80\x9cthreat,\xe2\x80\x9d blaming it for the deaths of officers and adding, \xe2\x80\x9cWe are going to have to perhaps talk with the attorney general about it.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Today the test is how Trump\xe2\x80\x99s new attorney general will view the Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s responsibility, conferred by Congress in 1994 after the beating of Rodney King, to investigate local police departments for a \xe2\x80\x9cpattern or practice\xe2\x80\x9d of civil rights violations. With more than 12,000 police departments across the country, the division couldn\xe2\x80\x99t exercise anything approaching true national oversight even if it wanted to. (Perez and Gupta\xe2\x80\x99s total of 25 investigations was equal to that of the Clinton Justice Department over eight years; the Bush administration initiated 21.) But the Obama Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s willingness to enter cities like Chicago, Baltimore and Ferguson, Mo., after high-profile encounters between the police and African-Americans meant that officers everywhere had to reckon with the possibility of federal investigation \xe2\x80\x94 at a time when many of them already felt under siege.'b'Consider Chicago, which throughout the campaign was Trump\xe2\x80\x99s exemplar of a city rife with \xe2\x80\x9ccarnage.\xe2\x80\x9d Chicago has been 'b'reeling'b' from a major spike in gun violence and killings, but it is also a city with a sordid history of police abuses. Under Gupta, the civil rights division opened an investigation into the city\xe2\x80\x99s police department in 2015, after the release of a video of the killing of Laquan McDonald, a 17-year-old who was shot 16 times by an officer while the teenager was walking away. After hundreds of hours of interviews with police officers, residents and city officials, the civil rights division issued an unsparing 'b'set of findings'b' in January, faulting the police department for routinely using excessive force (especially against African-Americans and Latinos), a deficient system for investigating police misconduct and a poor structure of supervision, promotion and training. The Chicago Fraternal Order of Police 'b'welcomed'b' the parts of the report about training and promotion. But the union objected to the Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s implication that race affects police decision-making, arguing that officers are simply targeting criminal behavior in high-crime areas, not African-Americans and Latinos.'b'Now the police have an attorney general who has been unwilling to give credence to their critics. Confronting Gupta in November 2015 at a Senate hearing called the War on Police, Sessions suggested that the civil rights division was going \xe2\x80\x9cbeyond fair and balanced treatment\xe2\x80\x9d of law enforcement. Sessions has also 'b'called'b' court-monitored consent decrees, which the Justice Department has used to settle school desegregation cases in addition to police investigations, \xe2\x80\x9cone of the most dangerous, and rarely discussed, exercises of raw power.\xe2\x80\x9d The problem, in other words, isn\xe2\x80\x99t what the police are doing. It\xe2\x80\x99s that the federal government is interfering.'b'In appearing to categorically oppose consent decrees and signaling a lack of interest in broad new investigations, Sessions appears to be going further than even police groups do. \xe2\x80\x9cNo officer worth his badge would say \xe2\x80\x98no investigations\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89if an investigation is warranted,\xe2\x80\x9d James Pasco, executive director of the Fraternal Order of Police, told me. But that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean the police wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like the Justice Department to back off in Chicago, where no consent decree was negotiated before Obama\xe2\x80\x99s and Gupta\xe2\x80\x99s departures, and it\xe2\x80\x99s now up to Sessions to decide what an agreement of any kind should look like, or whether it should exist at all.'b'The question for the country is what happens if local police departments, relieved of the realistic prospect of federal oversight, feel their hands are untied in tense situations. On an evening in January when Fox News ran a segment on the recent spike in murders in Chicago, Trump tweeted a few minutes later that he could \xe2\x80\x9csend in the feds!\xe2\x80\x9d It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to know what Trump meant, but he was not talking about protecting civil rights. The next time a disturbing video of a police shooting surfaces, it\xe2\x80\x99s easy to imagine Trump and Sessions supporting the police without question and directing a crackdown on protesters, especially if they\xe2\x80\x99re mostly black. That would connect the Trumpist edition of law and order to the apparent project of undermining the rights of minorities and their claim on the national conscience.'b''b'Another way'b''b' in which'b' the Department of Justice could move to advance the Sessions and Bannon agenda is through its approach to voting laws. The civil rights division\xe2\x80\x99s powers in this area flow mostly from the Voting Rights Act, which Congress passed in 1965 to help African-Americans vote. Section 5 of the act gave the Justice Department unusual authority to review any proposed change to an election practice in a state or county with a history of low minority registration, mostly in the South. The division\xe2\x80\x99s voting section has largely seen its role ever since as safeguarding the rights of minority voters. During both Democratic and Republican administrations, Justice Department lawyers used Section 5 to block localities from redrawing district lines or closing polling places \xe2\x80\x94 and to block strict voter-ID laws from taking effect.'b'But in 2013, in a 5-to-4 Supreme Court decision in 'b'Shelby County v. Holder'b', Chief Justice John Roberts Jr. argued for the majority that Section 5 was no longer necessary because \xe2\x80\x9cthings have changed dramatically\xe2\x80\x9d in the South. After the ruling, however, Southern states rushed to make it more difficult for people to vote. Hundreds of polling places were closed throughout the region, and Texas and North Carolina passed strict voter-ID laws. North Carolina also ended same-day registration and out-of-precinct voting and reduced the early-voting period to one week from two.'b'The Obama Justice Department responded by joining civil rights groups in suing Texas and North Carolina, claiming the laws had the effect of discriminating against minority voters. Matching voter rolls to the records in state and federal databases, experts hired by the Justice Department determined that more than 600,000 voters in Texas lacked the required ID and that they were disproportionately black and Latino. An appeals court ruled against Texas last July. Nine days later, a second appeals court struck down North Carolina\xe2\x80\x99s law, saying the restrictions \xe2\x80\x9ctarget African-American voters with almost surgical precision.\xe2\x80\x9d Obama urged other states to heed the lesson in his final speech as president: \xe2\x80\x9cWe should be making it easier, not harder, to vote.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Trump and Sessions are poised to do precisely the opposite, creating a rationale for restricting voting instead of expanding it. On Monday, the Justice Department switched positions by 'b'dropping the key claim'b' that Texas passed its voter ID law with the intent to discriminate. Over the last few months, the president has claimed that millions of people voted illegally in November. In fact, just 'b'four cases'b' of in-person voter fraud have been identified from the 2016 presidential election, and a Loyola Law School 'b'study in 2014'b' discovered only 31 credible allegations of fraud in a sample of one billion votes. Pressed for evidence by Bill O\xe2\x80\x99Reilly on Fox, Trump promised that Vice President Mike Pence would lead a commission to investigate the nation\xe2\x80\x99s voting rolls. A week later, Stephen Miller 'b'falsely insisted'b' on TV that \xe2\x80\x9c14 percent of noncitizens\xe2\x80\x9d are registered to vote \xe2\x80\x94 portraying the country as vulnerable to shadowy foreign hordes who undermine the very core of American democracy.'b'Raising the alarm about illegal voting furthers the Republican Party\xe2\x80\x99s current strategy for preserving power. For more than a decade, Republican state legislators have used the myth of widespread fraud to justify tightening voting and registration rules. This year, 46 new bills to restrict access to voting and registration have been introduced in 21 states, 'b'according to the Brennan Center for Justice'b'. Making it harder to vote tends to help Republicans win office, in part because it tends to have a disproportionate impact 'b'on minority voters'b', who are 'b'less likely'b' to have a required form of ID and who generally support Democrats. Over the next four election cycles, the national share of eligible voters who are minorities is 'b'projected'b' to rise steadily, to about 40 percent from about 30 percent. Bluntly put, Trump\xe2\x80\x99s supporters, who were 90 percent white, can continue to put Republican candidates over the top only if an increasing number of minority voters stay away from \xe2\x80\x94 or are kept away from \xe2\x80\x94 the polls.'b'The Republican hunt for voter fraud has 'b'a history'b'. After George W. Bush narrowly beat Al Gore in 2000, his attorney general created an election-integrity task force, spurring United States attorneys to search for voter fraud. One of them, David Iglesias, a Republican in New Mexico, looked into more than 100 complaints but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find enough evidence for a single prosecution. He was fired in 2006 along with eight other United States attorneys. 'b'An investigation'b' by the Justice Department later found that most of the dismissals were a serious abuse of executive power, because they were a response to the attorneys\xe2\x80\x99 refusing to bring unsubstantiated voter-fraud charges or politically motivated charges against Democratic officials, or both as in Iglesias\xe2\x80\x99 case. Congressional hearings related to the firings took down Alberto Gonzales, Bush\xe2\x80\x99s attorney general at the time.'b'Bush appointees in the civil rights division were also caught trying to illegally push out career civil rights lawyers and replace them with conservatives. In an internal email, one Bush appointee, Brad Schlozman, wrote to another Republican: \xe2\x80\x9cMy tentative plans are to gerrymander all those crazy libs right out of the section.\xe2\x80\x9d By the end of Bush\xe2\x80\x99s term, most of the career attorneys had been pushed out or had left. \xe2\x80\x9cThey attacked everything we were supposed to stand for,\xe2\x80\x9d says Joe Rich, who spent 36 years in the division.'b'During Obama\xe2\x80\x99s presidency, new career lawyers were hired, but Sessions could try to transfer them or lay them off. The Heritage Foundation has called for reducing the civil rights division\xe2\x80\x99s budget by $58 million, or about one-third.'b'It\xe2\x80\x99s not clear yet who will lead Sessions\xe2\x80\x99s civil rights division. In the short term, Sessions has put the division in the hands of conservatives who have a record of defending voting restrictions. Tom Wheeler, the acting head, advised Texas Republicans when they wrote their voter-ID law. John Gore, the No. 2, has defended jurisdictions against voting rights challenges at the law firm Jones Day in Washington, working frequently with Michael Carvin, a founder of the conservative legal movement in the Reagan Justice Department. (Sessions also worked for that Justice Department as a United States attorney in west Alabama, and in 1985 'b'he prosecuted'b' three black civil rights activists for voter fraud. The case, which collapsed at trial, was one reason he was not confirmed for a federal judgeship the following year.)'b'In 2012, Gore and Carvin defended Florida when it was sued \xe2\x80\x94 by the Justice Department as well as civil rights groups \xe2\x80\x94 over an effort to purge the rolls of noncitizens. Florida was implementing the 1993 Motor Voter law, which, in addition to improving access to registration, instructed states to remove people, within limits, from the rolls if they had moved away or died. The state wrongly flagged thousands of American citizens, most of them Latino. Florida 'b'lost the suit'b' on appeal and stopped the purge. Now Gore will help decide the Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s position if another state is suspected of expunging voters unlawfully. And he or other Sessions appointees could shift the work of the career lawyers in the voting section to push states to go through their voter rolls. Though keeping the rolls up to date is a good idea in theory, when states flag voters who have a Latino name or are naturalized citizens, the burden is on the voters to prove the state has erred. Otherwise they can\xe2\x80\x99t vote.'b''b'To turn back 'b'the clock on immigration, Sessions, Bannon and Trump would have to prevent more immigrants from coming in and remove those who are here. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s Jan. 27 executive order banning refugees and travelers from seven majority-Muslim countries, reportedly overseen by Bannon and Miller, was the initial bid to shut the door. On Feb. 20, the Department of Homeland Security announced the new guidance that vastly broadened the definition of who is considered a priority for deportation.'b'It falls to immigration agents at the Department of Homeland Security, not the Department of Justice, to pick people up. But Sessions has a major role to play. To begin with, he oversees the nation\xe2\x80\x99s immigration courts. The Feb. 20 guidelines call for the Justice Department to send a surge of judges to the border to turn people around with a type of speedy removal, entailing only the barest form of legal process \xe2\x80\x94 a practice that Trump just expanded far beyond what any president has authorized (and which now applies in the interior of the country as well). Sessions also has the authority to streamline appeals, by expanding a cursory form of review used during the Bush years, and to overturn Obama-era decisions that have made it easier to receive asylum or get legal counsel.'b'Democratic mayors and governors have vowed not to cooperate with a federal crackdown, and Trump has responded with an order asking the Departments of Justice and Homeland Security to withhold federal funds from them. It will be up to Sessions to decide how far to go in trying to cancel grants that cities receive from his department (for drug treatment and crime prevention, for example).'b'Detention and deportation on a mass scale would be a gargantuan task \xe2\x80\x94 divisive, enormously costly and legally fraught. The only feasible way to get millions of undocumented immigrants out of the country, as Trump has promised, is to create a climate that induces immigrants to leave on their own. \xe2\x80\x9cYou basically would have [to] self-deport,\xe2\x80\x9d Sessions 'b'said on CNN'b' last fall. That means \xe2\x80\x9cleveraging fear,\xe2\x80\x9d as Kamal Essaheb, policy director for the National Immigration Law Center, puts it. The Feb. 20 guidelines direct immigration agents to \xe2\x80\x9crefer appropriate cases for criminal prosecution.\xe2\x80\x9d Those cases would go to federal prosecutors and could lead to yearslong prison terms. An estimated eight million people go to work in this country every day without papers. Sending a rash of them to prison could do more than the threat of deportation to send laborers across the country packing.'b'Sessions has supported an experiment in self-deportation before. In June 2011, when he was a senator, Alabama enacted the 'b'most restrictive'b' immigration law in the country. Called H.B. 56, it gave law enforcement the authority to ask for a driver\xe2\x80\x99s papers, mandated the arrest of people who lacked proper documents and required people to show proof of citizenship when they interacted with a government agency. Residents were required to provide identification to renew a mobile-home license or register a child for school.'b'Immigrants 'b'left Alabama'b' for fear of being arrested; others were afraid to send their children to school. Laura Ingraham, the conservative radio host, invited Sessions on her show to discuss H.B. 56 that fall. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think it\xe2\x80\x99s bad\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9call these Hispanic kids have disappeared from schools?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cAll I would just say to you is it\xe2\x80\x99s a sad thing that we\xe2\x80\x99ve allowed a situation to occur for decades in which large numbers of people are in the country illegally,\xe2\x80\x9d 'b'Sessions answered'b'.'b'Many business leaders opposed H.B. 56 for taking away their workers and customers. The law was projected to cost the state between $2 billion and $11 billion in lost G.D.P. in its first year, according to 'b'an estimate'b' by a University of Alabama economist. One farmer challenged a state legislator to pick his tomatoes for him. But in 'b'an interview'b' in The Daily Caller, Sessions claimed that the opposition to H.B. 56 was \xe2\x80\x9can effort by leftist, activist immigration advocates.\xe2\x80\x9d He continued: \xe2\x80\x9cIncredibly, the Department of Justice has joined in on that aggressively.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'At the time, the Obama Justice Department was suing Alabama in federal court over the immigration restrictions alongside a coalition of churches and civil rights groups. Concerned about the impact on families, Obama\xe2\x80\x99s lawyers argued that Alabama was going further than federal law allowed. \xe2\x80\x9cOur visions of immigrant rights and civil rights were inextricably intertwined,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom Perez, then head of the civil rights division, told me. In court, the plaintiffs invoked a 1982 Supreme Court decision, 'b'Plyler v. Doe'b', which found that undocumented children have a constitutional right to an education.'b'In November 2013, the Justice Department succeeded in permanently blocking most of H.B. 56. The civil rights divisions of the Department of Justice and the Department of Education followed with a letter in 2014 to every school district in the country, warning against any policy that could dissuade students from enrolling because of their families\xe2\x80\x99 immigration status. The Justice Department also defended Obama\xe2\x80\x99s actions to allow young people brought to the United States as children, called Dreamers, and their close relatives to work or go to school without fear of deportation. Sessions could review that directive and call for withdrawing it, perhaps as a misuse of prosecutorial discretion. In other words, the Justice Department, especially in the last few years, has operated in several ways to make undocumented families more secure. Sessions can repurpose the machinery to do something else entirely.'b''b'Before Sessions was'b' confirmed as attorney general, Sally Yates, a holdover from the Obama administration, was serving in that role. When Trump issued his refugee and travel ban on Jan. 27, she refused to defend the order in court, saying she was not convinced that his order was lawful or consistent with the Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s obligation to \xe2\x80\x9cstand for what is right.\xe2\x80\x9d Trump quickly fired Yates amid a predictably partisan debate over whether she was acting on an important principle or failing to do her job.'b'In almost all circumstances, the top law-enforcement official in the executive branch is expected to side with the chief of that branch \xe2\x80\x94 the president. Yet we want the attorney general to exercise his or her own judgment when we fear presidential wrongdoing. Nixon\xe2\x80\x99s attorney general, Elliot Richardson, and then the acting attorney general who was next in line, William Ruckelshaus, are celebrated for resigning when Nixon ordered them to fire the special prosecutor who was investigating his role in the Watergate break-in. Sessions is already facing questions about his independence for not recusing himself from any F.B.I. investigation of the Trump campaign\xe2\x80\x99s ties to Russia. Because Sessions was an architect of the particular nationalist vision that drives Trump\xe2\x80\x99s approach to policing, voting and immigration, it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to imagine what the president (or Bannon) would want to do in those arenas that Sessions would not be ready to defend. Unlike Yates, he is in accord with their aims. He can simply perform the attorney general\xe2\x80\x99s ordinary duties, by doing his utmost to ensure Trump\xe2\x80\x99s orders survive legal scrutiny.'b'What can stop Trump, Bannon and Sessions from using the Justice Department to achieve their goals? So far, judges across the country have prevented Trump from carrying out his refugee and travel ban, and they\xe2\x80\x99ve accepted lawsuits against him for review.'b'Over time, however, more federal judges will be Trump appointees. It\xe2\x80\x99s not just Neil Gorsuch\xe2\x80\x99s nomination to the Supreme Court that\xe2\x80\x99s important for determining the legal fate of Trumpism. Rulings by lower court judges set expectations and establish rationales. Because of the high number of vacancies and older judges, Trump may be able to appoint a greater share of federal judges than any first-term president in 40 years.'b'Judges are supposed to be entirely independent of the president even if he appoints them. The Constitution depends on it. And there are other constraints. The Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s career staff exerts a strong pull toward the center. The public\xe2\x80\x99s restive mood and high level of engagement so far matter, too. It\xe2\x80\x99s no sure thing that Sessions, Bannon and Trump will succeed in carrying out their shared vision. But they\xe2\x80\x99re likely to work together, as long as they\xe2\x80\x99re each in office, to test how far the country will let them go.'b''b''b''b''b''b'L'b'ast summer, early in the morning, I stood out in the main square of Florence to watch the tourists come in. It was quiet. A Zamboni-like street cleaner drove its rounds, leaving wet circles on the paving stones. A vendor unpacked tarp-wrapped souvenirs from the back of his white van. When the crowds began to arrive \xe2\x80\x94 tour groups from Japan, China, Germany, Spain \xe2\x80\x94 they seemed less like people than like weather. They surged into the square, pooling and drifting. They clicked selfies in front of the statues. A small herd of Segways rolled past, one rider singing fake opera at the top of his lungs. I watched a tour group from Arizona (clearly identifiable by their neck badges) approach the white figure of Michelangelo\xe2\x80\x99s David, towering on a pedestal in front of City Hall. One of the tourists pointed to it and said, in a tone of amused contempt: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the most famous statue in the world, and they just leave it outside. No big deal \xe2\x80\x94 just hose off the pigeon crap.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The implication was clear: Italy was a backward country, incapable of protecting its cultural treasures. To be fair, the tourist was not the first person to make this accusation. In his history \xe2\x80\x9cThe Italians,\xe2\x80\x9d Luigi Barzini writes that one of the basic pleasures Italy reliably provides for visitors is \xe2\x80\x9cthat of feeling morally superior to the natives.\xe2\x80\x9d I sometimes felt this pleasure myself. The inefficiency of the Italian bureaucracy, whether selling you a postage stamp or fixing a street, was often marvelous to behold. And indeed, the statue the man was pointing at had obviously suffered from standing outside: The marble was striped with dirt.'b'But the tourist was, in one very important respect, wrong.'b'He was pointing not at the actual David but at a full-scale marble replica. Michelangelo\xe2\x80\x99s real statue did once stand in this spot, but it was moved, for its own protection, 143 years ago. The original is now in a museum across town, shielded from the elements, perfectly safe.'b'Or at least that\xe2\x80\x99s how we like to think of it. We are conditioned to believe that art is safe, beyond the reach of the grimy world. We don\xe2\x80\x99t hang the Mona Lisa next to an archery range. We put her in a fortress: walls, checkpoints, lasers, guards, bulletproof glass. There are scholars, textbooks, posters \xe2\x80\x94 a whole collective mythology suggesting that the work will live forever. But safety is largely an illusion, and permanence a fiction. Empires hemorrhage wealth, bombs fall on cities, religious radicals decimate ancient temples. Destruction happens in any number of ways, for any number of reasons, at any number of speeds \xe2\x80\x94 and it will happen, and no amount of reverence will stop it.'b''b'Few humans on'b' earth know this melancholy truth better than the citizens of Florence. They are born into a profound intimacy with decay. The city was the epicenter of the Renaissance \xe2\x80\x94 home to such art-history superheroes as Giotto, Brunelleschi, Donatello, Cellini and Leonardo da Vinci \xe2\x80\x94 and the relics of that period have been under siege, more or less constantly, ever since. In 1497, the fanatical monk Savonarola sent his followers door to door to gather the city\xe2\x80\x99s nonreligious art, books, clothing, musical instruments, then piled it all 50 feet high in the central square and set it on fire: the infamous Bonfire of the Vanities. (The spectacle was such a success that he repeated it the following year.) In 1895, earthquakes shook Florence so hard that citizens, fearing aftershocks, spent the night sleeping out in the streets. The 20th century brought Nazis and Mafia car bombs. This November will mark the 50th anniversary of the great Florentine flood of 1966, an inundation that overtook much of the city center, killing dozens of people and destroying old masterpieces.'b'Today, the perpetual engine of Florentine destruction seems only to be getting bolder. Its latest target is its most ambitious yet: the mascot of the Renaissance, shining ideal of the human form, one of the most celebrated artworks in this or any other city \xe2\x80\x94 Michelangelo\xe2\x80\x99s David.'b'The trouble is the David\xe2\x80\x99s ankles. They are cracked. Italians first discovered this weakness back in the 19th century, and modern scientists have mapped the cracks extensively, but until recently no one claimed to know just how enfeebled the ankles might be. This changed in 2014, when a team of Italian geoscientists published a paper called \xe2\x80\x9cModeling the Failure Mechanisms of Michelangelo\xe2\x80\x99s David Through Small-Scale Centrifuge Experiments.\xe2\x80\x9d That dry title concealed a terrifying story. The paper describes an experiment designed to measure, in a novel way, the weakness in the David\xe2\x80\x99s ankles: by creating a small army of tiny David replicas and spinning them in a centrifuge, at various angles, to simulate different levels of real-world stress. What the researchers found was grim. If the David were to be tilted 15 degrees, his ankles would fail.'b'The seed of the problem is a tiny imperfection in the statue\xe2\x80\x99s design. The center of gravity in the base doesn\xe2\x80\x99t align with the center of gravity in the figure itself; when the base is level, in other words, the David\xe2\x80\x99s body is slightly off-balance. There is, as the article nicely puts it, \xe2\x80\x9can eccentricity of the loads.\xe2\x80\x9d This places extra pressure on the David\xe2\x80\x99s narrowest part: his ankles. As long as the statue is perfectly upright, the eccentricity of the loads is tolerable. But there is very little margin for error. If you tilt the base even slightly, the stress on the ankles sharply increases.'b'Now it just so happens that, for a very long time, before he was moved into his protective museum, the David was leaning slightly. No one is sure exactly why. He stood, for more than 300 years, in the spot where I saw the tourist from Arizona scoff at the dirty replica.
