It didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter that the place had no connection to the British university or that this Oxford was a small town north of Detroit. \xe2\x80\x9cMy father,\xe2\x80\x9d Korbin said, \xe2\x80\x9creally likes the idea of an Oxford diploma.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Even as U.S.-China relations have slipped toward mutual antagonism, the flood of Chinese students coming to the United States has continued to rise. Roughly 370,000 students from the mainland are enrolled in American high schools and universities, six times more than a decade ago. Their financial impact \xe2\x80\x94 $11.4 billion was contributed to the American economy in 2015, according to the Department of Commerce \xe2\x80\x94 has turned education into one of America\xe2\x80\x99s top \xe2\x80\x9cexports\xe2\x80\x9d to China.'b'It is a strange historical moment when the elites of a rising power send their only sons and daughters, products of China\xe2\x80\x99s former one-child policy, to the schools of a geopolitical rival. Yet the idea of a liberal Western education exerts an almost talismanic hold over China\xe2\x80\x99s ruling classes. While the country\xe2\x80\x99s educational emphasis on rote memorization churns out some of the world\xe2\x80\x99s best test-takers, many Chinese families harbor worries that diverge sharply from those of the tiger parents of popular conception. They fret about the toll competition exacts from their coddled offspring; they wonder if their child\xe2\x80\x99s creativity is being stifled. Even President Xi Jinping, who is presiding over a crackdown on Western influences in China\xe2\x80\x99s schools, allowed his daughter to attend Harvard. According to a 2016 survey conducted by a Shanghai-based research firm, 83 percent of China\xe2\x80\x99s millionaires are planning to send their children to school abroad. The average age, according to the poll, has dropped to 16 today from 18 in 2014 \xe2\x80\x94 the first time it has reached the high-school level.'b'In 2005, only 641 Chinese students were enrolled in American high schools. By 2014, that student population approached 40,000 \xe2\x80\x94 a 60-fold increase in a single decade \xe2\x80\x94 and it now accounts for nearly half of all international high-school students in the United States. \xe2\x80\x9cParents realize that they have to start earlier if they want their children to get into a top U.S. university,\xe2\x80\x9d says Nini Suet, founder of Shang Learning, a boutique consultancy with headquarters in Beijing that charges $25,000 to $40,000 to help Chinese kids prepare for and apply to American boarding schools. \xe2\x80\x9cFamilies are looking for any edge they can get.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'As a new administration vowing \xe2\x80\x9cAmerica First\xe2\x80\x9d settles into the White House, there is uncertainty about how long this phenomenon can last. It already faces headwinds within China. A slowing economy has cut into family savings, and a depreciating currency makes American educations more expensive. There are also fewer Chinese students to go around: The population of 18-to-23-year-olds has dropped by nearly a quarter in the last decade.'b'But the exodus of Chinese students continues for now, driven not just by a push from China but also by a pull from the United States. For each rich Chinese kid who enters an American school \xe2\x80\x94 whether public or private, college or high school \xe2\x80\x94 the multiplier effect means that entire communities can be buoyed by the buying power of the world\xe2\x80\x99s second-largest economy.'b'That\xe2\x80\x99s the hope, at least. The reality is more complicated. Much of the money spent by Chinese families desperate for American diplomas ends up with the intermediary companies that connect them with cash-hungry schools. These matches have yielded unusual results. According to an article in Foreign Policy magazine, nearly 60 percent of the high-school students end up in religious schools, despite coming from the world\xe2\x80\x99s largest atheist state. (Their parents welcome the messages of safety, discipline and moral values that emanate from these faith-based institutions.) Another cohort winds up in what are effectively diploma mills, which confer visas and credentials but little adult supervision.'b'Public high schools are the newest frontier: Less than 5 percent of all Chinese \xe2\x80\x9cparachute kids\xe2\x80\x9d (as they\xe2\x80\x99ve come to be called) are currently in public schools, but certain U.S. school districts have begun to rely on them to offset budget cuts and increase cultural diversity at the same time as private academies are reaching saturation points.'b'Few public school districts have deeper ties to China than Oxford, Mich., outside Detroit. In 2010, the town sought to create the first pipeline of Chinese students into a public high school, one that embodied its high school\xe2\x80\x99s motto \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cwhere the globe is our classroom\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 even as it brought tuition money to the school. As the number of foreign students grew, other Midwestern schools sought to emulate Oxford\xe2\x80\x99s success. But when a Beijing education company proposed building a multimillion-dollar dormitory for Chinese students on the Oxford campus, a community battle ensued.'b'Chance put Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s family at the starting point of Oxford\xe2\x80\x99s experiment, in Shenyang. His parents grew up without proper educations in rural villages haunted by the memories of famine. His father, Yang Huaiguo, migrated to Shenyang and scavenged for scrap metal before finding success in the boiler-repair business and real estate. But he worried about Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s education and the almost unrelenting pressure to study for the two exams that determine a Chinese student\xe2\x80\x99s future: the high-school entrance exam, the 'b'zhongkao'b', and the university-entrance exam, the 'b'gaokao'b'. There seemed to be no way out, until Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s school opened an international wing in partnership with Oxford. The pitch was enticing: After spending 10th grade in the program, Korbin was guaranteed a place at Oxford High School for two years, until graduation.'b'His father insisted that this wasn\xe2\x80\x99t just about family prestige or future job prospects. \xe2\x80\x9cI also want my son to understand, in a way that I never could,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the world is bigger than Shenyang, bigger than China.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'On his first 'b'days in Oxford, Korbin marveled at the blue skies, so different from northeastern China, and the absence of skyscrapers. All of America, in his TV-fueled imagination, was supposed to look like New York. Beyond its main street and century-old storefronts, Oxford (population 3,500) is a patchwork of gravel pits and horse stables, wooded subdivisions and a strip mall containing a single Chinese restaurant. Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s host family lived in a house on a leafy cul-de-sac, with two basketball hoops in the driveway and a trampoline out back. Suddenly Korbin had four blond American siblings and a host mother he called \xe2\x80\x9cMom.\xe2\x80\x9d His host father worked as an engineer in an automobile industry that blamed the loss of thousands of jobs in the last recession on a single culprit: China.'b'Korbin immersed himself in Americana: football games, big-box stores, even a Christian megachurch with its own rock band. He was hardly alone in his American adventure. Michigan has become a particularly popular destination for Chinese public-high-school students in the United States, and more than a dozen kids from Shenyang lived nearby. Even at Oxford, where Korbin and 23 other students ended up because their academic program in China and its agent, the BCC International Education Group, had partnered with the school, there were another 19 Chinese kids brought in by a Beijing-based company, Weiming Education Group. The two crowds didn\xe2\x80\x99t mix much, in part because most of the Weiming students lived in a dormitory at Rochester College, a liberal-arts Christian school half an hour away. Korbin felt lucky with his American home stay.'b'His life in America was very different from that of his predecessors. When the first Chinese students arrived in the United States in the wake of the Cultural Revolution \xe2\x80\x94 and later, after the 1989 Tiananmen massacre \xe2\x80\x94 many were so poor that they collected aluminum cans or worked janitorial jobs to survive. Today\xe2\x80\x99s Chinese students tend to be far wealthier than their American counterparts, particularly in public high schools. Even among the middle-class students at Oxford, the Chinese kids stirred up envy, and some resentment, by flaunting multiple versions of just-released iPhones. (Korbin had only one.) The Chinese boys carried hundreds of dollars in cash and often wore a different pair of designer sneakers \xe2\x80\x94 Nike, Puma, Adidas \xe2\x80\x94 each day. Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s Chinese housemate, Oscar Kou, who liked to talk about his father\xe2\x80\x99s fleet of luxury cars, spent several thousand dollars on a laptop so powerful that it blew out the fuses in their host family\xe2\x80\x99s house.'b'Making American friends wasn\xe2\x80\x99t as simple as Korbin had imagined. In the hallways at Oxford High School, whose student body numbers 1,845, the Chinese kids clustered together, chattering in Mandarin. Korbin longed to interact with his American classmates, but every time he tried, the conversations fizzled when he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand their cultural references or slang. Still, Korbin made no secret of his mission. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a Chinese boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he told his classes, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I really, really want to make American friends. It\xe2\x80\x99s the most important thing to me.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Perhaps his best chance came at the homecoming dance that fall. As he walked across the floor under the spinning lights of a disco ball, Korbin worked up his nerve and asked an American girl to dance. She just laughed. Another girl, another rejection. Finally, the third entreaty succeeded \xe2\x80\x94 just as a slow song started up and the dancers turned into a tangle of embracing couples. Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s feet stopped, and his arms locked at his sides. \xe2\x80\x9cI totally froze,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. The girl drifted off to rejoin her friends, leaving Korbin alone, wondering if a Chinese boy could ever find his footing in America.'b''b'If your initial'b' encounter with William Skilling took place in the Beijing Capital International Airport, as mine did, it would be easy to take him for a missionary. Dressed in crisply ironed khakis and a white button-down shirt, his short hair meticulously combed, he was headed to a dusty city in the Chinese interior. As superintendent of Oxford Community Schools, Skilling did see himself as a sort of evangelist for global education. On this trip to China, his 19th, he was reviewing plans for Weiming to build its multimillion-dollar dorm on land bordering Oxford High School. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will be the first of its kind at any American public school,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'For Skilling, a former high-school government and economics teacher, the dorm project was the culmination of years of networking with Chinese officials, educators and businessmen. His first foray into the China market came in 2008, when with the Chinese government\xe2\x80\x99s help he started what would become one of the largest Mandarin-language programs in American schools. Today more than 2,300 students in Oxford schools, K through 12, take daily classes in Chinese. \xe2\x80\x9cThe greatest challenge we face in American education,\xe2\x80\x9d Skilling said, \xe2\x80\x9cis preparing students to work and live in a global world that is changing 24-7.\xe2\x80\x9d The Mandarin classes were also part of a local government plan to lure Chinese investment to southeastern Michigan. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a reason that we, as a state, weren\xe2\x80\x99t making inroads into China,\xe2\x80\x9d Skilling told me. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Chinese knew Michigan was negative, blaming China for its woes. The way to turn this around was to pay China the highest compliment: by building a world-class Mandarin-language program.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Beijing returned the favor. In 2013, Hanban \xe2\x80\x94 the state agency that runs China\xe2\x80\x99s network of controversial Confucius Institutes, which promote Chinese culture, language and propaganda worldwide \xe2\x80\x94 recognized Oxford Community Schools as the \xe2\x80\x9cConfucius Classroom of the Year.\xe2\x80\x9d Skilling parlayed such recognition into sister-school agreements with 20 Chinese schools. Over the past seven years, at least 40 different Oxford teachers and administrators have visited China. One major deal was struck with Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s public school in Shenyang, which opened an international school for Chinese students, like Korbin, aiming to go to an American high school with a famous name.'b'With that deal, Skilling had lined up his first supply of Chinese students for Oxford. He also devised a way around the United States regulations that restricted international students to just one year in public high schools. By inviting a local college to sponsor the F-1 student visas for Chinese students in their second year, Skilling maintained that they could remain at the high school \xe2\x80\x94 and keep paying tuition \xe2\x80\x94 so long as they were also enrolled in and paying tuition for full-time college-level courses. The second year was important: Chinese families want their children to have enough time to prepare for the American college process. The Department of Homeland Security raised no immediate objections, Skilling told me. \xe2\x80\x9cThis had never been done before, so there were no regulations or road maps to follow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThis was literally a blank slate.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Soon, Oxford was attracting Chinese companies with even bigger ambitions. In a trip to Michigan in late 2012, Weiming\xe2\x80\x99s president, Lin Hao, laid out a vision in which 10,000 Chinese students would enroll in high schools in the United States, starting with experimental districts in the American heartland. Weiming, which bills itself as one of China\xe2\x80\x99s largest private education companies, has followed a similar strategy at home, building 15 campuses with more than 30,000 students in nine provincial cities. As Lin told a visiting superintendent from rural southern Ohio: \xe2\x80\x9cIn China, we have an old saying: \xe2\x80\x98Revolution begins in the countryside.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Within a year, Oxford signed a memorandum of understanding with Weiming that ensured an even greater supply of Chinese students \xe2\x80\x94 up to 200 of them annually for the next 20 years. For each student, Weiming would pay Oxford Community Schools $10,000 a year. It was a boon for a district facing budgetary pressures, but it was significantly less than the $40,000 that Chinese families paid Weiming for the full package of tuition, room and board, insurance and English-support classes. (The company says its fees have since gone down to about $30,500, as it competes for China\xe2\x80\x99s middle-class market.)'b'Weiming also offered American public schools a bigger prize. If the number of Chinese students reached a certain threshold \xe2\x80\x94 from 80 to 100 students \xe2\x80\x94 the company promised to build a multimillion-dollar student center and dormitory, at no cost to the school. As the Weiming contingent at Oxford grew, architects in Beijing and Michigan began drawing up blueprints for the dorm. Shortly after I met Skilling at the airport in the spring of 2014, he returned to Beijing to pore over blueprints of the proposed dorm with Lin. They met in Lin\xe2\x80\x99s office, which was designed to replicate the room where Chairman Mao met President Nixon in 1972 to re-establish relations between their two countries. The wall behind Lin\xe2\x80\x99s desk is covered with antique maps, one for each Chinese province where Weiming has built a school. It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be long, perhaps, before a map of Michigan adorned the wall. \xe2\x80\x9cLin Hao and I understand each other,\xe2\x80\x9d Skilling told me, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause we\xe2\x80\x99re both visionaries.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'The loudspeakers in 'b'Oxford High School started blaring halfway through Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s music-appreciation class. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a lockdown!\xe2\x80\x9d a voice announced. \xe2\x80\x9cAn armed intruder has entered the building!\xe2\x80\x9d Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s classmates leapt from their seats and, crouching low, rushed toward the door.'b'Korbin didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand the commotion. His English had improved, but his vocabulary was limited \xe2\x80\x94 and the loudspeakers were always a challenge. He had gotten used to the rock music that played between classes, creating the kind of happy chaos that would never be allowed in a regimented Chinese school. But this time the tone was grave: \xe2\x80\x9cTeachers, secure your classrooms!\xe2\x80\x9d Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s confusion only deepened when the music instructor hustled everyone into the girls\xe2\x80\x99 bathroom next door. \xe2\x80\x9cHurry up!\xe2\x80\x9d she yelled. When Korbin made it to the bathroom stalls, the teacher locked the door behind him \xe2\x80\x94 and they waited, in hushed excitement, until the drill was over.'b'Later, Korbin laughed about the experience. He had nearly forgotten that his mother\xe2\x80\x99s fears about American life \xe2\x80\x94 the guns, riots, school shootings \xe2\x80\x94 had almost scuttled his chances of studying in the United States. His father had made a reconnaissance visit to the country before he started, largely to reassure his wife that it was safe enough for their son. The only dangers Korbin faced in Oxford came from his innocent campaign for American friends.'b'One day, a group of jocks and slackers invited Korbin into the boys\xe2\x80\x99 bathroom for a few puffs from an electronic cigarette. The initiation was unusual for a strait-laced Chinese kid, but he was thrilled to be part of an American posse. \xe2\x80\x9cCool kids never study,\xe2\x80\x9d he was told, so Korbin, until then a conscientious A student, eased off his schoolwork. He started lifting weights, thinking he might attract a girlfriend \xe2\x80\x9cafter I build some muscle.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But when his new friends began pressing Korbin to join in hazing other kids, he pulled away. He wanted nothing to do with shouting racial epithets at the school\xe2\x80\x99s few African-American students or taunting other Chinese kids with curse words in Mandarin. \xe2\x80\x9cI tried so hard to make American friends, but I lost all of my own Chinese style and character,\xe2\x80\x9d Korbin told me last year. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not that eager to be part of American culture anymore. I would like to be myself, Chinese.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'With this part of his American dream tarnished, Korbin buried himself in his studies \xe2\x80\x94 and emerged as a patriot. In September 2015, after Beijing held a missile-studded military parade to celebrate Japan\xe2\x80\x99s defeat in World War II, he posted a picture on social media of the Chinese flag with the words \xe2\x80\x9cGreat China!\xe2\x80\x9d At school, he nearly came to blows with a student who was badmouthing the Chinese \xe2\x80\x9cdictatorship.\xe2\x80\x9d The object of his anger was not an American but a fellow Chinese student.'b'Korbin and Oscar left their host family and moved in with four other Chinese students under the care of a local grandmother. Korbin stopped lifting weights. He no longer wanted an American girlfriend. He barely interacted with American students anyway, because everyone in his college-level classes \xe2\x80\x94 the requirement to maintain his visa during his second year \xe2\x80\x94 was Chinese.'b'Still, Korbin remembers his senior year fondly. After he regained his academic focus, his grade-point average rose to near the top of his class \xe2\x80\x94 a 3.96 \xe2\x80\x94 and his standardized-test scores climbed just in time for his college applications. It felt good to be a nerd again. \xe2\x80\x9cI realized that I\xe2\x80\x99m an only son, the last of my family,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me. \xe2\x80\x9cMy parents pay a lot for me to come here, so why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I study hard?\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'\xe2\x80\x9cAre you here'b' for the meeting about the Chinese invasion?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'I had just pulled into the Oxford school parking lot when a bearded man in a baseball cap, an auto engineer, appeared at my car window and grumbled about the subject of that night\xe2\x80\x99s public forum: Weiming\xe2\x80\x99s plan to build its Oxford High School dorm. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it really our job to be educating Chinese kids?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.'b'Until October 2014, Oxford\xe2\x80\x99s move into China\xe2\x80\x99s orbit \xe2\x80\x94 the Mandarin-language program, the influx of Chinese students \xe2\x80\x94 had met little resistance. But the proposed dorm for Chinese students hit a nerve. A board meeting erupted in chaos. The local newspaper, The Oxford Leader, ran an editorial titled \xe2\x80\x9cMaking a Deal With the Devil.\xe2\x80\x9d And now, as the engineer and 60 other residents gathered in the school atrium, Skilling found himself on the defensive. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m here to defuse the situation,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'b'After touting the benefits of cultural exchange, Skilling outlined the finances. When the school reached its goal of 200 tuition-paying Chinese students, he said, an estimated $1.3 million in annual net revenue would flow into school coffers. The bigger incentive, he argued, was the multimillion-dollar dorm, which would include eight classrooms for use by all students. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t pay a penny,\xe2\x80\x9d Skilling said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a win-win.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Near the end of a contentious question-and-answer session, Skilling talked about how this project would bring money from China back into Michigan. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve heard Donald Trump say that China is trying to take advantage of us,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI agree. We\xe2\x80\x99re willing to sell our souls to get a foot in China, and it\xe2\x80\x99s a mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d A woman behind me whispered to her companion: \xe2\x80\x9cBut isn\xe2\x80\x99t that just what he\xe2\x80\x99s done?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Behind the scenes, a group of Oxford citizens began looking into the international program. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think the community objects to having foreign students here,\xe2\x80\x9d says Kallie Roesner-Meyers, a horse-stable owner who started the group, known as Team 20. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there was so much secrecy and misinformation around the whole China setup that we needed to find out more.\xe2\x80\x9d In the spring of 2015, Team 20, which sent the district a flurry of Freedom of Information Act requests, discovered, among other things, that the school board had agreed to a 20-year deal based on Skilling\xe2\x80\x99s recommendation.'b'In response to communication from Roesner-Meyers, federal agents questioned Skilling about his consulting work for Weiming. (Skilling says he ceased consulting before the Oxford deal went through.) One of them also wanted to know more about the visa maneuver by which Chinese students were staying for two years instead of one. According to Skilling, the agents said they \xe2\x80\x9cdidn\xe2\x80\x99t see any issue,\xe2\x80\x9d and he told a reporter that Roesner-Meyers \xe2\x80\x9cneeds to settle down.\xe2\x80\x9d But the pressure only persisted. Skilling retired later that year, and Weiming, without giving any explanation, put the dorm project on hold.'b'Weiming still has roughly 100 of its students at Oxford. And with thousands more students in its Chinese schools expecting to come to the United States, the search for a flagship location continues. In 2015, the company bid $12.6 million for a 58-acre property in West Hartford, Conn. It proposed to turn the former University of Connecticut site into an international school for 500 students, some of whom would feed into the city\xe2\x80\x99s public high schools. Local officials supported the plan, but last May the City Council, responding to vocal opposition to a little-known Chinese company taking control of land in the city center, voted against it.'b'Hovering in the background of the West Hartford debate was the suspension of the Weiming dorm in Oxford \xe2\x80\x94 and the government\xe2\x80\x99s unresolved investigation into its international program. By November, the Department of Homeland Security offered a verdict. The visa maneuver would not be permitted in the future, a blow to the model Oxford pioneered.'b'Oxford has not been punished, but it will no longer be allowed to keep Chinese students for more than one year on its campus. The current crop of 41 second-year Chinese students has been allowed to stay, though they must now take all of their classes at Rochester College, the institution that sponsors their visas, instead of Oxford High School. In June, after a semester of isolation, these kids will even earn their high-school diplomas. The future of the first-year students is up in the air. They may not be able to graduate from Oxford next year, and it is too late to re-enter China\xe2\x80\x99s 'b'gaokao'b' system. So they and their families may be back on the international-student carousel, scrambling to find another school to attend next year.'b''b'The alphabetical seating 'b'at Oxford High School\xe2\x80\x99s graduation ceremony last June put Korbin Yang in one of the last rows, surrounded by a clutch of Chinese classmates whose surnames begin with the letters X, Y or Z. They formed a sort of school-within-a-school, watching the parade of American graduates they barely knew. Many local students received raucous ovations as they crossed the stage. Korbin\xe2\x80\x99s name \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cgraduating summa cum laude, Jinkai Yang\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 elicited only a muffled clap or two. In the fall, Korbin headed to Pennsylvania State University. His parents were proud that he had gotten into a Top 50 university where almost 2,500 Chinese students were already enrolled.'b'When I visited Korbin last summer in Shenyang, he took me to an American-style craft-beer pub his father partly owns. Over a game of pool, he spoke positively about his experience at Oxford. Still, he admitted, he left Michigan after two years without a single American friend. That surprised him. \xe2\x80\x9cWeirdly, I think the experience made me appreciate Chinese culture even more,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. It\xe2\x80\x99s a common sentiment among Chinese students abroad, who find that their foreign experiences sharpen their sense of national pride. Over the summer, Korbin started delving into Chinese history books and training in kung fu. In America, he found his Chinese core.'b'Now halfway through his first year at Penn State, Korbin can spend entire days without speaking a word of English. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m around my Chinese friends all the time,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t get a chance to know American friends.\xe2\x80\x9d The current political climate may only isolate him further. Korbin is in America legally, studying hard and leaning toward a major in electrical engineering. But how welcoming is a country that increasingly regards his homeland as an economic and security threat? If Trump is serious about being tough on trade, Chinese students, while not in the foreground of such a fight, could be an easy lever for either side to pull. The collateral damage of restricting visas would be devastating, not only for the students themselves but also for high schools and universities, especially across the Midwest, that have become dependent on the billions of dollars the Chinese contribute economically every year.'b'An even bigger threat may lie within China. Late last year, President Xi\xe2\x80\x99s ideological campaign against foreign influences targeted the kind of schools that prepared Korbin for America. How this crackdown will affect the flow of Chinese students overseas is unclear. Parents may be compelled to send their children abroad at even younger ages to escape the closing cage.'b'For Korbin, the lack of American buddies and reawakened sense of national identity notwithstanding, high school in America still left a deep impression on him. Last Christmas, after exams, he went back not to Shenyang, but to Oxford. His second host mother gave him two hoodies and some of his favorite chocolate, and cooked a Christmas meal. Korbin presented her with a mug and played with the dogs he had helped care for as puppies. \xe2\x80\x9cI definitely wish I was still there,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me, and he sounded like just another first-year college kid, missing home.'b''b''b''b''b'As a lonely teenager growing up in Virginia, I fed off any pop culture that could show me different ways of being from what I saw on \xe2\x80\x9cThe Cosby Show\xe2\x80\x9d reruns or read about in an Ann M. Martin book. This was the early 2000s, before social platforms had taken off: LiveJournal was still in its infancy; Tumblr had not yet been created. Friendster and Myspace, the most popular of the networks that did exist, were more about sharing perfectly angled photos than having conversations or bouncing ideas off someone. When, in college, a spirited English teaching assistant (who once canceled class for the week to attend a riot-grrrl punk reunion show in Washington) introduced me to zines and the early feminist publishing movement of the 1990s, I felt as if I had been given a lifeline to the outside world. Those self-published, unofficial magazines offered tangible glimpses of radical feminism, social-justice movements, queer history and subcultures that I always knew existed but had little access to. The world seemed to open up for me.'b'In theory, the maturation of the internet should have killed off the desire for zines entirely. The web is a Gutenberg press on steroids, predicated on free software platforms created by companies that invest considerable sums to lure people to their sites and make exactly the kind of content I craved growing up. Millions, maybe hundreds of millions, of posts are published to social-media sites each day. And yet somehow, it can feel impossible to engage with new ideas, even as our compulsive inability to stop scrolling exposes us to an unending stream of new content. Yes, you can catch tweetstorms on Twitter, watch someone\xe2\x80\x99s life unfold on Instagram, do deep dives into hashtags on Tumblr or watch video diaries on YouTube that explore diverse perspectives, but the clutter of everything else happening at the same time online can make it difficult to really digest and absorb the perspective being offered.'b'Which might be part of the reason zines never disappeared \xe2\x80\x94 and are even available in abundance in 2017. A few months ago, I walked into a Laundromat in Brooklyn where a former cellphone kiosk had been transformed into a feminist queer shop called the Troll Hole. I was thrilled to find it stocked with the same kinds of small booklets I consumed in college, though much better designed and produced. They contained nonbinary coming-of-age stories, photo essays featuring gender nonconforming people of Latin-American descent, trans Muslim narratives, first-generation essays, fat-positive imagery. I scooped up as many as I could rationally read in one sitting.'b'Many of the offline zine projects I came across have some online presence, too. Sula Collective, for example, which describes itself as a journal by and for people of color, actually started out on the web as an art magazine for people growing up \xe2\x80\x9cin the suburbs and Deep South,\xe2\x80\x9d as one of its founders, Kassandra Pi\xc3\xb1ero, put it to me. It was meant for anyone who \xe2\x80\x9cdidn\xe2\x80\x99t have access to galleries and events.\xe2\x80\x9d Pi\xc3\xb1ero is 21, and the only world she has ever known is one that is also lived partly online. But she found that publishing on the internet often had the unintended and unconscious effect of causing her to cater to the aesthetics of those platforms. \xe2\x80\x9cThe internet should be a place with no rules, and freedom, but it\xe2\x80\x99s not,\xe2\x80\x9d Pi\xc3\xb1ero said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a certain pressure to conform to certain aesthetics.\xe2\x80\x9d It was something I had noticed myself. Each social-media platform tends to reward certain behaviors and styles of posting, all in the interest of building fans and followers who are invested in the performance of a persona (maybe even more so than the Geppetto-like person orchestrating it all). Instagram is a place for intimate-seeming photos, Twitter for clever quips and collaborative memes. Facebook demands an unmitigated rawness that can be terrifying at times. With all, the works are often made to fit the platform, not the other way around.'b''b'Producing zines can'b' offer an unexpected respite from the scrutiny on the internet, which can be as oppressive as it is liberating. Shakar Mujukian, publisher of The Hye-Phen \xe2\x80\x94 a zine by and about queer and trans Armenians who, as he puts it, often \xe2\x80\x9cfeel as ignored and invisible as their motherland\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 told me via email that just because technology can fully replace something doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it should. He described zines as the precursor to personal blogs, but personal blogs have been on the decline over the last decade. And zines can\xe2\x80\x99t get replies or hateful remarks in a comments section. Publishing ideas outside the mainstream can make an author incredibly vulnerable; the web is polluted with a culture of toxicity that invites attacks. Zines, in Mujukian\xe2\x80\x99s vision, \xe2\x80\x9care essentially about reclamation. You get to make your own media and define your own narrative in the way you want to and can.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Karen Gisonny is the periodicals librarian at the New York Public Library and specializes in alternative publications and zines. We\xe2\x80\x99ve spoken over the years about alternative media and the role that it plays among the people who make it and consume it. She noted that zines allow for an \xe2\x80\x9celement of freedom that\xe2\x80\x99s not beholden to anyone.\xe2\x80\x9d We think of the web as a place for freedom, but with zines, authors control every aspect, from the design to the distribution. When I visited her at the library, she showed me some of her newest acquisitions, which included the first issue of Dr. RAD\xe2\x80\x99s Queer Health Show, a guide for self-exams and checkups for all gendered bodies, and Blue Collar Review, a journal of progressive working-class literature that is made in Virginia. She explained that zines could be seen as a historical record of the current moment. To their creators, zines can feel like necessary means of defiance, even resistance to cultural norms that rarely acknowledge them.'b'Devin N. Morris, who edits and publishes 3 Dot Zine, told me that he sees self-publishing as a political and radical act. He\xe2\x80\x99s a young queer artist from Baltimore, and the zines he creates reflect that experience and create a historical narrative that otherwise would be ignored. For him, the act of creating a zine is more about defining his reality on his terms and legitimizing it than it is about the novelty of making indie media and distributing it. It was a sentiment I heard from almost every zine creator I spoke to. Morris, who recently hosted an indie-press fair at the Museum of Contemporary African Diasporan Arts, said that zines have a way of encouraging people to have \xe2\x80\x9cinspiring interactions in real life.\xe2\x80\x9d He described a hunger to physically interact beyond simple likes or direct messages. Social apps weren\xe2\x80\x99t made to inspire that desire; they were created so that there would be no need.'b'And it perhaps reflects why zines can feel so much more intimate than a Facebook post. The deliberation and care that goes into making them is important. The internet is especially adept at compressing humanity and making it easy to forget there are people behind tweets, posts and memes.'b''b''b''b''b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not talking politics at all,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom Brady said in response to the first of several 'b'questions about, predictably, politics'b'. Brady has been getting a lot of these, beginning with Super Bowl week\xe2\x80\x99s Opening Night on Monday at Minute Maid Park in Houston. This is not your average 'b'stick-to-sports Super Bowl'b' week in America. The chaos in Washington, bedlam at airports and protests across the country serve as an unsettling crowd noise to even the serene superstar quarterback for the New England Patriots.'b'Brady is friends with President Trump. So are the Patriots\xe2\x80\x99 owner, Robert Kraft, and Coach Bill Belichick. No sports team has been more closely associated with a new president, or perhaps any president, at least since Richard Nixon very publicly adopted the Washington Redskins as his own and even called occasional plays for Coach George Allen. Like Trump, the Patriots are a divisive juggernaut that tend to engender strong passions for and against. The team represents a kind of sporting ideal of Trump\xe2\x80\x99s promise to make \xe2\x80\x9cAmerica win so much, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be bored of winning.\xe2\x80\x9d New England, which will appear in an N.F.L.-record ninth Super Bowl on Sunday, is a team that wins so much that a lot of America has become, yes, bored of its winning. And no small number of fans are convinced that the Patriots (like Trump) achieve their victories through dubious means and wish they would just go away and get off their TVs forever.'b'The three main architects of the team\xe2\x80\x99s success \xe2\x80\x94 Kraft, Belichick and Brady \xe2\x80\x94 have taken pains to emphasize that their allegiance to Trump is based on friendship and loyalty, nothing more. They play golf and attend weddings and call to congratulate one another on elections and Super Bowl wins, those kinds of everyday things.'b'They are quick to assert their bipartisan or apolitical bona fides. Kraft has been a supporter of many Democrats and Democratic causes over the years; Belichick declared himself apolitical in response to queries last fall after Trump at a campaign rally in New Hampshire read aloud from an effusive note of congratulations that Belichick had sent him.'b'Brady started getting questions about Trump last fall when a red \xe2\x80\x9cMake America Great Again\xe2\x80\x9d cap was spotted in his Gillette Stadium locker, reportedly put there by Kraft. Brady at first gave sheepish, gee-whiz responses suggesting that it would be cool to have a golf pal as president. (\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99d be a putting green on the White House lawn,\xe2\x80\x9d he predicted.) But the questions took on an edgier urgency as Trump\xe2\x80\x99s victories and offenses kept mounting \xe2\x80\x94 ending with Trump where he is now, and Brady dropping into a reflexive kneel-down every time Trump\xe2\x80\x99s name is mentioned to him.'b'Brady, whose makeshift podium on the Minute Maid field on Monday night was swarmed with a few hundred reporters, was the central battlefield of incoming Trump-related questions. He countered with his most anodyne deflections. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m just a positive person\xe2\x80\x9d became Brady\xe2\x80\x99s go-to blow off, which he also deployed in the face of several questions about Deflategate, the air-pressure-in-footballs scandal given new life this week by the prospect of Commissioner Roger Goodell\xe2\x80\x99s having to award the Super Bowl trophy to the man he suspended for four games to begin this season.'b'Other Atlanta Falcons and Patriots players were also invited to weigh in on political matter but mostly demurred. The Falcons receiver Mohamed Sanu, a practicing Muslim, was asked to discuss Trump\xe2\x80\x99s executive order banning immigration from seven majority-Muslim countries. He declined politely, other than to say that it was \xe2\x80\x9c a very tough situation\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9chard for me to talk about right now.\xe2\x80\x9d I asked two of the more thoughtful members of the Patriots, the defensive linemen Alan Branch and Chris Long, if they had anything to say about these developments. They absolutely did, they said, and would speak out at some point later, but they did not want to create any \xe2\x80\x9cdistractions\xe2\x80\x9d during Super Bowl week. The Patriots tight end Martellus Bennett did say that if the Patriots won on Sunday and were invited to the White House, he would probably not go, because \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t support the guy that\xe2\x80\x99s in the [White] House.\xe2\x80\x9d And really, was that so distracting?'b'One enduring reason the Patriots are constantly being asked about their Trump connections is that the new president is hardly shy about reminding people of them.'b'He has been dropping Brady\xe2\x80\x99s name \xe2\x80\x94 and Kraft\xe2\x80\x99s, and Belichick\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x94 at every opportunity over the last several months. This first became evident to me in the fall of 2015 as Trump was enjoying his unlikely rise to front-runner status in the Republican primaries and I had several visits with him for a profile I was writing for the magazine. A few months earlier, I had written a story about Brady, who had just won his fourth Super Bowl and was then embroiled in the Deflategate fiasco, in which the league said it was \xe2\x80\x9cmore probable than not\xe2\x80\x9d that Brady was \xe2\x80\x9cat least generally aware\xe2\x80\x9d that a minuscule amount of air pressure might have been removed from the footballs he used in the first half of the Patriots\xe2\x80\x99 45-7 blowout of the Indianapolis Colts in the A.F.C. championship game.'b'When Trump learned that I had recently spent time with Brady and around the Patriots, he became even more animated on the subject \xe2\x80\x94 and insistent that I enlist the star quarterback to say nice things about him for my story. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a good friend of mine,\xe2\x80\x9d Trump said about Brady for the first of many times. \xe2\x80\x9cA really good friend of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d Trump declared himself \xe2\x80\x9cdisgusted\xe2\x80\x9d by the N.F.L.\xe2\x80\x99s treatment of Brady over Deflategate. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so ridiculous what they\xe2\x80\x99re doing to him.\xe2\x80\x9d He mentioned he had just spoken to Brady.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe said: \xe2\x80\x98Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x94 he calls me Mr. Trump, which he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, because we play golf all the time. Anyway, he says: \xe2\x80\x98Mr. Trump \xe2\x80\x94 Donald,\xe2\x80\x99 he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t even know what the [expletive] to call me. It\xe2\x80\x99s the craziest thing. He\xe2\x80\x99s a friend of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Also, Kraft \xe2\x80\x94 great friend, great friend. George Steinbrenner \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cbig time winner\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 was Trump\xe2\x80\x99s best friend, he said, and now Kraft is right up there, too, as one of his favorite guys. Kraft had visited him a few days earlier, and Trump said he chastised the Patriots owner for not fighting harder against the league\xe2\x80\x99s suspension of Brady, in addition to other sanctions levied by the N.F.L. against the Patriots (including the loss of a first-round draft pick and a record fine). \xe2\x80\x9cYou know, Bob Kraft is also one of my best friends,\xe2\x80\x9d Trump told me. \xe2\x80\x9cI said, \xe2\x80\x98Bob, you should have never made the deal.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cBob said, \xe2\x80\x98I had a wink from the commissioner,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Trump added, meaning that Kraft seemed to think that by standing down and not fighting the N.F.L., the league would reduce Brady\xe2\x80\x99s penalty on appeal.'b'Kraft was under pressure, Trump explained. \xe2\x80\x9cHe choked, just like Romney choked. He said: \xe2\x80\x98You know what? They winked at me.\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98Bob when you make a deal, you should have gotten it all wrapped up.\xe2\x80\x99 Who ever heard of making a deal like that? Now you got this mess.\xe2\x80\x9d Kraft should never have trusted Goodell, he said.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe commissioner is a weak guy,\xe2\x80\x9d Trump said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen he made the Ray Rice deal, everybody said: You\xe2\x80\x99re stupid. You\xe2\x80\x99re weak. And it was such a weak deal. So now he\xe2\x80\x99s going overboard with their star, Brady.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'He added: \xe2\x80\x9cThe commissioner is a dope. He\xe2\x80\x99s a stupid guy.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'By far the best deal Kraft ever made was hiring Belichick, Trump said. Who, by the way, is also \xe2\x80\x9ca great, great friend\xe2\x80\x9d of Trump\xe2\x80\x99s, even though everyone just assumes he\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9ca really rough guy.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cSo I go to the Patriots game last year,\xe2\x80\x9d Trump said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m on the sidelines with Kraft. He\xe2\x80\x99s got Les Moonves right here. He\xe2\x80\x99s got a lot of different people. And Belichick comes over in his Patriots sweatshirt and the hoodie and the whole thing. He hugs me, and he kisses me, and he said: \xe2\x80\x98I love you. You\xe2\x80\x99re the greatest.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Trump sat at his cluttered desk and seemed almost dreamy at the memory, as if the reception from Belichick genuinely moved him. \xe2\x80\x9cHe just feels warmly toward me, Belichick does,\xe2\x80\x9d Trump said. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that the craziest thing?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'After the Deflategate ruling was handed down by the commissioner that spring, Trump said he tried to persuade Brady to sue the league personally. \xe2\x80\x9cI said, \xe2\x80\x98Tom.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x94 I gave him a lawyer. \xe2\x80\x94 I said: \xe2\x80\x98Here\xe2\x80\x99s what you do. Sue the N.F.L. for $500 million tomorrow. Sue \xe2\x80\x99em up in Boston, for everything. They\xe2\x80\x99ll come to the table.\xe2\x80\x99 He said, \xe2\x80\x98Aw, man.\xe2\x80\x99 He really was torn. He\xe2\x80\x99s not Trump. He said, \xe2\x80\x98I just want to win another Super Bowl.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Trump told Brady that he understood.'b'A few weeks later, the future president was showing me around Trump National Golf Club in Rancho Palos Verdes, Calif. We were accompanied by Damon Winter, a Times photographer who had, a few months earlier, photographed Brady for the profile that was published in February. At one point, as Trump showed me around his 7,242-yard golf Xanadu on the Pacific, he looked over at Winter. \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s got a better body, me or Tom Brady?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. (No answer from Winter that I recall.)'b'In the course of my time with Trump, he kept urging me to call Brady and ask him about his great friendship with Trump. Brady would say great things, no doubt. \xe2\x80\x9cAsk him, \xe2\x80\x98How is Trump as a golfer?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Trump urged.'b'Brady had been particularly hunkered down during the Deflategate ordeal. N.F.L. politics can be brutal like the real-life kind. Plus, the flare-up in the press over his friendship with Trump was not making Brady any more eager to engage beyond his usual helpings of football platitudes. Finally, after Trump kept insisting, I reached out to Brady, fully expecting a polite stiff-arm from the quarterback, and who could blame him? \xe2\x80\x9cI really have no interest in political talk right now,\xe2\x80\x9d Brady wrote. \xe2\x80\x9cI have learned way too much about politics the last seven months.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'H'b'ours before the start of the Women\xe2\x80\x99s March on Washington on Jan. 21, every Metro car leading to the National Mall was packed so tight that it could take on no more riders. At station after station, the train doors would open, and the passengers would look out on throngs of fellow protesters \xe2\x80\x94 women, men, children, babies, the occasional dog \xe2\x80\x94 waiting on the platform. As people emerged from underground into the morning air, it was hard to tell where to go, so they found their way by gauging the human density, moving until they reached a spot so full of people that they could no longer move at all. There was an enormous rally happening somewhere in there \xe2\x80\x94 activists and celebrities speaking into microphones \xe2\x80\x94 but much of the crowd couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see or hear anything except rumbling waves of cheers. By the time they were meant to march, the crowd was so large that it already stretched across the entire route, from the rally site near the Capitol to the Ellipse near the White House.'b'The signs they carried spoke to any number of issues: immigration, abortion, race, the environment, inequality, the new president. REFUGEES WELCOME, KEEP YOUR LAWS OUT OF MY VAGINA, BLACK LIVES MATTER, SCIENCE IS REAL, FLINT NEEDS CLEAN WATER, NOBODY LIKES YOU. The handmade pink \xe2\x80\x9cpussyhats\xe2\x80\x9d that many marchers wore \xe2\x80\x94 a reference to Donald Trump\xe2\x80\x99s caught-on-tape boasts about grabbing unsuspecting women by the genitals \xe2\x80\x94 had been sneered at in the days before the march. They were called corny, girlie, a waste of time. Seen from above, though, on thousands of marchers, their wave of color created a powerful image.'b'It was, somewhat unexpectedly, one of the largest mass demonstrations in American history. Millions of protesters \xe2\x80\x94 estimates range from three to five million \xe2\x80\x94 took to the streets of Washington, Los Angeles, New York, Palm Beach, Fla., Boise, Idaho, even Fargo, N.D. Sister demonstrations were held in Thailand, in Malawi, in Antarctica. The energy of almost every group alarmed or incensed by Trump\xe2\x80\x99s election seemed to have poured into a single demonstration. That it happened on the day after his inauguration was not surprising. What was striking was that all these people had come together under the auspices of a march for women.'b'Just two months earlier, the left did not appear to be a unified front. The polls had barely closed before the infighting began. Some blamed Hillary Clinton for ignoring Wisconsin, or the Democratic National Committee for boxing other candidates out of the primary field. Some blamed identity politics, which made working-class white voters \xe2\x80\x9cfeel excluded,\xe2\x80\x9d according to Prof. Mark Lilla of Columbia. Others blamed white people, particularly the coastal ones who couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get their heartland relatives on their side.'b'But a crew of bummed-out, angry women was still aiming its ire at Trump. In the hours after his victory on Nov. 8, Teresa Shook, a retired lawyer in Hawaii, posted to Facebook, suggesting a march on Washington. Some women on the East Coast had the same idea. At first, these suggestions were so impulsive that they seemed almost metaphorical. But within days, tens of thousands of women had pledged to join in. Over the course of two months, the idea became something far bigger than initially imagined. Eventually, an entire organizing team would have permits, T-shirts, fleets of buses, portable toilets, celebrity sponsors and support from Gloria Steinem. Men meekly asked their female family members and Twitter followers: \xe2\x80\x9cAre we allowed to join?\xe2\x80\x9d By the middle of January, with the event shaping up to be 'b'the'b' anti-Trump demonstration, the New York magazine writer Jonathan Chait worried that \xe2\x80\x9cWomen\xe2\x80\x99s March\xe2\x80\x9d was too niche an organizing principle \xe2\x80\x94 it was a \xe2\x80\x9cbad name,\xe2\x80\x9d 'b'he tweeted'b', a divisive \xe2\x80\x9cbrand.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The opposite turned out to be true: Women led the resistance, and everyone followed. A march for women managed to crowd a broad opposition force onto its platform. In the weeks since the march, that energy has only spread. After Trump\xe2\x80\x99s executive order halting travel from seven Muslim-majority countries, the march\xe2\x80\x99s striking proof of concept \xe2\x80\x94 hit the streets, and a surprising number of others will join you \xe2\x80\x94 fueled more spontaneous actions in unexpected places: outside courthouses, Trump hotels, airport terminals, the offices of Senator Mitch McConnell. At each protest, you were likely to see a few pink cat ears poking out of the crowd, a reminder of the opposition\xe2\x80\x99s first gathering. It seems unlikely that any other kind of march would have turned out quite this way. In this moment, it happened that \xe2\x80\x9cwomen\xe2\x80\x9d was the one tent large enough to contain almost every major strain of protest against Trump.'b'Those who know their feminist history might see a paradox here. The women\xe2\x80\x99s movement has not always been a site for unity. It has been marked just as deeply by its fractures, failures and tensions. But more than a century of internal turmoil has also forced the movement to reckon with its divisions. Now, the question is whether it can bring even more Americans into the fold.'b''b'Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s loss on Nov. 8'b' was a pivotal, identity-shifting moment in the course of the American women\xe2\x80\x99s movement. In an evening, the would-be first female president was shoved to the side by what a sizable chunk of the nation saw as that classic historical figure: the male chauvinist pig. In parts of the popular imagination, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t just a loss for Clinton or for the Democratic Party. It was a repudiation of feminism itself.'b'But Clinton has always been a wary avatar of feminism. In 2008, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t run for president as a \xe2\x80\x9cwomen\xe2\x80\x99s candidate\xe2\x80\x9d; if anything, she campaigned with her sex in the closet and the strategist Mark Penn advising her to harden her image into an American Iron Lady. \xe2\x80\x9cThey do not want someone who would be the first mama,\xe2\x80\x9d he wrote in one memo. Years later, HBO\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cVeep\xe2\x80\x9d would satirize that posture through its own fictional politician. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t identify myself as a woman!\xe2\x80\x9d she tells her staff. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople can\xe2\x80\x99t know that. Men hate that. And women who hate women hate that, which I believe is most women.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In the eight years between Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s first and second presidential campaigns, though, something shifted: Feminism became fashionable. By the start of the Obama era, incisive women\xe2\x80\x99s blogs like Jezebel and Feministing had already hit the web and started throwing popcorn at the big screen of American culture, covering the same topics that women\xe2\x80\x99s magazines did \xe2\x80\x94 fashion, movies, sex \xe2\x80\x94 but taking on the women\xe2\x80\x99s magazines too. By Obama\xe2\x80\x99s second term, this model had thrived and multiplied so many times over that even a co-founder of the sports website Bleacher Report started his own women\xe2\x80\x99s site, Bustle. Soon enough, no corner of culture was safe from a feminist critique, from Christmas songs to \xe2\x80\x9cmanspreading.\xe2\x80\x9d Pop stars \xe2\x80\x94 people like Lady Gaga, Katy Perry and Taylor Swift \xe2\x80\x94 were asked if they were feminists, and if they shied away from the label, outrage would greet them online.'b'Feminism became increasingly popular, but in a very specific way \xe2\x80\x94 one attuned to the concerns of people with office jobs and time to spend online. The feminist priorities of this new media landscape tended to involve topics that middle-class women would experience firsthand: reproductive rights, catcalling, campus rape, professional opportunity, pop-culture representation. The writers setting its tone tended to be young women who were asked to produce large amounts of clickable copy, for not much money, in very little time, exploring feminist issues not through time-intensive reporting but through \xe2\x80\x9ctakes\xe2\x80\x9d on the women already making news: the work-life balance of Yahoo\xe2\x80\x99s chief executive, Marissa Mayer; the pay gap between Jennifer Lawrence and her male co-stars. Some content chased sexist slights down to the most passing personal concerns, like 'b'an item from The Huffington Post'b' that sighed: \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no proper way for a woman to cut her hair, let alone do anything right in this world.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'It\xe2\x80\x99s not that women\xe2\x80\x99s activist groups vanished or political organizing stalled. But it did become possible for an American woman to cultivate a relationship to feminism that was primarily consumerist: There were feminist TV shows to watch, feminist celebrities to follow, feminist clothes to buy. Unlike many other major social movements, women\xe2\x80\x99s liberation dovetails neatly with an important advertising demographic, a lesson capitalism absorbed more than a century ago. In 1908, the advertiser J.Walter Thompson hired suffragists to address the growing women\xe2\x80\x99s market. Over the next decades, the industry would slip women\xe2\x80\x99s rights messaging into ad copy. Old Dutch Cleanser offered \xe2\x80\x9cfreedom from household drudgery\xe2\x80\x9d; Shredded Wheat promised a \xe2\x80\x9cdeclaration of independence\xe2\x80\x9d from cooking. These days, even our bath products have achieved empowerment. Ads for Secret deodorant nudge us to ask for a raise, and those for Always prompt us to challenge stereotypes about girls. Dove wants us to feel beautiful at any size.'b'It\xe2\x80\x99s telling which strand of feminism these brands have deemed marketable: the one that doubles as self-help. This is a vision of feminism in which the primary thing that needs to change is a woman\xe2\x80\x99s frame of mind. Something similar happened to the pop stars who once hesitated to call themselves feminists \xe2\x80\x94 they came around to feminism by redefining feminism around themselves. To Lady Gaga, feminism was about protecting \xe2\x80\x9cthe integrity of women who are ambitious.\xe2\x80\x9d Taylor Swift realized, she said, that she had \xe2\x80\x9cbeen taking a feminist stance without actually saying so.\xe2\x80\x9d Feminism was being defined down to its most benign interpretation. It was less a political platform than a brand identity.'b'In 2013, \xe2\x80\x9cLean In,\xe2\x80\x9d by Sheryl Sandberg, raised this pop-cultural subtext to the level of text. Sandberg called the book \xe2\x80\x9csort of a feminist manifesto,\xe2\x80\x9d but it preached individual solutions to systemic problems, encouraging women to focus on \xe2\x80\x9cinternal obstacles\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cdismantle the hurdles in ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d This feminist mode, where personal success becomes synonymous with social progress, can be plugged into any number of political orientations. The latest model for the corporate-celebrity feminist brand is Ivanka Trump, who has built a lifestyle company under the hashtag #WomenWhoWork. A recent pitch neatly weds activist language with shoppable solutions: \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re committed to solving problems. If we can\xe2\x80\x99t find a solution, we\xe2\x80\x99ll make it ourselves (case in point: the Soho Tote, the ultimate work bag).\xe2\x80\x9d'b'By the time the 2016 campaign rolled around, Clinton wasn\xe2\x80\x99t just permitted to run as a feminist \xe2\x80\x94 she was practically obligated to. Her messaging shifted accordingly. Years of women\xe2\x80\x99s debating the right way to be a feminist had the side effect of forcing the first female major-party candidate to the left. In 2008, she argued that she wanted abortion to be \xe2\x80\x9csafe, legal and rare \xe2\x80\x94 and by rare I mean rare.\xe2\x80\x9d In last year\xe2\x80\x99s debates, she stopped qualifying her support. \xe2\x80\x9cI will defend Planned Parenthood,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in one. \xe2\x80\x9cI will defend Roe v. Wade, and I will defend women\xe2\x80\x99s rights to make their own health care decisions.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Meanwhile, her campaign mimicked the aesthetics of the pop-cultural feminist mode. The candidate affirmed her feminism in a video interview with Lena Dunham, posed in a Kim Kardashian selfie and made a cameo on \xe2\x80\x9cBroad City.\xe2\x80\x9d Her campaign posted a BuzzFeed-style listicle informing Latinos that Clinton was \xe2\x80\x9cjust like your 'b'abuela'b'.\xe2\x80\x9d (With the Twitter hashtag #NotMyAbuela, those voters begged to differ.) Her site sold embroidered pillows that said \xe2\x80\x9cA Woman\xe2\x80\x99s Place Is in the White House\xe2\x80\x9d and a T-shirt with a big \xe2\x80\x9cYAAAS, HILLARY!\xe2\x80\x9d printed over her senior portrait from high school. After Trump accused her of playing \xe2\x80\x9cthe woman\xe2\x80\x99s card,\xe2\x80\x9d her campaign introduced a free hot-pink \xe2\x80\x9cOfficial Hillary for America Woman Card\xe2\x80\x9d that drove more than $2 million in donations within days. One young woman earnestly prodded her at an Iowa campaign event: \xe2\x80\x9cIf you could choose, would you rather be the president or Beyonc\xc3\xa9?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Pop feminism, having been washed of its political urgency, was now being integrated back into politics at the highest level. The candidate who once shrank from feminism was positioning herself as an icon of the movement. Her image became closely aligned with two metaphors \xe2\x80\x94 the pantsuit and the glass ceiling \xe2\x80\x94 that speak to a particular kind of woman: a corporate careerist at the top of her field. A \xe2\x80\x9csecret\xe2\x80\x9d Facebook group, 'b'Pantsuit Nation'b', popped up to encourage Clinton supporters to wear pantsuits to the polls. When she clinched the Democratic nomination for president last June \xe2\x80\x94 the one she would formally accept the following month, dressed in suffragist white \xe2\x80\x94 Clinton called back to the Seneca Falls convention of 1848, where \xe2\x80\x9ca small but determined group of women, and men, came together with the idea that women deserved equal rights.\xe2\x80\x9d The feminist project started there, she implied \xe2\x80\x94 and she was going to finish it.'b'When Clinton lost, pop feminism suffered a crisis. As everyone pored over exit polls, some of the long-simmering fractures between different groups of women exploded into view. Ninety-four percent of black women voted for Clinton, but 53 percent of white women voted for Trump, perhaps more likely to see themselves in his vision of the world than in the pop feminism that fed Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s campaign. Despite Trump\xe2\x80\x99s palpable, eminently bloggable disrespect for women \xe2\x80\x94 and that infamous tape \xe2\x80\x94 he had successfully courted a faction of female voters. His win suggested that Americans were more comfortable with misogyny than many had thought, but it also burst the bubble of cheery pop feminism, which had achieved its huge popularity at the expense of class consciousness and racial solidarity.'b'In some places, you could watch the mood turn in a matter of days. The Pantsuit Nation Facebook group ceased its celebrations and became a site for sharing stories of pain and resilience. But when the group\xe2\x80\x99s founder, a Maine educator named Libby Chamberlain, announced a plan to channel the power of the group in real life, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly a call to activism \xe2\x80\x94 it was a coffee-table book. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a force, Pantsuit Nation,\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s see if we can harness that force within the pages of a book and see it on night stands and coffee tables all around the world.\xe2\x80\x9d The idea brought on a revolt. \xe2\x80\x9cThe N.R.A. with its five million members has a stranglehold on Congress,\xe2\x80\x9d one commenter wrote. \xe2\x80\x9cPantsuit Nation has four million members and decides its main mission is \xe2\x80\x98storytelling\xe2\x80\x99 and now, selling books. What a colossal waste.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But for some outside observers, this was a productive comeuppance. Rhon Manigault-Bryant, an associate professor of Africana studies at Williams College in Massachusetts, published \xe2\x80\x9cAn Open Letter to White Liberal Feminists,\xe2\x80\x9d 'b'on the website Black Perspectives'b', expressing her disappointment that it had taken Donald Trump to shake them into her reality. \xe2\x80\x9cI am delighted that you have received the potential awakening of a lifetime, and that now you might actually get what so many of us have been describing all along,\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote. \xe2\x80\x9cWelcome to that deep perpetual angst. Embrace it, and allow it to motivate you to a deeper form of action.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'In those same'b' November weeks, the nascent march-on-Washington project was navigating its own identity crisis. Some of the early organizers had romantic-comedy-type jobs \xe2\x80\x94 pastry chef, yoga instructor. One of the women, Bob Bland, a fashion designer, had amassed a small online following by designing \xe2\x80\x9cNASTY WOMAN\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cBAD HOMBRE\xe2\x80\x9d T-shirts and selling them online. \xe2\x80\x9cI had this whole network of \xe2\x80\x98nasty women\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98bad hombres,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d she told me. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter the election, they were looking to me like, \xe2\x80\x98What are we going to do next?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Disparate organizers convened around a Facebook event announcing a Million Women March. There was one major problem with this: In 1997, activists organized a Million Woman March in Philadelphia to address the particular concerns of black women. When this new march on Washington unwittingly chose a very similar name, it crystallized the idea that the nascent movement was being run by a handful of white women with no organizing history. Comments began pouring in from all sides.'b'The organizers had stumbled into a conflict that has dogged women\xe2\x80\x99s organizing from the very beginning: Of all the tensions that have coursed through the women\xe2\x80\x99s movement, none has ever been quite so pronounced as the one between white and black women. Consider what happened when Sojourner Truth showed up at a women\xe2\x80\x99s rights convention in Ohio in 1851. Frances Gage, the woman running the show, recalled the scene 12 years later: \xe2\x80\x9cThe leaders of the movement trembled on seeing a tall, gaunt black woman in a gray dress and white turban, surmounted with an uncouth sunbonnet, march deliberately into the church, walk with the air of a queen up the aisle and take her seat upon the pulpit steps.\xe2\x80\x9d A \xe2\x80\x9cbuzz of disapprobation\xe2\x80\x9d spread through the church. White women in attendance complained that a black woman\xe2\x80\x99s testimony would distract from the convention\xe2\x80\x99s focus. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let her speak, Mrs. Gage \xe2\x80\x94 it will ruin us,\xe2\x80\x9d one said. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery newspaper in the land will have our cause mixed up with abolition and niggers, and we shall be utterly denounced.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Throughout the convention, men arrived to speak out against women\xe2\x80\x99s suffrage. Women, they said, were too weak and helpless to be trusted with the power of the vote. Because \xe2\x80\x9cthere were very few women in those days who dared to \xe2\x80\x98speak in meeting,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d as Gage put it, their points went unchallenged until Truth stepped forward. White women hissed, but Truth\xe2\x80\x99s very identity nullified the arguments coming from both men and women in attendance. \xe2\x80\x9cThat man over there says that women need to be helped into carriages, and lifted over ditches, and to have the best place everywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody ever helps me into carriages, or over mud puddles, or gives me any best place. And ain\xe2\x80\x99t I a woman? Look at me! Look at my arm!\xe2\x80\x9d She rolled up her sleeve to the shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cI have plowed and planted, and gathered into barns, and no man could head me. And ain\xe2\x80\x99t I a woman?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In that moment, Truth shattered an idea of white femininity that had been used to both underpin and undermine the cause of suffrage. As a slave, she had worked in the fields like a man; as a free black woman, she could not rely on the offerings of white male gentility. Gage wrote that Truth\xe2\x80\x99s testimony compelled the white women in attendance to embrace her \xe2\x80\x9cwith streaming eyes, and hearts beating with gratitude.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'But two years later, Truth still drew jeers from white crowds when she attended women\xe2\x80\x99s meetings. A vision of whiteness was ingrained in the leaders and the arguments of the mainstream movement. Even the suffragists\xe2\x80\x99 signature white clothes were deliberately chosen to signal purity. This ideal of feminine virtue did not extend to black women, or working-class ones. Some suffragists made their racism and classism explicit. In 1894, a white woman at a meeting of the Brooklyn Woman Suffrage Association complained that New York had become an \xe2\x80\x9casylum for the trash of all nations,\xe2\x80\x9d arguing that women\xe2\x80\x99s suffrage ought to be restricted. \xe2\x80\x9cThink what it means to give it to all women,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cOur criminal and pauper men have wives; there are thousands of female operatives in tobacco factories and similar fields of labor; there are probably two million Negro women in this country who are but little uplifted above the plane of animals.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'One curious point of this history is that so many suffragists came from the antislavery movement. Elizabeth Cady Stanton and Susan B. Anthony, whose partnership would come to define the suffrage movement in the United States, started their activist careers as abolitionists. But after the Civil War, as black men and all women agitated for the right to vote, a political battle broke out over who would be enfranchised first. (Either way, black women would be last.) In 1865, Stanton lamented having to \xe2\x80\x9cstand aside to see \xe2\x80\x98Sambo\xe2\x80\x99 walk into the kingdom first,\xe2\x80\x9d as she put it in The National Anti-Slavery Standard.'b'Over time, these racial contours would harden into lasting institutions. When women\xe2\x80\x99s social clubs spread across the United States at the turn of the century, two models emerged. Whites-only clubs leveraged middle-class women\xe2\x80\x99s leisure time to campaign for social reforms. Black women, who largely worked outside the home, came together around urgent needs. One of the first actions of the black Chicago Women\xe2\x80\x99s Club was to raise money to prosecute a police officer who killed a black man. The main distinction between clubs, the black activist Fannie Barrier Williams wrote, was that for black women, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not a fad.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Black women distinguished themselves not only as suffragists but also as vocal critics of a movement that pushed one kind of justice aside in pursuit of another. In 1913, when thousands of suffragists marched on Washington to agitate for the vote, black women were instructed to march in the back. Ida B. Wells defied the order and marched with the delegation from Illinois, her home state. She wasn\xe2\x80\x99t just protesting for her right to vote. She was protesting the protest too.'b'This dynamic is not only a thing of distant history: In the thick of feminism\xe2\x80\x99s second wave, women were often still divided along lines of identity. In 1967, as the best-selling author Betty Friedan called the first meeting of the New York chapter of the National Organization for Women, she found herself at odds with a black activist and lawyer named Flo Kennedy, who pushed the women around her to make common cause with the antiwar and Black Power movements. Friedan and the meeting\xe2\x80\x99s host \xe2\x80\x94 Muriel Fox, the highest-ranking female executive at the world\xe2\x80\x99s largest public-relations agency \xe2\x80\x94 were not pleased. As Kennedy put it in her memoirs, they \xe2\x80\x9cwent bonkers.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Friedan\xe2\x80\x99s 1963 book, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Feminine Mystique,\xe2\x80\x9d had been an awakening for a class of white, married, middle-class women, and she pictured herself as the leader of what she called a \xe2\x80\x9cmainstream\xe2\x80\x9d feminist movement. When women at one 1970 march offered her a lavender armband to wear in solidarity with a NOW member recently attacked for her bisexuality, Friedan dropped it on the ground, furious at the attempt to add gay rights to her program.'b'Kennedy continually pushed in the opposite direction, trying to build bridges between feminist groups and other movements. At one point, Friedan admonished her to leave the feminist movement alone and \xe2\x80\x9cfocus her attention on matters of Black Power.\xe2\x80\x9d As the second wave matured, black women found themselves continually calling on it to consider a new approach, one that acknowledged the different needs of different women. As the black feminist and leftist Barbara Smith told the National Women\xe2\x80\x99s Studies Association in 1979, any feminism that didn\xe2\x80\x99t account for the specific concerns of black women, poor women, disabled women, lesbians and others was not really feminism \xe2\x80\x94 it was \xe2\x80\x9cmerely female self-aggrandizement.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'There has never'b' been one women\xe2\x80\x99s movement. It\xe2\x80\x99s difficult, for example, to say that the American feminist project started in Seneca Falls, N.Y., in 1848, because black women were not invited to that convention. It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to say that electing a woman as president would have been feminism\xe2\x80\x99s crowning achievement, because the success of one woman does not naturally trickle down to all. The history of the women\xe2\x80\x99s movement is one of warring factions and sharp self-criticism. But its 150 years of navigating internal disputes put it in a position to lead what seemed, at the end of last fall, like a highly divided left.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s embarrassing to me now to say it, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know the term \xe2\x80\x98intersectionality\xe2\x80\x99 when we started,\xe2\x80\x9d Bob Bland, the Women\xe2\x80\x99s March co-chairwoman, told me. Now she deployed it often to emphasize the growing diversity of the march. She told various reporters that she had met women working \xe2\x80\x9cin so many different intersectionalities\xe2\x80\x9d and hoped to reach a \xe2\x80\x9ca wide intersectionality of people\xe2\x80\x9d in a march that reflected \xe2\x80\x9call of the different intersections of human rights.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'That magic word comes from a 1989 paper by the legal scholar Kimberl\xc3\xa9 Crenshaw that was published in The University of Chicago Legal Forum. Crenshaw had studied cases in which black women sued their employers for what appeared to be \xe2\x80\x9ccompound discrimination\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 both racial and gender biases. But they were often told they lacked legal standing: Laws protected them from discrimination as African-Americans or as women, but not specifically as black women. Crenshaw used a traffic metaphor to describe the interlocking forms of oppression a person might face. Cars flowed through an intersection in all directions; when an accident happened, it could be caused by cars from any number of sides, or even all sides.'b'That metaphor would be plucked from Crenshaw\xe2\x80\x99s paper and grow in resonance over the next two decades, until \xe2\x80\x9cintersectionality\xe2\x80\x9d became a rallying cry \xe2\x80\x94 the main point of rhetorical resistance against the tide of single-issue feminist conversation. Even beneath the shiny surface of Obama-era pop feminism, dissenters took countless shots at its racial cluelessness, its lack of class-consciousness, its sometimes shallow concerns. Women of color convened on Twitter under hashtags like #SolidarityIsForWhiteWomen to detail their experiences of being sidelined in feminist conversations, and many on the left criticized the way a trickle-down, professional-oriented feminism was becoming popular just as income inequality between women was ballooning. (It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to \xe2\x80\x9clean in\xe2\x80\x9d to a job cleaning hotel rooms.) In recent years, intersectionality even popped up in People.com, on Bustle and in a tweet from Clinton.'b'Often the criticism that lies behind this word is brushed off, met with defensiveness, taken personally. (As the founder of Pantsuit Nation wrote to critics of her book deal: \xe2\x80\x9cThis is not the place for divisiveness.\xe2\x80\x9d) Women turn to feminism because they want to stand up and say something; it can be jarring for them be told to sit down and listen to someone else. But the concept became a useful tool for the march on Washington, which set about the task of uniting feminism\xe2\x80\x99s mainstream, popular arm and its dissenting factions \xe2\x80\x94 all in the space of two months.'b'Soon after the suggestion to march raced across the web, Vanessa Wruble \xe2\x80\x94 a white producer and co-founder of the media company OkayAfrica \xe2\x80\x94 made a pivotal intervention in its planning. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought the stakes were so high,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me. \xe2\x80\x9cIt needed to be an inclusive movement, or it was going to be a total disaster. I felt that it could damage the country.\xe2\x80\x9d At this critical moment, with the march quickly ballooning into something bigger than the initial organizers could handle on their own, Wruble reached out and urged them to drop the name Million Women March. Then she linked them up with her network, and soon three seasoned activists \xe2\x80\x94 Carmen Perez, Linda Sarsour and Tamika Mallory \xe2\x80\x94 got on board. These women hadn\xe2\x80\x99t necessarily supported Clinton, and they didn\xe2\x80\x99t necessarily identify as feminists. But they had experience organizing in communities of color and saw the march as an opportunity to reach a large new audience. When Sarsour got the call, she had just posted a comment on the march\xe2\x80\x99s Facebook page: \xe2\x80\x9cCan you include Muslim women and Muslim communities in the list?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The three women \xe2\x80\x94 one Chicana Latina, one Palestinian-American, one black \xe2\x80\x94 met through their involvement in Justice League NYC, a juvenile-justice initiative. In 2015, they organized a nine-day march from New York to Washington, ending in a rally at the Capitol that drew a small crowd. Now hundreds of thousands of women whose previous interest in justice may have been abstract at best were turning to them for leadership.
