The question, Perez told me, was \xe2\x80\x9cHow do we get them to understand that their liberation is bound with ours?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Meanwhile, the three had some catching up to do with the mainstream feminist perspective. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t have a lot of what I would consider to be deep, transformative relationships with white women,\xe2\x80\x9d Sarsour told me. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been learning a lot,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and working toward becoming \xe2\x80\x9cmore comfortable around this movement of feminism that I always felt didn\xe2\x80\x99t particularly include Muslim women.\xe2\x80\x9d The organizers appeared on the hip-hop radio morning show \xe2\x80\x9cThe Breakfast Club\xe2\x80\x9d and loaded their Instagram page with black feminist heroes. But they also posed for windswept photos in Vogue, and some dropped by the Wing, a private Manhattan women\xe2\x80\x99s club with a $2,250 annual membership fee. Their rally put Angela Davis on the same stage as Scarlett Johansson.'b'When I called Kimberl\xc3\xa9 Crenshaw in January, she had just returned home to Los Angeles from the march on Washington, where she walked with a group of women from the African American Policy Forum. Her group was so far back in the crowd that they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear the rally, and \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m kind of glad about it,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were in this sea of humanity.\xe2\x80\x9d Wading through the crowd, she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI saw all the different issues and people that had found their way under the banner of the Women\xe2\x80\x99s March. It was the embodiment of the intersectional sensibilities that a lot of us have been working on for a very long time.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The women\xe2\x80\x99s movement\xe2\x80\x99s tendency toward a singular perspective is \xe2\x80\x9cnot an exceptional problem for feminism,\xe2\x80\x9d Crenshaw told me. \xe2\x80\x9cPatriarchy works in such a way that these critiques never even surface in a lot of movements led by men. This conversation isn\xe2\x80\x99t always happening in other spaces. And if the conversation leads to more robust ways of thinking about women, feminism and social justice, it can be a very good thing.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe million-dollar question is: Can these feminisms live together under an anti-Trump banner?\xe2\x80\x9d Crenshaw said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt happened for 24 hours all across the world.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'When I made'b' my way back to my hotel after the march, the cheers of the crowd fading into the distance, I opened my laptop and saw a different version of what I had just seen in person. Now it was all filtered through my own social-media bubble \xe2\x80\x94 that of a middle-class white woman who lives in Brooklyn. Facebook\xe2\x80\x99s trending topics, tailored to fulfill each user\xe2\x80\x99s particular online habits, served me up a pop-celebrity version of the day\xe2\x80\x99s events. It pointed me toward the speeches of Scarlett Johansson and Madonna, and nobody else. As scenes of the march traveled through the media and across the web, the story spun out in even more directions. Twitter lit up with notes of internal dissent and snapshots of signs from the march: DON\xe2\x80\x99T FORGET: WHITE WOMEN VOTED FOR TRUMP and BLACK WOMEN TRIED TO SAVE Y\xe2\x80\x99ALL and I\xe2\x80\x99LL SEE YOU NICE WHITE LADIES AT THE NEXT #BLACKLIVESMATTER MARCH, RIGHT?'b'But for the moment, at least, Trump appears to be the great uniter. In the days and weeks since the march, its energy spilled into spontaneous actions across the country, with protesters coming together on behalf of Muslims and immigrants. Donations poured into Planned Parenthood and the American Civil Liberties Union. Congressional switchboards were inundated with calls. When 'b'The Washington Post polled Americans'b' post-march, it registered a huge shift in energy among Democrats, especially Democratic women, 40 percent of whom said they planned to get more involved in activism.'b'But liberals are not the only ones drawing inspiration from the protests. Flip to Fox News, click around conservative blogs or browse pro-Trump Twitter, and you can watch the demonstrations fuel a different kind of opposition narrative. After the march, Fox News set clips of rally speeches to foreboding music. Breitbart published photos with the headline \xe2\x80\x9cSee what a massive, Hillary shaped bullet America just dodged?\xe2\x80\x9d The right-wing Media Research Center aggregated the most \xe2\x80\x9cvile and ridiculous signs.\xe2\x80\x9d Twitter exploded with anti-Muslim attacks on Linda Sarsour, who was called a \xe2\x80\x9cterrorist\xe2\x80\x9d who \xe2\x80\x9cloves ISIS.\xe2\x80\x9d When the annual March for Life hit the Mall to demonstrate against abortion rights, The Blaze called it \xe2\x80\x9cthe real women\xe2\x80\x99s march.\xe2\x80\x9d (The Women\xe2\x80\x99s March did, at one point, remove the name of an anti-abortion group from its list of partners, after an uproar.) 'b'According to Public Policy Polling'b', 48 percent of Trump voters think the protesters who convened at airports to protest the travel ban were paid by George Soros. Trump tweeted recently: \xe2\x80\x9cProfessional anarchists, thugs and paid protesters are proving the point of the millions of people who voted to MAKE AMERICA GREAT AGAIN!\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In the first weeks of the Trump administration, the factions that split over his election are deepening along the same lines. Each side seems oddly confident in its political position. Trump supporters call themselves \xe2\x80\x9cthe silent majority,\xe2\x80\x9d while his critics identify as the \xe2\x80\x9cpopular vote.\xe2\x80\x9d When I called Eleanor Smeal, a co-founder of the Feminist Majority Foundation, and asked her whether the organization had any plans to reach out to the 53 percent of white women who voted for Trump, her response was to question the margin of error in the polls. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t really know if we lost the majority or not, and I believe that we did not,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me. \xe2\x80\x9cI think they\xe2\x80\x99re with us.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'For now, the factions of the left seem to have found an accord. But to regain any power in Washington, they will need to sway the center too \xe2\x80\x94 including some of those women who voted for Trump. The white women of the left, many of whom are just now finding their footing as activists, have been eager to dissociate from that group. Mention the 53 percent, and they\xe2\x80\x99re quick to tell you that they\xe2\x80\x99re of the 47. But of all the people who marched on Washington last month, they may be among the best positioned to reach across that aisle. \xe2\x80\x9cI know of no other time when it would be more important,\xe2\x80\x9d Barbara Smith, the black feminist and leftist, told me. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not my work to do, but somebody ought to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b''b''b''b''b'W'b'hen Indira Islas was in third grade at Centennial Arts Academy, a public elementary school in Gainesville, Ga., she decided it was time to get serious. It was 2006, and she was in the lowest reading group in her class. She had been in that group since arriving two years earlier, speaking no English, in Gainesville, a city of 38,000 just northeast of Atlanta\xe2\x80\x99s booming outer suburbs. But being at the bottom went against everything she believed about herself. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to be with the smart kids,\xe2\x80\x9d she recalls. Starting the year before, in second grade, she read every volume of the \xe2\x80\x9cMagic Tree House\xe2\x80\x9d books in her elementary-school library, a series about two ordinary siblings who climb into their backyard treehouse and time-travel to Pompeii, the Wild West, the ice age, feudal Japan and beyond. \xe2\x80\x9cI absolutely loved them,\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was like going on adventures all over the world.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'It was also the opposite of her own life. Indira left Mexico for the United States at age 6 with her parents and two younger sisters. Her mother cleaned houses when she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t caring for the children \xe2\x80\x94 there would eventually be seven of them \xe2\x80\x94 and her father worked in construction, and there was no money for after-school lessons or soccer clubs, let alone traveling. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d hear about trips and experiences of my white friends, and I remember thinking: I\xe2\x80\x99ll never go to the beach or Disney World for spring break,\xe2\x80\x9d Indira says. Her parents told her that education was all that mattered, and she had to spend all her free time inside, reading and writing. \xe2\x80\x9cI tell my children this country is a blessing to all the people living here,\xe2\x80\x9d her mother told me. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you have the opportunity to be good, it\xe2\x80\x99s very important to take it.\xe2\x80\x9d Indira took this advice to heart. By the time she was in fifth grade, her reading skills had improved so much that she tested into the top reading group. By middle school, she consistently got A\xe2\x80\x99s, which qualified her for a celebratory school trip every time report cards came out. \xe2\x80\x9cThey rewarded us by taking us skating or bowling,\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cI felt like I was so smart, just getting the chance to go out for the whole school day with friends. That\xe2\x80\x99s when I said: \xe2\x80\x98I can make it.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Indira began to throw herself into everything. At recess, she played soccer and basketball, competing so fiercely that everyone took notice. Boys usually picked other boys for their teams, and white kids tended to favor other white kids. But everyone started picking Indira. In middle school, she was on the track team, running long-distance races. Her coach was stunned by her determination. In meets, even when she won her event, she scolded herself unless she broke her previous record. After practices ended, she would keep running. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to think,\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d stay after practice and run and run and run.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Indira remembers understanding vaguely that it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t just poverty that set her and her family apart. Her parents had been doctors in Mexico. She admired pictures in their dresser drawer of the two of them in their 20s standing together, tall and proud in their white coats \xe2\x80\x94 before they all fled the violence of drug gangs who were then taking over their home state, Guerrero. When she asked her parents why they were no longer doctors, they explained it was because they were not American citizens. It didn\xe2\x80\x99t make sense to Indira. Why would her father have shed that beautiful crisp white coat for the fraying pants and shirts he now wore?'b'Soon after Indira turned 13, in 2011, she was riding home from track practice with her mother when another car sideswiped the family\xe2\x80\x99s Ford Expedition. The other driver, who was at fault, insisted on calling the police, according to Indira and a lawyer who assisted the family. Indira pleaded not to involve the police, explaining that her mother did not have a driver\xe2\x80\x99s license because she was not an American citizen. (In Georgia and most other states, undocumented immigrants cannot obtain driver\xe2\x80\x99s licenses.) But the driver said she needed a police report to get insurance to cover the damage to her car.'b'A police officer arrived and asked for Indira\xe2\x80\x99s mother\xe2\x80\x99s license. When she said she did not have one \xe2\x80\x94 a state crime \xe2\x80\x94 she was told to get out of the car. Indira got out, too. She remembers two of her younger siblings sleeping in the back, one in a booster seat, one in a car seat. Two elders from the church they attended arrived to ask for mercy. She has seven children, they told the officer. He responded that he was simply enforcing the law. Indira\xe2\x80\x99s mother turned to her and began to cry. \xe2\x80\x9cIndira, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what is going to happen,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re going to take me.\xe2\x80\x9d Indira remembers remaining strangely calm. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen she was being handcuffed, I said: \xe2\x80\x98Mom, everything is going to be O.K.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Indira\xe2\x80\x99s mother was held in Gainesville\xe2\x80\x99s Hall County jail for three days, but that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the most frightening part for the family. Hall is one of four counties in Georgia that have 'b'a formal agreement'b' to report arrests of undocumented immigrants to the Department of Homeland Security, which means that infractions as minor as a burned-out bulb above a license plate can spiral into deportation proceedings. Indira\xe2\x80\x99s mother says that her charge of driving without a license ultimately led to a referral to immigration court and a deportation order instructing her to leave the country within 30 days. She stayed, slipping into the shadows. Every day since, Indira says, she and her siblings have feared that their mother would be deported. It would take only one more traffic stop.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat woke me up,\xe2\x80\x9d Indira says. \xe2\x80\x9cUntil then, I thought the world was happy.\xe2\x80\x9d In fact, she now realized, it was only American citizens who seemed truly happy. \xe2\x80\x9cIt must feel pretty good, I guess, to not have to worry about whether your family could be taken away any day.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Indira has wanted'b' to be a doctor for almost as long as she can remember. When she was 10, her family was shopping for groceries at Sam\xe2\x80\x99s Club, and she spotted a large book about human anatomy. She became so excited about it that her parents bought it for her birthday, even though it was well above her reading level \xe2\x80\x94 and their price range. She began working her way through it, mesmerized, and when she got stuck, her mother would explain whatever had stumped her.'b'She was determined to go to college and medical school and fulfill her parents\xe2\x80\x99 interrupted dream. In her junior year, Indira began researching college options. She would be a strong applicant. She was consistently at or near the top of her class; she was on the high-school track and soccer teams; she volunteered over 1,000 hours a year at the local hospital, 'b'a record'b' in the history of the teen-volunteer program; and she led her school\xe2\x80\x99s chapter of the Hispanic Organization Promoting Education (HOPE), which encouraged Latino students \xe2\x80\x94 who made up just over half the district\xe2\x80\x99s population \xe2\x80\x94 to stay in school and graduate.'b'She was distressed to discover that Georgia barred undocumented immigrants from attending its top public universities and charged them out-of-state tuition at all others \xe2\x80\x94 triple the rate for citizen residents. She then turned to researching financial aid and learned that Congress barred her from accessing federal Pell grants, loans, scholarships and work-study jobs \xe2\x80\x94 the most common forms of assistance for low-income students. At first, she greeted this as just another set of obstacles to surmount, but as time went on, she began to despair. She would retreat to the classroom of her science teacher, Teresa Leach, who had become her mentor, in need of encouragement. \xe2\x80\x9cThere were a couple of times when I just cried to her because I was tired,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI questioned myself if it was all worth the effort.\xe2\x80\x9d All the while, Indira told me, she held onto her religious conviction that God had a plan, and that she must respect it.'b'At a college fair attended by representatives of numerous Georgia colleges, she asked admissions officers what kind of help was available for undocumented students. No one had any to offer her. She switched her focus to private colleges and was admitted to Atlanta\xe2\x80\x99s Agnes Scott, which she says awarded her $20,000 annually in financial aid, less than half of what she needed. She researched full-ride private scholarships and found two for undocumented students, but she was selected for neither. She was awarded seven small scholarships, which totaled $10,000, enough to go to a nearby public commuter college for only one semester at the out-of-state tuition rate.'b'Last May, Indira attended her graduation ceremony at Gainesville High School, but she had nowhere to go next. In every picture from that day, she wears a wide smile, but she was in pain inside, particularly when she caught a glimpse of her mother in the crowd, looking distraught. Unable to bring herself to celebrate with friends, she went home to be with her family.'b'Days later, a friend told her about a philanthropic organization called TheDream.US, which was offering undocumented students full four-year scholarships to Delaware State University or Eastern Connecticut State University. The application was demanding, and only 76 students would be chosen. She poured herself into the essays, spending hours composing them alongside an English teacher, Cindy Lloyd. She applied to Delaware State, a historically black college in Dover, five hours closer to home than Eastern Connecticut. In late June, she received an email from TheDream.US. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw \xe2\x80\x98Congratulations,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d she remembers, \xe2\x80\x9cand I read no more.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'In late August, Indira made the 11-hour drive with her parents from Gainesville to Delaware State in unusual silence. She was thinking hard about each of her six younger siblings, wondering how they would fare without her. Over breakfast at a Cracker Barrel in South Carolina, when her mother pressed her about how she was feeling, she talked only of her concerns about not being at home to help everyone. When she arrived on campus \xe2\x80\x94 a flat expanse of grassy courtyards and buildings amid strip malls, auto dealers and chain restaurants just beyond Dover\xe2\x80\x99s historic capital area \xe2\x80\x94 she found 33 other \xe2\x80\x9copportunity scholars,\xe2\x80\x9d just as worried and hopeful as she was. All of them were assigned to a dorm about a quarter of a mile from the D.S.U. campus, a former Sheraton hotel acquired a few years earlier by the university as part of an expansion. They bonded instantly, traveling as a posse from classes to the library to the cafeteria, often ending up together late at night in the dorm lobby or in a lounge that had been a large hotel suite on the second floor.'b'In their first month on campus, the opportunity scholars were invited to 'b'a welcoming ceremony'b' in the school\xe2\x80\x99s Martin Luther King Jr. Student Center with Gov. Jack Markell; the Democratic senator Tom Carper; Donald Graham, a founder of TheDream.US; and D.S.U.\xe2\x80\x99s president, Harry Williams. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is not just an opportunity for you; it is an opportunity for the state of Delaware,\xe2\x80\x9d Markell told them. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is sad to see your own home state reject such talent and potential.\xe2\x80\x9d He pronounced himself \xe2\x80\x9cthrilled that you\xe2\x80\x99re here.\xe2\x80\x9d It was the first time many of the students could recall being welcomed anywhere. \xe2\x80\x9cWe felt rejection our whole lives from our own states,\xe2\x80\x9d Indira said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were here only three weeks, and we already met the governor and the senator. It felt like saying \xe2\x80\x98Haha!\xe2\x80\x99 to Georgia.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'Of the 34 'b'opportunity scholars enrolled at D.S.U., 28 are from Mexico and one each is from Ecuador, El Salvador, Peru, Gabon, Gambia and Trinidad and Tobago. Their families are a composite portrait of the economic forces that have drawn undocumented immigrants to the nation\xe2\x80\x99s small towns and metropolitan heartland. Their parents work in poultry plants, on factory lines, in warehouses, on construction sites, in restaurants; they clean and paint houses and schools, tend gardens. \xe2\x80\x9cThey make everything look perfect for the tourists,\xe2\x80\x9d Yulma Lopez, who left Mexico at age 3, said of her parents\xe2\x80\x99 work for a landscaping company in Charleston, S.C. Almost all their parents work illegally, but many pay income taxes, having obtained federal tax-identification numbers. And some, including Indira\xe2\x80\x99s father, have secured temporary federal permission to work and drive lawfully.'b'While most of the students are 18 or 19, typical for college freshmen, some have worked for years in hopes of one day saving enough for college. Olivia Bekale, who is 27 and grew up in Baton Rouge, arrived in Louisiana from Gabon as a child. She graduated from high school in 2008 with a 3.9 G.P.A. from the Louisiana School for Math, Science and the Arts, a prestigious state-supported boarding school for top achievers. Unable to afford college, she cycled from one low-wage position to the next \xe2\x80\x94 server at the Melting Pot, a fondue restaurant; retail sales consultant for Sprint; customer-service agent for Marriott; pharmacy tech for Walgreens. Olivia, who had wanted to be a doctor since an aunt died of AIDS when she was 5, had been out of high school for eight years when she learned of the opportunity scholarship; she applied immediately.'b'All but one of the students were enrolled in the Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals program, also known as DACA. Created in 2012 by an Obama-administration executive action, DACA allowed teenagers and young adults who came to the United States illegally as children with their parents to register with the government and in turn receive a two-year renewable protection against deportation, along with work permits and Social Security numbers. Most of the students, like Indira, signed up at age 15, as soon as they were eligible. With DACA, Indira, who is now 18, was able to get a driver\xe2\x80\x99s license and a job at a Publix supermarket when she was in high school, working 20 hours a week as a cashier and bagger. Being able to work and drive legally, free of the fears her mother faced, and fitting in with her classmates, Indira says, was \xe2\x80\x9cliving the American dream.\xe2\x80\x9d With her income from Publix, she even was able to get braces for her teeth.'b'The starting point for all of their dreams was education, and the quest for it has been central to the experience of undocumented young people since long before Indira and her classmates were born. In the late 1970s, when undocumented immigrants had yet to move in large numbers beyond border states, Texas passed a law authorizing local school districts to ban them from public schools or charge them tuition. In a landmark decision in 'b'Plyler v. Doe'b' in 1982, a narrowly divided Supreme Court struck down the law, finding that undocumented children had a constitutional right to free K-through-12 public education. The opinion blamed a dysfunctional immigration system for creating the crisis by failing to keep out undocumented immigrants or provide them a path to citizenship. \xe2\x80\x9cAlready disadvantaged as a result of poverty, lack of English-speaking ability and undeniable racial prejudices, these children, without an education, will become permanently locked into the lowest socioeconomic class,\xe2\x80\x9d Justice William Brennan wrote for the majority, quoting the district-court opinion. The case also introduced the argument that undocumented children were legally blameless, unlike their parents: \xe2\x80\x9cThe classification at issue deprives a group of children of the opportunity for education afforded all other children simply because they have been assigned a legal status due to a violation of law by their parents,\xe2\x80\x9d Justice Lewis Powell wrote in a concurring opinion.'b'Undocumented children poured into the nation\xe2\x80\x99s schools over the next generation, and as they reached college age, they coalesced into a movement, advocating access to higher education as well as full citizenship. In 2001, they began calling themselves Dreamers, now an estimated 2.1 million young immigrants who have grown up as Americans in almost every way except for their passports. The name came from the Dream Act (Development, Relief and Education for Alien Minors), introduced in Congress in 2001 by Senator Richard Durbin, a Democrat from Illinois, and Senator Orrin Hatch, a Republican from Utah, and for which activists fought for over a decade. The measure, which would have put undocumented children on a path to citizenship, never passed, but the vast network of Dreamers became a compelling political force.'b'In 2001, hundreds of them turned out to testify in Texas in favor of legislation to allow undocumented residents to pay in-state college tuition if they graduated from Texas high schools and lived in the state for three years. \xe2\x80\x9cSomething magical happened when those kids told their stories,\xe2\x80\x9d says the former Texas state representative Rick Noriega, a Democrat who sponsored the bill. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a humanizing of a very real issue dealing with children\xe2\x80\x99s dreams and hopes. Every heart on that committee was touched, Republicans and Democrats.\xe2\x80\x9d The legislation passed both houses almost unanimously and was signed by Rick Perry, then governor of Texas and now President Donald Trump\xe2\x80\x99s pick for energy secretary. Texas became the first state, followed quickly by California, to allow Dreamers to pay in-state tuition. Today, 21 states charge Dreamers the same tuition as legal residents, including six carried by Trump \xe2\x80\x94 Florida, Kansas, Nebraska, Oklahoma, Utah and Texas.'b'In many of those states, however, the once-bipartisan issue has turned politically treacherous. In Texas, efforts to repeal the tuition law come closer to passing every year, and Noriega says there is no chance the original measure would pass today. The leading national opponents of in-state tuition for Dreamers include the Republican senator Jeff Sessions, Trump\xe2\x80\x99s choice for attorney general, and the secretary of state of Kansas, Kris Kobach, a Republican who was a leader of Trump\xe2\x80\x99s transition team on immigration. Each argues that students who are in the United States illegally should not get a public benefit in any state that is denied to a citizen from another state. In other words, if Dreamers pay in-state tuition in Texas, citizen students next door in Arkansas and Oklahoma \xe2\x80\x94 or Massachusetts, for that matter \xe2\x80\x94 should have the same right. \xe2\x80\x9cHow much sense does that make, to have people here illegally, and they have more benefits than those who are here legally?\xe2\x80\x9d Sessions asked in a Senate floor statement. Kobach used the same argument to bring class-action lawsuits against in-state tuition for Dreamers in Kansas and California. Judges found no legal basis for the claims and dismissed the cases.'b'The larger debate over how to treat an estimated 11 million immigrants who came here illegally has been at a stalemate for decades, with advocates seeking a \xe2\x80\x9cpath to citizenship\xe2\x80\x9d for law-abiding families who have been in the country for years and opponents denouncing \xe2\x80\x9camnesty\xe2\x80\x9d for people who broke the law to enter the country. Amid hardening resistance in Congress to immigration reform, Dreamers brought pressure on Obama \xe2\x80\x94 including sit-ins and hunger strikes at his 2012 campaign offices \xe2\x80\x94 to use his executive power to create DACA. The program, announced on June 15, 2012, the 30th anniversary of the Supreme Court\xe2\x80\x99s Plyler decision, proved transformative for Dreamers. They have entered college, taken on-the-books jobs, received driver\xe2\x80\x99s licenses, bought cars. They now fly on planes, passing effortlessly through airport security. They still lack legal immigration status, but no longer are they exactly undocumented. \xe2\x80\x9cDACAmented,\xe2\x80\x9d many have called themselves.'b'Even in states where they pay in-state tuition, Dreamers still struggle to afford college because they are disproportionately low-income and have no access to federal financial aid. Fewer than 10 percent of Dreamers who graduate from high school enroll in college. At a time when college graduates earn 70 percent more than those without degrees, these numbers conjure the 1982 warning by the Supreme Court that undocumented children could become a permanent underclass.'b'In response, a handful of philanthropies have adopted the cause of sending students with DACA status to college. The biggest of these, TheDream.US, has raised $90 million to eventually finance 4,000 students at public colleges, with significant contributions from Donald Graham, former publisher and chief executive of The Washington Post, and his family; Mark Zuckerberg and Priscilla Chan; Bill and Melinda Gates; the hedge-fund executive William Ackman; and Michael Bloomberg, among others. (I was a reporter at The Washington Post from 1980 to 2008.) In 2014, TheDream.US began offering Dreamers full scholarships in states that charge them in-state tuition. Last year, in partnership with Delaware and Connecticut, the organization created the opportunity-scholarship program for students from the other 29 states, financed by $41 million in philanthropy from Graham and his family and two anonymous donors. The governors of Delaware and Connecticut agreed to charge roughly in-state tuition rates for the 34 scholars at Delaware State and the 42 at Eastern Connecticut State \xe2\x80\x94 a total of $80,000 per student for tuition, room and board for four years. In an effort to pre-empt political opposition, Graham says, the philanthropy works only with schools, like Delaware State and Eastern Connecticut State, that have excess capacity, so that undocumented students are not displacing citizens. And private donors pay all expenses, so that no state dollars are spent.'b'Still, when The Delaware State News ran an article in September about the D.S.U. opportunity scholars, the online comments complained that undocumented immigrants, not citizens, were benefiting. \xe2\x80\x9cTrump isn\xe2\x80\x99t perfect, but I will vote for him because he puts Americans FIRST,\xe2\x80\x9d wrote a reader named John Huff of Magnolia, Del. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are plenty of kids who are citizens who have the same dream and should come first.\xe2\x80\x9d And as news of the scholarship spread on the Delaware campus this fall, a number of African-American students told Dreamers that they resented that their own families had to go into debt for a portion of their education costs while the DACA students got full scholarships.'b'By then, Trump had mobilized anti-immigrant anger in large swaths of the country, having kicked off his campaign criticizing Mexican immigrants \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re bringing drugs. They\xe2\x80\x99re bringing crime. They\xe2\x80\x99re rapists\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 and vowing to build a wall on the border to keep them out. In stump speeches, he promised to deport all 11 million undocumented immigrants and, in his first 100 days in office, to terminate DACA, labeling it \xe2\x80\x9cillegal amnesty.\xe2\x80\x9d Both vows became instant applause lines.'b''b'Indira declared her'b' major in biological sciences at the beginning of the semester and started a demanding pre-med curriculum with six classes, including biology and chemistry, both requiring labs. Her parents had insisted she not take a job, in order to devote herself to education, freeing up four hours a night that she had spent working in high school. With that extra time, she found the academic challenges manageable. Much harder was living apart from her close-knit family for the first time in her life.'b'Her mother texted her daily. \xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, 'b'hija'b',\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote one recent morning, using the Spanish word for daughter. \xe2\x80\x9cMay God bless you today in school. Please be kind to everyone.\xe2\x80\x9d That night, over FaceTime, Indira talked with two of her younger sisters, who like her were born in Mexico and are undocumented. One, a junior in high school, is already on a quest for college scholarships. She and Indira came up with potential essay ideas and discussed her r\xc3\xa9sum\xc3\xa9. Then Indira helped the other, a freshman who is the smartest of all the family\xe2\x80\x99s children, Indira says, with physics homework. On weekends, she FaceTimes with her four youngest siblings \xe2\x80\x94 a sixth grader, a fourth grader, a third grader and a first grader \xe2\x80\x94 all of whom were born in Georgia and are citizens.'b'Separation from family, from home, even from Mexican food made most of the opportunity scholars profoundly lonely. Estephany Martinez, a petite criminal-justice major with long black hair, couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stop thinking about her sisters in the first weeks. \xe2\x80\x9cWhenever we came home from school, all four sisters would sit in the living room and do our homework and talk and watch TV,\xe2\x80\x9d she recalled wistfully of her life in Winder, Ga. In Delaware, \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t have anybody that cared for me. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t have anyone to come home to.\xe2\x80\x9d In early September, she summoned all the scholars to a gathering in the dorm\xe2\x80\x99s second-floor lounge. \xe2\x80\x9cAll right, you guys, we\xe2\x80\x99re going to be here for each other,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat part of our lives \xe2\x80\x94 being undocumented \xe2\x80\x94 is critical to who we are. We have to share our stories.\xe2\x80\x9d Everyone crowded in, sitting on the sofa, spilling onto the floor, sitting shoulder to shoulder on counters that once were part of a kitchenette, sprawling into one another\xe2\x80\x99s space.'b'Carlos Gonzales of Manteo, N.C., a lanky and cheerful marketing major whose mother is a restaurant cook, broke down crying when he recalled the violence that drove his family from Mexico City. He, his mother and his younger sister moved to North Carolina when he was 7. It was his mother who encouraged him, beginning in elementary school, to reach for college. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to live the life we\xe2\x80\x99re living now,\xe2\x80\x9d she told him. In high school, he was an honor student and varsity wrestler and runner, working nights and weekends at McDonald\xe2\x80\x99s in his Outer Banks town. When he received the email telling him he was an opportunity scholar, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cI hugged my mom and cried for two hours. The only reason I stopped was I had to go to work.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Indira told the harrowing story that led to her own family\xe2\x80\x99s departure from Mexico. In 2004, when she was 6, three masked gunmen broke into their home, which housed her parents\xe2\x80\x99 clinic, and robbed them of everything \xe2\x80\x94 money, jewelry, a new computer, a television, cameras and medications. They filed a report with the police, they said, who didn\xe2\x80\x99t investigate, in deference to cartels then taking over Guerrero, now the most violent state in Mexico. An uncle of her father\xe2\x80\x99s already had been killed. In subsequent years, a cousin of her mother\xe2\x80\x99s, a veterinarian, was kidnapped and never found. Two nephews disappeared. Her mother\xe2\x80\x99s sister has been kidnapped twice \xe2\x80\x94 most recently this past November \xe2\x80\x94 and returned only after her family paid steep ransoms.'b'Weeks after the robbery, Indira\xe2\x80\x99s family of five arrived in the United States on a tourist visa that her father procured a month earlier in hopes of taking everyone to Disney World. Instead, they went to Gainesville, where her father\xe2\x80\x99s brother worked in construction \xe2\x80\x94 one of thousands of Mexican workers who flocked to the north Georgia community in the last 25 years, swelling the Hispanic population to more than 40 percent in 2013 from 8 percent in 1990. Indira said her parents are certain they would have been killed had they stayed. They decided to forfeit their careers for their family\xe2\x80\x99s safety. \xe2\x80\x9cI no longer saw my future, but I saw my children\xe2\x80\x99s future,\xe2\x80\x9d her father said to me.'b'Antonio Patino, a computer-science major who is a lifeguard and plays bass and guitar in his spare time, told the group about riding with his family in their car in 2015 in Lawrenceville, Ga., when a police officer pulled them over, though none of them understood why. His father, who is undocumented and processes returns for a local manufacturer, was driving but did not have a license. Antonio and his mother, younger sister and brother all watched in terror as his father was handcuffed, placed in a police cruiser and driven off to jail. As it turned out, he was released the next day after paying a fine of more than $800 and was not referred to immigration court for further proceedings, but the incident shook Antonio\xe2\x80\x99s sense of belonging in America. \xe2\x80\x9cI felt like I got slapped in the face just for living, trying to be a normal person in this beautiful country,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt feels like a hole inside me.\xe2\x80\x9d He said he now found himself gripped with fears for his parents\xe2\x80\x99 safety at random moments during his days at D.S.U. It is as if he has swapped roles with his mother and father. \xe2\x80\x9cLike I\xe2\x80\x99m now the parent and they\xe2\x80\x99re the child, and I\xe2\x80\x99m worried for them,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me. \xe2\x80\x9cNot being there, all these what-ifs swarm into my mind. What if out of nowhere they get pulled over again?\xe2\x80\x9d Calling them and hearing their voices usually comforts him. But after one such call, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cI went outside, and I had to cry a little. I was feeling like I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help them.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'A number of students shared the enormous sacrifices they had seen their parents make for them. Juan Chavez, 23, who grew up in Plymouth, Ind., and worked for five years after high school, told of his mother suffering a breakdown after her divorce from his father. He saw it as a response to the crushing instability of their lives, moving from one home to another in search of shelter. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s the strongest person I\xe2\x80\x99ll ever know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s my role model. My father figure as well as my mother.\xe2\x80\x9d He continued: \xe2\x80\x9cI felt so helpless to make things better. I decided almost right then I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to college and medical school if it takes me the rest of my life.\xe2\x80\x9d He is now a psychology and pre-med major, intending to become a psychiatrist.'b'On and on the students went until almost 3 a.m., the common threads in their stories drawing them closer. It was the first time most of them had talked openly about being undocumented, but instead of feeling exposed, they felt safer. Until then, Antonio had gone out of his way to avoid conversations with non-Dreamers at D.S.U. about his scholarship, not wanting to have to explain that he got it because he was undocumented. The next day, though, he fell into conversation with a student who asked him how he happened to come all the way from Georgia to D.S.U., and he said without hesitation: \xe2\x80\x9cI got a scholarship.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhat for?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m undocumented,\xe2\x80\x9d Antonio said, surprised at how comfortable this felt.'b'\xe2\x80\x9cO.K., man, that\xe2\x80\x99s cool,\xe2\x80\x9d the student said.'b'After their long night talking, the scholars also better understood what had propelled them all for as long as they could remember. Throughout high school, the opportunity scholars watched undocumented friends and siblings give up and drop out, shamed and beaten down by public scorn over illegal immigration and the dead-end options awaiting even those who excelled in high school. But they kept on striving, steeled to the insults, positioning themselves for a breakthrough they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t yet see. Now this all made sense. \xe2\x80\x9cThis pain \xe2\x80\x94 it pushes us,\xe2\x80\x9d Estephany said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s motivation. It has made me who I am. It makes me go through every day.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cNow we know what drives us,\xe2\x80\x9d Indira said.'b''b'One morning in'b' mid-October at 9:50 a.m., 10 minutes before Indira\xe2\x80\x99s analytic-chemistry class was scheduled to start, she and two other opportunity scholars were already ensconced in the three center front-row seats, notebooks, pens and textbooks at the ready. Indira was wearing a Harvard sweatshirt that a classmate bought for her when their high-school A.P.-history class visited Boston. (Indira couldn\xe2\x80\x99t afford to go.) \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to get there one day,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a confident smile. Most of the other students didn\xe2\x80\x99t arrive until class was about to begin \xe2\x80\x94 or later \xe2\x80\x94 and there was little competition for the front rows.'b'A similar scene unfolded that morning in the ultramodern science center, in computational-thinking class, where Antonio and Jose Reyes Rios, another computer-science major, sat front and center with an African-American classmate named Hanqaamo Lintisio, who is from Maryland and has a track scholarship. The three had formed a study group and tutored one another so effectively that they all scored above 100 on the midterm. (They nailed the bonus question.) Theirs were the only A\xe2\x80\x99s in the class.'b'The Dreamers gather daily at a long Formica table in a D.S.U. cafeteria for food and conversation. At lunch, Carla Moreno propped her English composition textbook, \xe2\x80\x9cPatterns for College Writing,\xe2\x80\x9d against a napkin holder, securing it with an apple. She paged through a chapter while eating her salad and chili dog. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just a quiz,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I want to keep my A.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'\xe2\x80\x9cI deal with a lot of students, and I feel like the Dreamers are at a different level,\xe2\x80\x9d says Kevin Noriega, the adviser for their scholarships. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re saying, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to make this happen because it\xe2\x80\x99s my only option.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d Of the 488 scholars funded by TheDream.US who began college in 2014, 94 percent remain enrolled after their sophomore year; research shows that only 66 percent of low-income college students nationally return after one year. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a low-income population with retention rates like Harvard\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d Donald Graham says. Because beneficiaries of TheDream.US have full rides, they avoid a common problem faced by other disadvantaged students: running short of money for costs not covered by Pell grants or other forms of aid.'b'One night in October before a biology exam, Indira went to the D.S.U. athletic center for a workout to relieve stress. She was armed with a stack of homemade flash cards and her iPhone, on which she had downloaded discussions of test topics from various websites. While pounding out three miles on the treadmill, she flipped through her study cards, then plugged in her earbuds for a YouTube lecture on glycolysis. Returning to her dorm, she sat down at her desk for a final review. Hanging on the wall in front of her was a collage of family photographs. One showed the whole family of nine around their dinner table. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I\xe2\x80\x99m struggling, I look at a picture of my mom or dad, and I say, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m working for that person,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d she said. That night, her studying complete, she took a last look at her parents\xe2\x80\x99 picture and fell asleep listening to recordings of her biology professor\xe2\x80\x99s lectures. She got an A on the exam.'b'Indira\xe2\x80\x99s determination to become a doctor requires more than a little imagination, because under current law in Georgia and many other states, licenses to practice medicine are reserved for citizens and legal residents. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not planning my life based on the way things are today,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m thinking of the future.\xe2\x80\x9d In her eyes, America is a land of ever-unfolding opportunity. Over Columbus Day weekend, she visited Washington for the first time with her roommate, Karen Baltazar, who is from Lawrenceville, Ga., and also wants to be a doctor. At the Franklin Delano Roosevelt Memorial, Indira stood underneath 'b'Roosevelt\xe2\x80\x99s Four Freedoms'b' \xe2\x80\x94 freedom of speech, freedom of worship, freedom from want, freedom from fear \xe2\x80\x94 her arms raised high above her head, as if exulting in the message. Her smile was radiant. Karen snapped a picture, and Indira posted it on her Facebook page along with the caption, \xe2\x80\x9cI have never forgotten the reasons my parents brought me to this great country.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Such unsinkable optimism, which I heard from many D.S.U. Dreamers in the early fall, is hard to maintain, and many of the estimated 65,000 undocumented immigrants who graduate from high school every year \xe2\x80\x94 and thousands who drop out \xe2\x80\x94 simply can\xe2\x80\x99t muster it. In a recent book, \xe2\x80\x9cLives in Limbo,\xe2\x80\x9d Roberto G. Gonzales, an assistant professor at Harvard\xe2\x80\x99s graduate school of education, writes that academically successful Dreamers are far outnumbered by those who become casualties of \xe2\x80\x9cthe deeper and more far-reaching consequences of being undocumented: living in poverty, having parents and family members who also bear the burdens of being undocumented, watching friends moving forward but being unable to join them, watching opportunities pass you by, navigating a world of exclusions while constantly looking over your shoulder.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'At least some of the opportunity scholars\xe2\x80\x99 optimism last fall came from their assumption, based on reported polls, that Hillary Clinton would be the next president. They were heartened by her speeches about keeping families together and pursuing comprehensive immigration reform. At the very least, they were confident that DACA would remain in effect under her leadership. Though they didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe Trump could win, he unnerved them with his speeches branding Mexicans criminals who stole Americans\xe2\x80\x99 jobs and lived off their tax dollars. They took this rhetoric as a personal affront and were horrified by the hatred they saw on the faces of those who cheered his words. \xe2\x80\x9cThe only thing that depresses me is so many people support him,\xe2\x80\x9d Jose Reyes Rios, the computer-science major, who aspires to work at Google one day, said in late September.'b'On election night, a group of Dreamers gathered around the flat-screen TV in the dorm lobby, many of them studying as they watched returns. Indira had exams the next day in math and biology and arrived equipped with her flash cards. The mood turned dark after Trump won Florida. Everyone\xe2\x80\x99s mind flooded with his vows that they had discounted until now \xe2\x80\x94 to revoke DACA, to deputize local police departments to enforce federal immigration laws. \xe2\x80\x9cWe will issue detainers for all illegal immigrants who are arrested for any crime whatsoever, and they will be placed into immediate removal proceedings,\xe2\x80\x9d Trump said last summer in Arizona. As all of them knew well, these crimes included one of their parents\xe2\x80\x99 daily activities \xe2\x80\x94 driving without a license. If DACA disappeared, and with it their Social Security numbers, their own driver\xe2\x80\x99s licenses would be worthless. So would their federal work authorization, meaning most would lose the jobs that paid for their books, phones, clothes, travel home and anything else they needed.'b'By 1 a.m., Indira became too distressed to concentrate. Olivia Bekale was one of four opportunity scholars who watched until Trump was declared the president-elect. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were just looking at each other and crying,\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cWe hugged each other and went to our rooms.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'On the Sunday'b' after the election, when they gathered in the dorm lobby, the opportunity scholars were struggling with something unfamiliar: despair. They had registered for DACA with the Department of Homeland Security, which now knew exactly where they were. \xe2\x80\x9cLife is going to be like it is for our parents,\xe2\x80\x9d said Victor Hernandez of Coats, N.C., stunned and shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThey could come pick us up and take us away any time.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Social media brought aftershocks for all of them as they discovered that many of their best friends voted for Trump. Almost all of Antonio\xe2\x80\x99s high-school swim teammates in Lawrenceville had Trump filters on their Facebook and Snapchat profile pictures. \xe2\x80\x9cAll I could think was: You voted against me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I do to you?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The day after the election, Indira couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to call her mother until the afternoon. Instead, she confided her fears in text messages to her former science teacher, Teresa Leach. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never been so disappointed in this country,\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never felt so powerless. ... I\xe2\x80\x99m scared about my family, my mom. ... Not sure if I\xe2\x80\x99ll even get my degree, much less go to medical school here.\xe2\x80\x9d When Indira finally did call home, her mother insisted she was not afraid and told Indira that education was now even more important.'b'Several of Indira\xe2\x80\x99s friends back home told her they doubted Trump would follow through on all his campaign promises, and in any case, they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t imagine he would target her. \xe2\x80\x9cThese are people who helped me get to where I am, who remind me they love me, and I love them,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut they\xe2\x80\x99ll never understand what we feel. They say, \xe2\x80\x98He won\xe2\x80\x99t do that.\xe2\x80\x99 Do they think he\xe2\x80\x99s going to send back all the immigrants except Indira?\xe2\x80\x9d'b'A lifeline of sorts arrived the week after the election, when the students received letters from TheDream.US, Governor Markell and the president of D.S.U., pledging to stand behind their scholarships no matter what became of DACA. Donald Graham lined up attorneys to represent them if anyone challenged their right to be in school.'b'But their bigger fears were for their parents. Antonio and Indira went home for Christmas to find their families filled with trepidation. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re trying to be invisible, trying to stay hidden,\xe2\x80\x9d Antonio\xe2\x80\x99s father said, \xe2\x80\x9cdo only the things we have to do to live, like go to work, go buy groceries.\xe2\x80\x9d During the 2015 holiday season, they took a driving tour of the Christmas lights and celebrated New Year\xe2\x80\x99s Eve in downtown Lawrenceville. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we can\xe2\x80\x99t go to any festivities because for us it\xe2\x80\x99s very dangerous,\xe2\x80\x9d his mother told me in their apartment in a complex in Buford, Ga. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are a lot of police there.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Antonio\xe2\x80\x99s father said he worries about driving his daughter, Litzy, 15, home from her swim-team practice at rush hour, when the police presence increases. Litzy was born in Georgia, and he thought of asking her to leave the team until she is old enough to drive because, as a citizen, she will be able to get a driver\xe2\x80\x99s license. \xe2\x80\x9cBut then I said to myself: \xe2\x80\x98No! That\xe2\x80\x99s crazy. That\xe2\x80\x99s why we\xe2\x80\x99re here \xe2\x80\x94 for our kids. So they can take advantage of every opportunity.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d He continues to ferry her \xe2\x80\x94 carefully \xe2\x80\x94 during rush hour.'b'Indira found her mother more frightened than at any time since receiving her deportation order. Her mother says she has no choice but to drive when someone at her church asks for help, when her children call for rides home and when she is needed at a charity medical clinic where she volunteers as a doctor\xe2\x80\x99s assistant and translator. \xe2\x80\x9cLife changed after the election,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me in December, in her living room decorated with framed academic awards for each child. She was surrounded by her kids, who listened carefully. \xe2\x80\x9cThe children know I don\xe2\x80\x99t have a driver\xe2\x80\x99s license. They know at any time maybe the police will take me again. \xe2\x80\x98If I can\xe2\x80\x99t come back,\xe2\x80\x99 I tell them, \xe2\x80\x98you have to go to school every day. You have to study hard \xe2\x80\x94 even harder.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b'By the time'b' Indira and the other opportunity scholars returned to campus in January, all of them had come to the same conclusion: There was now only one thing they could control \xe2\x80\x94 their education. \xe2\x80\x9cThe only way we can fight back is to excel in school,\xe2\x80\x9d Indira wrote to me in a text message. She felt weary in the aftermath of the election, but when she had this epiphany, she said: \xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t tired anymore. I had that drive, that hunger to just come out on top. I was angry. I was staying at the library longer, going to the gym a lot more.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Estephany Martinez focused on her life\xe2\x80\x99s mission. \xe2\x80\x9cMy goal is being a police chief \xe2\x80\x94 something that makes my voice louder,\xe2\x80\x9d said the criminal-justice major. \xe2\x80\x9cI have to educate myself to get there. I want to show people who don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in us: \xe2\x80\x98I got all the way here. I\xe2\x80\x99m starting from the bottom with education, and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to get there.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d'b'As they settled into the semester, they monitored everything Trump and his close allies were saying about immigration, their moods shifting with each utterance. Back in December, they felt hopeful when Trump, in his interview as Time Magazine\xe2\x80\x99s Person of the Year, said approving things about Dreamers and indicated that he would deport serious criminals before other undocumented immigrants. They were discouraged in January when Jeff Sessions, in his confirmation hearing to become attorney general, appeared unconcerned about the consequences of revoking DACA. They took heart two days later when Paul Ryan, the speaker of the House, said in a CNN town-hall meeting that he wanted Congress to pass legislation making Dreamers \xe2\x80\x9cright with the law.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Indira and other Georgia scholars were exhilarated in early January when 'b'a state court ordered'b' the Board of Regents to allow DACA holders to pay in-state tuition. But their hopes collapsed when a state appeals court 'b'stayed the decision'b'; Trump appeared likely to revoke DACA before Georgia Dreamers could reap the benefits. \xe2\x80\x9cI said: \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99ll happen next, but we\xe2\x80\x99re here in this place, and the only way we can win is to succeed in our education,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x89\xe2\x80\x9d Indira told me.'b'Soon after classes started, the opportunity scholars learned that their collective grade point average for the first semester was a 3.76. Six of the 34 students had achieved a perfect 4.0. Indira was one of them. \xe2\x80\x9cSucceeding for me is how I can get my revenge,\xe2\x80\x9d she told me over the phone, interrupting her biology homework for a few minutes. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to break the stereotype of us being here taking jobs away and not helping the economy. I want Trump to see we\xe2\x80\x99re the total opposite of what he thinks.\xe2\x80\x9d'b''b''b''b''b'Over the past two years of writing this column about immigrants and their food, I\xe2\x80\x99ve cooked with a Filipina nurse who used scalpels to debone chicken; a Senegalese family that eats out of the same dish to emphasize everyone\xe2\x80\x99s responsibility to one another; a Mexican Popsicle maker who tried to heal the ache of her divorce by sharing literal sweetness. There was a Slovakian pierogi master whose last act before leaving for the United States was opening the barn doors to set her animals free; a Polish vegetarian who learned to make 'b'bigos'b', a meatfest of a stew, just so she could share her mother\xe2\x80\x99s recipe; and a Palestinian family that lives the American dream by hosting a Thanksgiving every night.'b'As a writer, I wrote this column feeling honored to be entrusted with their stories. And as the child of Chinese immigrants, I wrote this column looking up to my subjects \xe2\x80\x94 as I do to my own parents \xe2\x80\x94 for carrying the burden of living between two worlds. For finding their footing while having to bridge where they\xe2\x80\x99re from with where they are.'b'I\xe2\x80\x99ve been thinking about this because this is my last column for the magazine \xe2\x80\x94 another dream project beckons, which I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll hear more about soon \xe2\x80\x94 but also because of tomatoes and eggs.'b'A few weeks ago, I felt a sudden, irresistible craving for Chinese stir-fried tomatoes and eggs. A dish of savory, sweet-tart tomato sauce folded around soft-scrambled eggs, it hits every pleasure center in the brain and makes it easy to scarf down a lot of rice, fast. When I worked in Chinatown, it was a staple of every $4 buffet in the neighborhood. A version of it with beef was my younger brother\xe2\x80\x99s favorite food when we were growing up, and by \xe2\x80\x9cfavorite food,\xe2\x80\x9d I mean it was basically the only thing he would eat for the first eight years of his life. (So much so that once, for some reason clear only to jerk older brothers, I squirted ketchup into his apple juice to make fun of him. We fought, he won, I drank the juice.)'b'It\xe2\x80\x99s the kind of dish that people say is the first thing they learned to cook, that fed them when they left home, that inspires sudden and irresistible cravings. But when my hunger struck, I had no idea how to make it. I looked in my Chinese cookbooks, but it appeared in exactly none of them. Calling up my mother to ask her, I knew, would be like asking her to describe how to tie shoelaces: almost impossible to articulate, buried so deep in her muscle memory. In Chinese cooking, this dish is like air, present and invisible.'b'I knew that I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to figure out a recipe for it, because I realized that my not knowing how to make this dish was akin to my Cantonese getting rusty, to not knowing when Chinese New Year is every year. It\xe2\x80\x99s because I\xe2\x80\x99m not an immigrant, only a son of immigrants, and so I know only the frayed facsimile of the world that my parents grew up in. Being part of a culture without living in it is like being in a long-distance relationship. You can make it work with grand displays of affection and splendid visits, but you don\xe2\x80\x99t get to have coffee together on a Sunday morning \xe2\x80\x94 the little things, the stuff daily life is built on. I knew that if I were to have this recipe, it would have to come to me through my people or not at all.'b'So I went online and found recipe after recipe, with an eye toward cobbling together my own. I read the cookbook author Genevieve Ko\xe2\x80\x99s version and took from it the idea of just lightly cooking the eggs before finishing them in the tomatoes. I read Chichi Wang\xe2\x80\x99s version, on Serious Eats, and lifted her brilliant use of fragrant rice wine in the eggs and ketchup in the sauce. I read dozens of blog posts, mostly relating the same story over and over again \xe2\x80\x94 a story of nostalgia, of Mom\xe2\x80\x99s cooking, of home. I read the comments, also telling the same: Thank you, thank you, I\xe2\x80\x99ve missed this dish, thank you, thank you. And after all this reading, I started to realize what I was really seeing: people, just like me, missing a knowledge that they felt should be in their bones, coming to someone else\xe2\x80\x99s recipes to connect them to where they came from while being rooted in where they are.'b''b'Recipe: 'b''b'Chinese Stir-Fried Tomatoes and Eggs'b''b''b''b''b''b'Late one Friday night in early November, Jun Rekimoto, a distinguished professor of human-computer interaction at the University of Tokyo, was online preparing for a lecture when he began to notice some peculiar posts rolling in on social media. Apparently Google Translate, the company\xe2\x80\x99s popular machine-translation service, had suddenly and almost immeasurably improved. Rekimoto visited Translate himself and began to experiment with it. He was astonished. He had to go to sleep, but Translate refused to relax its grip on his imagination.'b'Rekimoto wrote up his initial findings in a blog post. First, he compared a few sentences from two published versions of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Great Gatsby,\xe2\x80\x9d Takashi Nozaki\xe2\x80\x99s 1957 translation and Haruki Murakami\xe2\x80\x99s more recent iteration, with what this new Google Translate was able to produce. Murakami\xe2\x80\x99s translation is written \xe2\x80\x9cin very polished Japanese,\xe2\x80\x9d Rekimoto explained to me later via email, but the prose is distinctively \xe2\x80\x9cMurakami-style.\xe2\x80\x9d By contrast, Google\xe2\x80\x99s translation \xe2\x80\x94 despite some \xe2\x80\x9csmall unnaturalness\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 reads to him as \xe2\x80\x9cmore transparent.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'The second half of Rekimoto\xe2\x80\x99s post examined the service in the other direction, from Japanese to English. He dashed off his own Japanese interpretation of the opening to Hemingway\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cThe Snows of Kilimanjaro,\xe2\x80\x9d then ran that passage back through Google into English. He published this version alongside Hemingway\xe2\x80\x99s original, and proceeded to invite his readers to guess which was the work of a machine.'b'NO. 1:'b'NO. 2:'b'Even to a native English speaker, the missing article on the leopard is the only real giveaway that No. 2 was the output of an automaton. Their closeness was a source of wonder to Rekimoto, who was well acquainted with the capabilities of the previous service. Only 24 hours earlier, Google would have translated the same Japanese passage as follows:'b'Rekimoto promoted his discovery to his hundred thousand or so followers on Twitter, and over the next few hours thousands of people broadcast their own experiments with the machine-translation service. Some were successful, others meant mostly for comic effect. As dawn broke over Tokyo, Google Translate was the No. 1 trend on Japanese Twitter, just above some cult anime series and the long-awaited new single from a girl-idol supergroup. Everybody wondered: How had Google Translate become so uncannily artful?'b''b'Four days later,'b' a couple of hundred journalists, entrepreneurs and advertisers from all over the world gathered in Google\xe2\x80\x99s London engineering office for a special announcement. Guests were greeted with Translate-branded fortune cookies. Their paper slips had a foreign phrase on one side \xe2\x80\x94 mine was in Norwegian \xe2\x80\x94 and on the other, an invitation to download the Translate app. Tables were set with trays of doughnuts and smoothies, each labeled with a placard that advertised its flavor in German ('b'zitrone'b'), Portuguese ('b'baunilha'b') or Spanish ('b'manzana'b'). After a while, everyone was ushered into a plush, dark theater.'b'Sadiq Khan, the mayor of London, stood to make a few opening remarks. A friend, he began, had recently told him he reminded him of Google. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, because I know all the answers?\xe2\x80\x9d the mayor asked. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d the friend replied, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause you\xe2\x80\x99re always trying to finish my sentences.\xe2\x80\x9d The crowd tittered politely. Khan concluded by introducing Google\xe2\x80\x99s chief executive, Sundar Pichai, who took the stage.'b'Pichai was in London in part to inaugurate Google\xe2\x80\x99s new building there, the cornerstone of a new \xe2\x80\x9cknowledge quarter\xe2\x80\x9d under construction at King\xe2\x80\x99s Cross, and in part to unveil the completion of the initial phase of a company transformation he announced last year. The Google of the future, Pichai had said on several occasions, was going to be \xe2\x80\x9cA.I. first.\xe2\x80\x9d What that meant in theory was complicated and had welcomed much speculation. What it meant in practice, with any luck, was that soon the company\xe2\x80\x99s products would no longer represent the fruits of traditional computer programming, exactly, but \xe2\x80\x9cmachine learning.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'A rarefied department within the company, Google Brain, was founded five years ago on this very principle: that artificial \xe2\x80\x9cneural networks\xe2\x80\x9d that acquaint themselves with the world via trial and error, as toddlers do, might in turn develop something like human flexibility. This notion is not new \xe2\x80\x94 a version of it dates to the earliest stages of modern computing, in the 1940s \xe2\x80\x94 but for much of its history most computer scientists saw it as vaguely disreputable, even mystical. Since 2011, though, Google Brain has demonstrated that this approach to artificial intelligence could solve many problems that confounded decades of conventional efforts. Speech recognition didn\xe2\x80\x99t work very well until Brain undertook an effort to revamp it; the application of machine learning made its performance on Google\xe2\x80\x99s mobile platform, Android, almost as good as human transcription. The same was true of image recognition. Less than a year ago, Brain for the first time commenced with the gut renovation of an entire consumer product, and its momentous results were being celebrated tonight.'b'Translate made its debut in 2006 and since then has become one of Google\xe2\x80\x99s most reliable and popular assets; it serves more than 500 million monthly users in need of 140 billion words per day in a different language. It exists not only as its own stand-alone app but also as an integrated feature within Gmail, Chrome and many other Google offerings, where we take it as a push-button given \xe2\x80\x94 a frictionless, natural part of our digital commerce. It was only with the refugee crisis, Pichai explained from the lectern, that the company came to reckon with Translate\xe2\x80\x99s geopolitical importance: On the screen behind him appeared a graph whose steep curve indicated a recent fivefold increase in translations between Arabic and German. (It was also close to Pichai\xe2\x80\x99s own heart. He grew up in India, a land divided by dozens of languages.) The team had been steadily adding new languages and features, but gains in quality over the last four years had slowed considerably.'b'Until today. As of the previous weekend, Translate had been converted to an A.I.-based system for much of its traffic, not just in the United States but in Europe and Asia as well: The rollout included translations between English and Spanish, French, Portuguese, German, Chinese, Japanese, Korean and Turkish. The rest of Translate\xe2\x80\x99s hundred-odd languages were to come, with the aim of eight per month, by the end of next year. The new incarnation, to the pleasant surprise of Google\xe2\x80\x99s own engineers, had been completed in only nine months. The A.I. system had demonstrated overnight improvements roughly equal to the total gains the old one had accrued over its entire lifetime.'b'Pichai has an affection for the obscure literary reference; he told me a month earlier, in his office in Mountain View, Calif., that Translate in part exists because not everyone can be like the physicist Robert Oppenheimer, who learned Sanskrit to read the Bhagavad Gita in the original. In London, the slide on the monitors behind him flicked to a Borges quote: \xe2\x80\x9c'b'Uno no es lo que es por lo que escribe, sino por lo que ha le\xc3\xaddo'b'.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'Grinning, Pichai read aloud an awkward English version of the sentence that had been rendered by the old Translate system: \xe2\x80\x9cOne is not what is for what he writes, but for what he has read.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'To the right of that was a new A.I.-rendered version: \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not what you write, but what you have read.\xe2\x80\x9d'b'It was a fitting remark: The new Google Translate was run on the first machines that had, in a sense, ever learned to read anything at all.'b''b'Google\xe2\x80\x99s decision'b' to reorganize itself around A.I. was the first major manifestation of what has become an industrywide machine-learning delirium. Over the past four years, six companies in particular \xe2\x80\x94 Google, Facebook, Apple, Amazon, Microsoft and the Chinese firm Baidu \xe2\x80\x94 have touched off an arms race for A.I. talent, particularly within universities. Corporate promises of resources and freedom have thinned out top academic departments. It has become widely known in Silicon Valley that Mark Zuckerberg, chief executive of Facebook, personally oversees, with phone calls and video-chat blandishments, his company\xe2\x80\x99s overtures to the most desirable graduate students. Starting salaries of seven figures are not unheard-of. Attendance at the field\xe2\x80\x99s most important academic conference has nearly quadrupled. What is at stake is not just one more piecemeal innovation but control over what very well could represent an entirely new computational platform: pervasive, ambient artificial intelligence.'b'The phrase \xe2\x80\x9cartificial intelligence\xe2\x80\x9d is invoked as if its meaning were self-evident, but it has always been a source of confusion and controversy. Imagine if you went back to the 1970s, stopped someone on the street, pulled out a smartphone and showed her Google Maps. Once you managed to convince her you weren\xe2\x80\x99t some oddly dressed wizard, and that what you withdrew from your pocket wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a black-arts amulet but merely a tiny computer more powerful than the one that guided Apollo missions, Google Maps would almost certainly seem to her a persuasive example of \xe2\x80\x9cartificial intelligence.\xe2\x80\x9d In a very real sense, it is. It can do things any map-literate human can manage, like get you from your hotel to the airport \xe2\x80\x94 though it can do so much more quickly and reliably. It can also do things that humans simply and obviously cannot: It can evaluate the traffic, plan the best route and reorient itself when you take the wrong exit.'b'Practically nobody today, however, would bestow upon Google Maps the honorific \xe2\x80\x9cA.I.,\xe2\x80\x9d so sentimental and sparing are we in our use of the word \xe2\x80\x9cintelligence.\xe2\x80\x9d Artificial intelligence, we believe, must be something that distinguishes HAL from whatever it is a loom or wheelbarrow can do. The minute we can automate a task, we downgrade the relevant skill involved to one of mere mechanism. Today Google Maps seems, in the pejorative sense of the term, robotic: It simply accepts an explicit demand (the need to get from one place to another) and tries to satisfy that demand as efficiently as possible. The goal posts for \xe2\x80\x9cartificial intelligence\xe2\x80\x9d are thus constantly receding.'b'When he has an opportunity to make careful distinctions, Pichai differentiates between the current applications of A.I. and the ultimate goal of \xe2\x80\x9cartificial 'b'general'b' intelligence.\xe2\x80\x9d Artificial general intelligence will not involve dutiful adherence to explicit instructions, but instead will demonstrate a facility with the implicit, the interpretive. It will be a general tool, designed for general purposes in a general context. Pichai believes his company\xe2\x80\x99s future depends on something like this. Imagine if you could tell Google Maps, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to go to the airport, but I need to stop off on the way to buy a present for my nephew.\xe2\x80\x9d A more generally intelligent version of that service \xe2\x80\x94 a ubiquitous assistant, of the sort that Scarlett Johansson memorably disembodied three years ago in the Spike Jonze film \xe2\x80\x9cHer\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94 would know all sorts of things that, say, a close friend or an earnest intern might know: your nephew\xe2\x80\x99s age, and how much you ordinarily like to spend on gifts for children, and where to find an open store. But a truly intelligent Maps could also conceivably know all sorts of things a close friend wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, like what has only recently come into fashion among preschoolers in your nephew\xe2\x80\x99s school \xe2\x80\x94 or more important, what its users actually want. If an intelligent machine were able to discern some intricate if murky regularity in data about what we have done in the past, it might be able to extrapolate about our subsequent desires, even if we don\xe2\x80\x99t entirely know them ourselves.'b'The new wave of A.I.-enhanced assistants \xe2\x80\x94 Apple\xe2\x80\x99s Siri, Facebook\xe2\x80\x99s M, Amazon\xe2\x80\x99s Echo \xe2\x80\x94 are all creatures of machine learning, built with similar intentions. The corporate dreams for machine learning, however, aren\xe2\x80\x99t exhausted by the goal of consumer clairvoyance. A medical-imaging subsidiary of Samsung announced this year that its new ultrasound devices could detect breast cancer. Management consultants are falling all over themselves to prep executives for the widening industrial applications of computers that program themselves. DeepMind, a 2014 Google acquisition, defeated the reigning human grandmaster of the ancient board game Go, despite predictions that such an achievement would take another 10 years.'b'In a famous 'b'1950 essay'b', Alan Turing proposed a test for an artificial general intelligence: a computer that could, over the course of five minutes of text exchange, successfully deceive a real human interlocutor. Once a machine can translate fluently between two natural languages, the foundation has been laid for a machine that might one day \xe2\x80\x9cunderstand\xe2\x80\x9d human language well enough to engage in plausible conversation. Google Brain\xe2\x80\x99s members, who pushed and helped oversee the Translate project, believe that such a machine would be on its way to serving as a generally intelligent all-encompassing personal digital assistant.'b''b'What follows here 'b'is the story of how a team of Google researchers and engineers \xe2\x80\x94 at first one or two, then three or four, and finally more than a hundred \xe2\x80\x94 made considerable progress in that direction. It\xe2\x80\x99s an uncommon story in many ways, not least of all because it defies many of the Silicon Valley stereotypes we\xe2\x80\x99ve grown accustomed to. It does not feature people who think that everything will be unrecognizably different tomorrow or the next day because of some restless tinkerer in his garage. It is neither a story about people who think technology will solve all our problems nor one about people who think technology is ineluctably bound to create apocalyptic new ones. It is not about disruption, at least not in the way that word tends to be used.'b'It is, in fact, three overlapping stories that converge in Google Translate\xe2\x80\x99s successful metamorphosis to A.I. \xe2\x80\x94 a technical story, an institutional story and a story about the evolution of ideas. The technical story is about one team on one product at one company, and the process by which they refined, tested and introduced a brand-new version of an old product in only about a quarter of the time anyone, themselves included, might reasonably have expected. The institutional story is about the employees of a small but influential artificial-intelligence group within that company, and the process by which their intuitive faith in some old, unproven and broadly unpalatable notions about computing upended every other company within a large radius. The story of ideas is about the cognitive scientists, psychologists and wayward engineers who long toiled in obscurity, and the process by which their ostensibly irrational convictions ultimately inspired a paradigm shift in our understanding not only of technology but also, in theory, of consciousness itself.'b'The first story, the story of Google Translate, takes place in Mountain View over nine months, and it explains the transformation of machine translation. The second story, the story of Google Brain and its many competitors, takes place in Silicon Valley over five years, and it explains the transformation of that entire community. The third story, the story of deep learning, takes place in a variety of far-flung laboratories \xe2\x80\x94 in Scotland, Switzerland, Japan and most of all Canada \xe2\x80\x94 over seven decades, and it might very well contribute to the revision of our self-image as first and foremost beings who think.'b'All three are stories about artificial intelligence. The seven-decade story is about what we might conceivably expect or want from it. The five-year story is about what it might do in the near future. The nine-month story is about what it can do right this minute. These three stories are themselves just proof of concept. All of this is only the beginning.'b'Jeff Dean, though his title is senior fellow, is the de facto head of Google Brain. Dean is a sinewy, energy-efficient man with a long, narrow face, deep-set eyes and an earnest, soapbox-derby sort of enthusiasm. The son of a medical anthropologist and a public-health epidemiologist, Dean grew up all over the world \xe2\x80\x94 Minnesota, Hawaii, Boston, Arkansas, Geneva, Uganda, Somalia, Atlanta \xe2\x80\x94 and, while in high school and college, wrote software used by the World Health Organization. He has been with Google since 1999, as employee 25ish, and has had a hand in the core software systems beneath nearly every significant undertaking since then. A beloved artifact of company culture is 'b'Jeff Dean Facts'b', written in the style of the Chuck Norris Facts meme: \xe2\x80\x9cJeff Dean\xe2\x80\x99s PIN is the last four digits of pi.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cWhen Alexander Graham Bell invented the telephone, he saw a missed call from Jeff Dean.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cJeff Dean got promoted to Level 11 in a system where the maximum level is 10.\xe2\x80\x9d (This last one is, in fact, true.)'b'One day in early 2011, Dean walked into one of the Google campus\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cmicrokitchens\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 the \xe2\x80\x9cGoogley\xe2\x80\x9d word for the shared break spaces on most floors of the Mountain View complex\xe2\x80\x99s buildings \xe2\x80\x94 and ran into Andrew Ng, a young Stanford computer-science professor who was working for the company as a consultant. Ng told him about Project Marvin, an internal effort (named after the celebrated A.I. pioneer Marvin Minsky) he had recently helped establish to experiment with \xe2\x80\x9cneural networks,\xe2\x80\x9d pliant digital lattices based loosely on the architecture of the brain.
